Defining the Litigant (Khasm) in Hanafi Judicial Procedure
Defines the procedural concept of khasm with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of khasm to identify the proper litigant in various contractual and estate disputes.

Core Idea
A litigant is the person against whom a lawsuit may be heard individually — not everyone present is a party.

In this lesson, we examine a key passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that defines who qualifies as a litigant (khasm) in Hanafi qada. Understanding this definition is crucial for determining the correct parties to a lawsuit, as only a khasm can be sued or represented. The author provides numerous examples from pawn, lease, loan, bequest, estate, agency, and sale contracts to illustrate the rule.

Guided Path
1
State the core definition of khasm
Identify the key phrase: 'the litigant is the one against whom the suit is heard individually by law' (الخصم من تسمع الدعوى عليه بانفراده شرعاً). This is the central rule.
Bi-infirādihi (individually) is the critical condition.
2
Exclude the non-litigant present person
The definition excludes a person who is present in court but is not a khasm. The phrase 'to exclude the case where he judged against a present person who is not a litigant' shows that mere presence does not confer standing.
3
Apply to pawn (rahn) and lease/loan contracts
Work through the examples: pawnbroker (rāhin) is not a litigant in absence of mortgagee (murtahin) and vice versa. Similarly, lessor (mu'jir) and lessee (musta'jir) are not litigants for claims against each other in certain contexts. Identify why: the claim does not rest individually on that person.
The locus of the claim determines who is individually liable.
4
Analyze estate and agency examples
The creditor (gharīm) of the deceased is not a litigant for a debt claim against the deceased; the litigant is the heir or executor (waṣī). One heir can represent all. In hire (sa'aya), the agent (ma'mur) is the litigant unless the principal is a sultan.
5
Distinguish sale scenarios
For a valid sale before delivery, both parties are litigants. For a fasid (void) sale, before delivery only the seller is litigant; after delivery only the buyer. Apply the definition: the claim is individually relevant to the party in possession or who holds the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقلنا على غير خصم حاضر لإخراج ما لو قضى على حاضر ليس بخصم وعلى خصم غائب فالخصم من تسمع الدعوى عليه بانفراده شرعاً، فخرج ما لو قضى على راهن في غيبة مرتهن وعكسه، وكذا في المؤجر مع المستأجر والمعير مع المستعير والموصى له ليس بخصم إلا في إثبات الوصاية أو الوكالة وغريم الميت ليس بخصم لمدعي الدين على الميت إنما الخصم وارث أو وصي وأحد الورثة خصم عن الباقي فيما للميت وما عليه، والخصم في دعوى السعاية المأمور لا الأمر إن كان الآمر سلطاناً وإلا فالأمر والمستأجر ليس بخصم لمدعي إجارة أو رهن أو شراء كالمستعير والمشتري خصم للكل، وكذا الموهوب له والخصم في دعوى المبيع قبل القبض العاقدان وفي المبيع الفاسد قبل القبض البائع وحده وبعده المشتري وحده وسيأتي تمامه في كتاب الدعوى .
And we say: [restricted] to a litigant who is present, to exclude the case where he judged against a present person who is not a litigant, and against a litigant who is absent. The litigant is the one against whom the suit is heard individually by law. Thus, it excludes the case where he judged against a pawnbroker in the absence of the mortgagee, and vice versa. Similarly, with the lessor and lessee, the lender and borrower; the legatee is not a litigant except in establishing the executorship or agency. The creditor of the deceased is not a litigant for a claimant of a debt against the deceased; rather, the litigant is the heir or executor. One of the heirs is a litigant for the rest regarding what belongs to the deceased and what is owed. The litigant in a claim of hire (al-sa'aya) is the agent (ma'mur), not the principal (amir), unless the principal is a sultan; otherwise, it is the principal. The lessee is not a litigant for a claimant of a lease, pledge, or purchase, like the borrower. The purchaser is a litigant for everything, and likewise the donee. The litigant in a claim regarding the sold item before delivery (qabd) is the two contracting parties; for a void (fasid) sale before delivery, it is only the seller; after delivery, it is only the buyer. Its completion will come in the Book of Lawsuits (Kitab al-Da'wa).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a precise definition of who is considered a litigant (khasm) in Hanafi judicial procedure. The author states that a litigant is someone against whom a lawsuit is heard individually (tusma' al-da'wa 'alayhi bi-infirādihi shar'an). This excludes people who are present but not parties to the suit. He then gives numerous examples: In a pawn contract, the pawnbroker (rāhin) is not a litigant in the absence of the mortgagee (murtahin), and vice versa. Similarly, in lease and loan contracts, the lessor (mu'jir) and lessee (musta'jir) are not litigants for claims against each other in certain contexts. A legatee (mūṣā lahu) is not a litigant unless the claim involves establishing executorship or agency. A creditor (gharīm) of a deceased is not a litigant in a claim against the deceased; the litigants are the heirs or executor. One heir can represent all others in matters of the estate. In a hire claim (sa'aya), the agent (ma'mur) is the litigant, not the principal, unless the principal is a sultan. The buyer is a litigant for all claims related to the purchase, and similarly the donee. For a sold item before delivery, both parties are litigants; for a void sale (bay' fāsid), before delivery only the seller, after delivery only the buyer.

Key Terms
الخصم (al-khaṣm): The person against whom a lawsuit is heard individually by law; the proper party to a judicial proceeding.
الوصي (al-waṣī): An executor or guardian appointed by will or by the judge; can represent the deceased or minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is the proper litigant in a claim for debt against a deceased person according to this passage?
A) The creditor of the deceased.
B) The legatee.
C) The heir or executor (waṣī).
D) The borrower of the deceased.

Key Takeaway
Only the person individually liable to the claim is the litigant; other parties, even if present, cannot be sued.

Rules for Wakil and Wasi in Judicial Proceedings
Procedural rules for representatives in court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the judge rules against the principal (absent/deceased) in the presence of a representative, and apply the rules of evidence and confession for wakil and wasi.

Core Idea
The judge rules against the principal, not the representative—only the representative's claims about agency or property determine their standing.

In Hanafi qada, when a party is absent or deceased, their representative (wakil or wasi) may appear. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the judge's ruling is against the principal, not the representative. It also explains nuanced rules for evidence and confession that determine whether the representative becomes a litigant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on representative presence
Understand that when a wakil or wasi appears, the judge rules against the absent/deceased principal, not against the representative. The record reflects this.
Exception to the general rule that judgment requires presence of the party.
2
Distinguish between wakil and wasi regarding property delivery
If a wasi claims property and the possessor confesses, the property is delivered to the wasi. For a wakil, it is not delivered solely on the agent's claim without proof.
Executorship is based on the deceased's appointment, giving the wasi stronger standing.
3
Apply the rules for evidence and confession in agency
If a wakil confesses to agency but denies property, he is not a litigant. If he confesses to property but denies agency, he is not forced to swear. Evidence is accepted if he provides it.
Confession is only binding against oneself, not against the principal.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf on oath for agency and on the son as litigant
Al-Khassaf holds that the wakil should be made to swear on agency; the preferred view is no oath. Also, regarding the son of the deceased as a litigant in establishing executorship: al-Khassaf says he is a litigant; some shaykhs disagree.
These ikhtilafat affect how the case proceeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا أن يحضر من يقوم مقامه كالوكيل والوصي ذكر المثالين ليبين أن القائم مقامه قد يكون بإنابته أو بإنابة الشرع، فالوصي إن كان من قبل الميت فهو بإنابته، وإن كان منصوب القاضي فهو بإنابة الشرع، وظاهر الاستثناء أن الوكيل والوصي إذا حضر فإن القاضي إنما يحكم على الغائب وعلى الميت ولا يحكم على الوكيل أو الوصي ويكتب في السجل أنه حكم على الميت وعلى الغائب بحضرة وكيله وبحضرة وصيه؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. وفي البزازية من اليمين ادعى أنه وكيل الغائب بقبض الدين أو العين إن برهن على الوكالة والمال قبلت وإن أقر بالوكالة وأنكر المال لا يصير خصماً ولا تقبل البينة على المال لأنه لم يثبت كونه خصماً بإقرار المطلوب لأنه ليس بحجة في حق الطالب. وإن أقر بالمال وأنكر الوكالة لا يستحلف على الوكالة لأن التحليف يترتب على دعوى صحيحة ولم توجد لعدم ثبوت الوكالة. وذكر الخصاف أنه يحلف على الوكالة والأوّل أصح، ولو أنكر الكل فهو كإنكار الوكالة وحدها ولو أقام البينة على المال والوكالة تقبل عند الإمام لأن الوكيل بقبض الدين خصم. وفصل الوصاية في المال كفصل الوكالة إلا في فصل وهو أنه إذا ادعى أن فلاناً الميت أوصى إليه بحفظ ماله وقبضه وله كذا عند هذا الحاضر فأقر الحاضر بالكل يؤمر بتسليم الدين والعين بخلاف الوكالة، وإن أقر بالوصاية والموت وأنكر المال يحلف، وإن أقر بالمال والموت وأنكر الوصاية ينصب القاضي وصياً ولا يحلفه لما ذكرنا أن دعوى الوصاية ليست بلازمة، وإن أقر بالوصاية والمال وأنكر الموت يحلفه على علمه كما في الوارث، وإن أقام بينة على كل ذلك تقبل في الكل ا هـ . وفيها من التاسع في نصب الوصي الخصم في إثبات الوصاية الوارث البالغ أو مديون الميت أو الموصى له. واختلفوا في ابن الميت فهو خصم على ما ذكره الخصاف وخالفه بعض المشايخ ولا تثبت بإقرار مديون الميت أو مودعه، وإذا ثبتت الوصاية بالبينة لمدعي الدين ثم حضر غريم آخر أو موصى له آخر لا يقضى للثاني بينة الأوّل وعند الثاني يقضى، وفي الوصية بأنواع البر يكتفى بتلك البينة بالإجماع ا هـ . وأطلق في الوكيل فشمل ما إذا كان وكيلاً في الخصومة والدعوى وما إذا كان وكيلاً للقضاء كما إذا أقيمت البينة عليه فوكل ليقضى عليه ثم غاب كما في القنية، وفيها من باب القضاء على الغائب : استمهل المدعى عليه بعد البيئة العادلة القاضي مدة معينة وغاب ومضت تلك المدة، فإن ظهر تعنته فله القضاء حال غيبته . ومثله عن الخجندي قال رضي الله عنه : واشتراطهما التغيب للقضاء عليه اختيار حسن قامت البينة على الوكيل فغاب فحضر
His statement: 'Except if someone who stands in his place appears, such as the agent (wakil) and the executor (wasi).' He mentioned the two examples to show that the representative may be by his appointment or by the appointment of the law. Thus, the executor, if appointed by the deceased, is by his appointment; if appointed by the judge, it is by the appointment of the law. The apparent meaning of the exception is that when the agent and executor are present, the judge only rules against the absent person and the deceased, and does not rule against the agent or executor. He writes in the record that he ruled against the deceased and the absent in the presence of his agent and in the presence of his executor; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. In al-Bazzaziyya: In the chapter on oaths, if he claims that he is the agent of the absent for collecting a debt or an 'ayn, if he proves the agency and the property by evidence, it is accepted. If he confesses to the agency but denies the property, he does not become a litigant, and evidence for the property is not accepted, because he is not proven to be a litigant by the confession of the sued (since it is not a proof in the right of the claimant). If he confesses to the property but denies the agency, he is not made to take an oath on the agency because the oath is based on a valid claim, which does not exist due to the lack of proof of agency. Al-Khassaf mentions that he is made to take an oath on the agency, but the first is more correct. If he denies everything, it is like denying the agency alone. If he produces evidence for the property and the agency, it is accepted according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the agent for collecting a debt is a litigant. The chapter on executorship regarding property is like the chapter on agency, except in one point: if he claims that so-and-so the deceased appointed him as executor to preserve his property and collect it, and that he has such-and-such with this present person, and the present person confesses to everything, he is ordered to deliver the debt and the 'ayn, unlike agency. If he confesses to the executorship and the death but denies the property, he is made to take an oath. If he confesses to the property and the death but denies the executorship, the judge appoints an executor and does not make him take an oath, because the claim of executorship is not binding. If he confesses to the executorship and the property but denies the death, he is made to take an oath on his knowledge, as with the heir. If he produces evidence for all that, it is accepted in everything. End. And in it (al-Bazzaziyya) from the ninth (section) on appointment of executor: the litigant in establishing executorship is the adult heir, the deceased's debtor, or the legatee. They differed regarding the son of the deceased: he is a litigant according to al-Khassaf, but some shaykhs disagreed. It is not established by the confession of the deceased's debtor or his depositary. If executorship is established by evidence for a claimant of debt, and then another creditor or legatee appears, the second is not judged by the first's evidence; according to the second (position), he is judged. In a will for various acts of charity, that evidence suffices by consensus. End. And the term 'agent' is absolute, including when he is an agent in litigation and lawsuit, and when he is an agent for judgment, as when evidence is established against him and he appoints an agent to be judged against, and then becomes absent, as in al-Qunyah. And in it from the chapter of judgment against an absent person: The defendant asked the judge for a specified period after just evidence, and absented himself, and that period passed; if his stubbornness appears, the judge may judge in his absence. Similarly from al-Khujandi, he said, may Allah be pleased with him: 'And their condition of absence for the judgment against him is a good choice.' Evidence was established against the agent, then he became absent, then he appeared...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the rules when a judge rules in the presence of a representative (wakil or wasi) of an absent or deceased person. The author clarifies that the judge does not rule against the representative but against the principal (absent or deceased), recording that the ruling was made in the presence of the representative. He then discusses complex scenarios from al-Bazzaziyya regarding evidence and confession in agency and executorship. For an agent claiming to collect a debt: if he provides evidence, it is accepted; if he confesses to agency but denies the property, he is not considered a litigant; if he confesses to the property but denies agency, he is not forced to swear on agency. For executorship, similar rules apply except that if the executor claims and the possessor confesses, the property is delivered to the executor, unlike agency where it is not delivered solely on the agent's claim. The passage also covers who can be a litigant in establishing executorship (adult heir, debtor, legatee) and notes a difference of opinion regarding the deceased's son. Finally, it mentions that if a defendant requests a delay and absents himself, the judge may rule after the delay if stubbornness appears; and similarly for the agent's absence.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): An agent appointed by a principal (absent or present) to act on his behalf, especially in litigation or financial matters.
الوصي (al-waṣī): An executor appointed by will (from the deceased) or by the judge to manage the estate of a deceased person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent (wakil) claims to be the agent of an absent person for collecting a debt and confesses to the agency but denies the property, what is the correct ruling according to the preferred view?
A) He becomes a litigant and evidence for the property is accepted.
B) He does not become a litigant and evidence for the property is not accepted.
C) He is made to take an oath on the agency.
D) The judge appoints an executor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, the presence of a wakil or wasi allows judgment against the absent/deceased principal, but the representative's own statements and evidence are subject to specific rules that affect their standing as a litigant.

Judgment Against an Absent Party – The Sabab Principle from Talkhis al-Jami'
Presents two key cases and qualification for judgment against absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the Hanafi rule that judgment against an absent party is valid when the present claimant's right is based on a cause (sabab) that is necessarily (la mahalah) linked to the absent party, and DISTINGUISH cases where the present party is only a cause in some respects.

Core Idea
Judgment against the absent is permitted when the present party's claim is a direct cause (sabab) for the right; otherwise rights would be lost.

In this lesson, we examine two classic cases from Talkhis al-Jami' where the Hanafi school permits judgment even though the affected party is absent. We'll see that the key is whether the present claimant's right arises from a cause that necessarily implicates the absent party. This is not arbitrary; the Hanafis base this on istihsan (equity) to prevent the loss of rights. We also explore the qualification 'sabab la mahalah' and contrast with cases where the present is only a partial cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases from Talkhis al-Jami'
Read the first two examples: slander of 'son of an adulteress' with proof of emancipation or lineage, and sole heir claiming inheritance. The court rules even though the affected (emancipator, relative, other heirs) are absent.
Both involve the present person establishing a fact that directly creates a right (freedom, lineage, inheritance)
2
Recognize the common element: sabab (cause)
In each case, the present party's successful proof directly causes the right. The claimant is not just a representative; their claim is the very cause of the ruling affecting the absent.
3
Understand the qualifier 'sabab la mahalah' (cause necessarily)
The author states that the present must be a cause 'necessarily' – meaning the cause is unconditional and directly links to the absent party. This excludes cases where the present is only a cause in some respects, like an agent.
Counterexamples: agent transferring a slave or woman – their presence does not make them a necessary cause for emancipation or divorce.
4
Contrast with the two counterexamples (agent cases)
When the present party is an agent, the court only restrains the agent's action, but does not rule on the substantive right against the absent principal. If the absent later appears, the proof must be re-established.
The agent is a cause for some aspects (possession) but not for the right itself.
5
Explain the Hanafi justification using istihsan
The objection (qiyas) demands the absent's presence, but the Hanafis adopt istihsan: if the present could not be made a representative, most disputes would stall because they involve absent parties, leading to loss of rights.
This also answers the Hanbali objection that the Hanafis are inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ومن النوع مسألتان تلخيص الجامع : الأولى قال لغيره يابن الزانية وأمه ميتة وادعى أنها كانت أمة لفلان فأقام ابنها بينة أن فلاناً أعتقها أو أقام بينة أنها فلانة بنت فلان القرشية فإنه يقضى بعتقها في الأولى وبنسبها في الثانية وإن كان المعتق والمنسوب إليه غائبين ويقضى بالحد على القاذف. الثانية أقام البينة أن نسبه يلتقي مع نسب الميت إلى جد الميت وأنهم لا يعلمون له وارثاً غيره فإنه بميراثه وإن لم يحضر أباؤهم ولا وكلاؤهم وفيه قضاء على الغائب ا هـ. قيدنا بأن يكون سبباً لا محالة للاحتراز عما يكون سبباً في حال ولا يكون سبباً في حال فإنه لا يكون قضاء على الغائب وذلك في مسألتين: الأولى الوكيل بنقل العبد إلى مولاه إذا برهن العبد على أنه حرره يقبل في حق قصر يد الحاضر لا في حق ثبوت العتق على الموكل، فلو حضر الغائب وأنكر لا بد من إعادة البينة الثانية الوكيل بنقل المرأة إذا برهنت أنه طلقها ثلاثاً يقبل في حق قصر يد الوكيل لا في إثبات الطلاق، وقد أنكر بشر المريسي القضاء على الغائب في هذه المسائل. قال في التحرير : وقد كان بعض العلماء يأبى انتصاب الحاضر خصماً عن الغائب في هذه المسائل ولا يقضى على الحاضر بشيء ما لم يحضر الغائب وهو القياس الظاهر إلا أنا نقول بأن عامة الخصومات يتصل طرف منها بالغائب فلو لم يجعل الحاضر خصماً لأدى إلى إبطال حقوق الناس؛ كذا في شرح التلخيص الفارسي و به اندفع ما اعترض به بعض الحنابلة من أن الحنفية منعوا القضاء على الغائب ثم تحيلوا له بما إذا كان سبباً وهو عين القضاء على الغائب ا هـ .
This is from the type: two issues from Talkhis al-Jami': The first: He said to another, 'O son of the adulteress,' and his mother is dead, and he claimed that she was a slave of so-and-so, so her son established proof that so-and-so emancipated her, or he established proof that she is so-and-so the daughter of so-and-so the Qurashi; then judgment is given for her emancipation in the first case and for her lineage in the second, even if the emancipator and the attributed person are absent, and the hadd punishment is imposed on the slanderer. The second: He established proof that his lineage meets the lineage of the deceased to the grandfather of the deceased, and that they know of no heir other than him; then he is given the inheritance, even if their fathers or their agents are not present, and in it is judgment against the absent one. End. We qualified it as being a cause necessarily, to exclude what is a cause in some aspects and not a cause in others, for then it is not judgment against the absent one. That is in two issues: The first: The agent for transferring a slave to his master, if the slave proves that he was liberated, it is accepted regarding restraining the present person's possession, not regarding establishing emancipation against the principal. So if the absent one appears and denies, the proof must be re-established. The second: The agent for transferring a woman, if she proves that he divorced her three times, it is accepted regarding restraining the agent's hand, not regarding establishing the divorce. And Bishr al-Marisi denied judgment against the absent in these issues. He said in al-Tahrir: Some scholars used to refuse appointing the present as a representative for the absent in these issues and would not judge anything against the present until the absent appeared; that is the apparent analogy. However, we say that most disputes have one side connected to the absent, so if the present were not made a representative, it would lead to the nullification of people's rights. Thus in the Persian commentary on al-Talkhis, and with this is repelled the objection by some Hanbalis that the Hanafis prohibited judgment against the absent and then devised a legal trick for it when there is a cause, which is itself judgment against the absent. End.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Talkhis al-Jami', explaining two cases where judgment can be given against an absent party. The first case involves someone falsely called 'son of an adulteress'; if the claimant proves that the deceased mother was a freed slave of a specific person or that she was of a specific Qurayshi lineage, then the court frees the claimant (if he is a slave) or establishes his lineage, even if the person who would be affected (the emancipator or the relative) is absent. The second case deals with establishing that the claimant is the sole heir of a deceased person; judgment for inheritance is given even if other potential heirs or their agents are absent. The author then qualifies that this only applies when the present party is 'a cause necessarily' (sabab la mahalah), meaning their claim directly causes the right. He gives two counterexamples where the present person is only a cause in some respects: (1) an agent transferring a slave to his master: if the slave proves freedom, the court restrains the agent from transferring but does not rule on the freedom itself against the absent master; if the master later denies, the proof must be repeated. (2) an agent transferring a woman: if she proves divorce, the court restrains the agent but does not rule on the divorce against the absent husband. Then Bishr al-Marisi (a Mu'tazili scholar) is noted to have rejected judgment against the absent altogether in such cases. The commentary from al-Tahrir responds that although the apparent qiyas (analogy) would require the absent party's presence, istihsan (equity) dictates that the present be made a representative, because otherwise many rights would be lost since most disputes involve absent parties. This also refutes the Hanbali objection that the Hanafis are inconsistent—permitting judgment against the absent through a 'cause' while ostensibly prohibiting it.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause that directly creates a legal right; in this context, the present party's claim is the cause for the ruling against the absent.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): A judicial ruling that affects an absent party, permissible when a present party is a cause for the right.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed to perform a legal act; here, an agent for transferring a slave or woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first case from Talkhis al-Jami', what is required for the court to rule on the claimant's freedom or lineage even though the master or relative is absent?
A) The claimant must produce the absent party in court.
B) The claimant must prove that his claim is a cause (sabab) for the right.
C) The claimant must have a written contract of emancipation.
D) The court must wait until the absent party appoints an agent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits judgment against an absent party when the present party's claim is a direct cause (sabab) for the right, based on istihsan to prevent loss of rights.

Cause by Origin vs. Cause by Continuation in Judicial Proof
Explains three cases where the present person is a cause by continuation, not origin, so judgment is not effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between cause by origin (sabab bi al-ibtida') and cause by continuation (sabab bi al-baqa') to determine whether a proof is accepted in cases of judgment against the absent party.

Core Idea
A cause that only continues a right does not permit judgment against an absent party — the origin must be proven.

In Hanafi procedural law, not every link to an absent person is sufficient to accept evidence. This passage establishes a critical restriction: the present person must be a cause (sabab) for the claim itself, not merely for the continuation of a right. Through three concrete examples — marriage of a slave girl, invalid sale, and preemption — the author illustrates when proof is rejected. Understanding this distinction is vital for anyone involved in Islamic judicial practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify key terms
Define 'sabab bi al-ibtida'' (cause by origin) and 'sabab bi al-baqa'' (cause by continuation). The origin refers to the initial creation of the right, while continuation refers to its persistence.
These terms are central to the passage's argument.
2
Parse the general rule
The passage states that if the causation is only by continuation (sabab bi al-baqa'), then the proof is not accepted at all, even if witnesses testify to the continuation.
The rule is absolute: 'لا يقبل مطلقاً'.
3
Analyze the first example (slave girl)
The buyer claimed the seller was the absent husband. The marriage is the origin, but the cause for the claim against the present buyer is only continuation because divorce could have occurred. Thus, proof is not accepted.
Even testifying to current status does not help.
4
Analyze the second example (invalid sale)
The buyer tried to prove an invalid sale from an absent party to prevent rescission. The cause is continuation of the void contract, not a new cause, so proof is rejected.
The defect in the sale does not create a new origin.
5
Analyze the third example (preemption)
The preemptor attempted to prove ownership from an absent seller. His right to preemption is based on that ownership, which is a continuation of the absent's title. Hence, proof is not accepted.
The preemptor's possession does not change the causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بكونه سبباً لما يدعي على الحاضر للاحتراز عما إذا كانت السببية باعتبار البقاء فإنه لا يقبل مطلقاً وذلك في مسائل : الأولى اشترى جارية وادعى أن البائع كان زوجها فلان الغائب واشتراها بلا علم بذلك فأنكر البائع فبرهن لم يقبل في حق الحاضر والغائب سبب في البقاء لجواز الطلاق بعده ، فلو تعرض الشهود للبقاء أيضاً لم تقبل أيضاً بأن قالوا إنها امرأته للحال لأن البقاء تبع للابتداء الثاني برهن المشتري فاسداً على البيع من غائب حين رأم البائع فسخ البيع للفساد لا يقبل مطلقاً وإن تعرضوا للبقاء. الثالثة في يده دار فبيعت دار بجنبها فأراد أخذها بالشفعة فزعم المشتري أن ما في يد الشفيع لغائب فبرهن الشفيع على شرائها من الغائب لا تقبل في حقهما.
And it is qualified as being a cause for what is claimed against the present as a precaution against when the causation is with respect to continuation, for then it is absolutely not accepted. That is in the following issues: The first: He bought a slave girl and claimed that the seller was her husband, so-and-so who is absent, and he bought her without knowledge of that; then the seller denied, so he proved; it is not accepted regarding the present and the absent is a cause in continuation because divorce is possible after it. Even if the witnesses testify to continuation as well, it is also not accepted, like if they say 'She is his wife now,' because continuation is dependent on origin. The second: The purchaser proved that the sale from the absent was invalid when the seller sought rescission of the sale due to invalidity; it is not accepted absolutely, even if they testify to continuation. The third: In his hand is a house, then a house adjacent to it was sold, so he wanted to take it by preemption; then the purchaser claimed that what is in the hands of the preemptor belongs to an absent person, so the preemptor proved that he bought it from the absent; it is not accepted regarding both of them.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines a restriction on the previous rule: the present person must be a cause (sabab) for the claim against him, not just a cause for its continuation (baqa'). If the causation is only with respect to continuation, the evidence is not accepted at all against either the present or the absent. The author gives three examples: (1) A person buys a slave girl and later claims the seller was her absent husband; the seller denies. If the buyer proves the claim, the proof is not accepted regarding the present or the absent because the cause (the marriage) is in the past and there is possibility of divorce thereafter—so it is only continuation. Even if witnesses say 'she is his wife now,' it is not accepted because continuation depends on origin. (2) A person buys something from an absent party but the sale is invalid (fasid). When the seller wants to rescind, the buyer proves the invalid sale from the absent; this proof is not accepted because the cause is only continuation of the void contract. (3) A person has a house; a neighboring house is sold and he wants preemption (shuf'a). The buyer claims the house in the preemptor's hand belongs to an absent person. The preemptor proves he bought it from that absent person; this proof is not accepted regarding either party because the preemptor's right is based on ownership that is only continuation, not a new cause.

Key Terms
سبب بالبقاء (sabab bi al-baqa'): A cause that is only for the continuation of a right, not its origin; such a cause does not allow judgment against the absent.
سبب بالابتداء (sabab bi al-ibtida'): A cause that is the origin of a right; such a cause allows judgment against the absent if the present party is the cause.
شفعة (shuf'a): Preemption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy a sold property at the price agreed by the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the slave girl, why is the buyer's proof not accepted to establish that the seller was her husband?
A) Because the buyer did not have witnesses.
B) Because the cause is only continuation, as divorce could have occurred after the sale.
C) Because the seller is present and denies.
D) Because the absent husband is not a party to the suit.

Key Takeaway
When the causation is only by continuation, evidence is rejected to protect absent parties from indirect judgment.

Sabab vs Shart: Judgment Against the Absent
Explains that a condition (shart) does not allow judgment against the absent, unlike a cause (sabab).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a direct cause (sabab) and a condition (shart) and apply the rule that a condition cannot initiate judgment against an absent party.

Core Idea
A condition suspends a ruling; it is not a direct cause, so it cannot initiate judgment against an absent person.

In Hanafi procedural law, not every claim can be used to initiate a case against an absent defendant. The key distinction is whether the claim is based on a direct cause (sabab) or a mere condition (shart). This lesson unpacks that distinction using examples from al-Jami' al-Asghar and clarifies the relied-upon position, including an important legal device (hila) that attempts to work around the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Understand that the passage distinguishes between a cause (sabab) that directly creates a right and a condition (shart) that suspends a right on a future event. The ruling concerns whether a claim based on a condition can be used to start a case against an absent person.
Recall Hanafi usul: a sabab is a direct cause like a contract or tort; a shart is a future event that triggers an existing obligation.
2
Analyze the example of conditional talaq
Examine the case from al-Jami' al-Asghar: a husband says, 'If so-and-so divorces his wife, then you are divorced.' The wife claims it happened while so-and-so is absent. The relied-upon view is that her proof is not accepted because it would initiate judgment against the absent based on a condition (someone else's divorce), not a direct cause.
The condition is the divorce of a third party; the wife is not claiming a direct act by her husband.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Shams al-A'imma al-Uzjandi held that even conditional claims are valid for initiating judgment. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq states the first view (invalid) is more correct (al-awwal asahh). Understand the reasoning: the majority sees it as initiating judgment against the absent without a direct cause.
This ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is the majority position.
4
Distinguish the accepted exception
Contrast with a condition that has already been fulfilled, e.g., a husband says 'If so-and-so enters the house, you are divorced,' and the absent so-and-so has already entered. Here the wife's proof is accepted by agreement because the condition is now a past fact, effectively a direct cause.
The condition is no longer future; it is verified, so judgment against the absent is not 'initiating' but enforcing an existing right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالسبب للاحتراز عن الشرط في الجامع الأصغر قال : إن طلق فلان امرأته فأنت طالق فادعت أنه طلقها وفلان غائب وبرهن يصح، وقيل يصح وبه أخذ شمس الأئمة الأوزجندي، والأوّل أصح لأن فيه ابتداء القضاء على الغائب بخلاف ما إذا قامت البينة أن زوجها قال لها إن دخل فلان الدار فأنت كذا وقد دخل فلان الغائب الدار وبره رهنت حيث يقبل اتفاقاً، والذي يفعله الناس فيما إذا أرادوا إقامة البينة على الغائب أنه وكله في قبض حقوقه على الناس يدعي واحد عند القاضي أن الغائب علق تلك الوكالة ببيع هذا الحاضر داره من فلان بكذا وقد باع هذا دار من فلان وتحقق الشرط وصار هو وكيلاً عن الغائب في القبض ولموكله على هذا المحضر كذا فيقول المدعى عليه نعم إنه وكله كما ذكر إلا أنه لم يوجد الشرط فيقيم الوكيل البينة على وجود الشرط فيقضي القاضي عليه بالبيع والوكالة لا تصح إلا على اختيار الإمام الأوزجندي لما فيه من إبطال حق الغائب؛ في البزازية.
And it is qualified with 'cause' to exclude 'condition' in al-Jami' al-Asghar. He said: If so-and-so divorces his wife, then you are divorced, and she claims that he divorced her while so-and-so is absent, and she proves it, it is valid. And it is said it is valid, and Shams al-A'immah al-Uzjandi followed that, but the first is more correct because it involves initiating judgment against the absent, unlike if the proof establishes that her husband said to her, 'If so-and-so enters the house, then you are such and such,' and so-and-so the absent has entered the house, and she proves, where it is accepted by agreement. And what people do when they want to establish proof against the absent is that he appoints him as agent to receive his rights from people. One claims before the judge that the absent suspended that agency on this present person selling his house to so-and-so for such price, and he has sold that house to so-and-so and the condition is realized, and he became an agent for the absent in receipt; and his principal has a claim against this present person. So the defendant says, 'Yes, he appointed him as you mentioned, but the condition did not occur.' Then the agent establishes proof of the occurrence of the condition, and the judge rules against him for the sale. And the agency is only valid according to the choice of Imam al-Uzjandi, because it involves nullifying the right of the absent. In al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage further distinguishes between a cause (sabab) and a condition (shart). If the present party's claim is based on a condition (e.g., someone else's action), it does not qualify as a direct cause, and therefore judgment against the absent is not initiated. The author gives an example from al-Jami' al-Asghar: If a husband says, 'If so-and-so divorces his wife, then you are divorced,' and the wife proves that so-and-so did divorce his wife while that person is absent, some say it is valid (Ibn al-Uzjandi's view), but the correct view is that it is not valid because it is initial judgment against the absent based on a condition. However, if the condition is something like entering a house, and the absent has entered, and the wife proves it, it is accepted by agreement. Then the author describes a common practice (hila): to establish proof against an absent party, people use an agency contract conditioned on a sale. The judge rules on the sale based on the condition, but this agency is only valid according to Uzjandi; otherwise it would nullify the absent's right. This shows the tension in allowing conditions to affect absent parties.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A direct cause that gives rise to a legal right, such as a contract, tort, or divorce.
شرط (shart): A condition that suspends a legal effect on a future event; it does not itself create a right until fulfilled.
حيلة (hila): A legal device used to achieve a permissible end indirectly; here, a conditional agency to prove a right against an absent party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, why is the wife's proof of divorce invalid when based on a condition like 'if so-and-so divorces his wife, you are divorced'?
A) Because the condition is not yet fulfilled.
B) Because it involves initiating judgment against the absent without a direct cause.
C) Because the husband did not explicitly divorce her.
D) Because the absent party must be present.

Key Takeaway
A condition (shart) does not suffice to initiate judgment against an absent person; only a direct cause (sabab) or a condition that has already occurred qualifies.

The Present as Representative for the Absent in Condition-Based Claims
Refines the ruling on condition vs cause representation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a present litigant represents an absent litigant only when the condition is harmless, and distinguish cause from condition in procedural representation.

Core Idea
A present can represent an absent only if the condition is harmless; harmful conditions block representation.

In Hanafi qada, a key question is whether a present litigant can represent an absent litigant when the claim involves a condition. This super-commentary refines the rule: it distinguishes between claims based on cause (sabab) and condition (shart). The correct view, per Rashid al-Din and the majority of mashayikh, is that representation is allowed only if the condition does not harm the absent. When the condition brings both benefit and harm, representation fails, and only the claim against the present is adjudicated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine whether the present can be a representative for the absent when the claim against the absent is a condition for the claim against the present.
This is a procedural question in Hanafi qada.
2
Understand the distinction
Differentiate between a cause (sabab) and a condition (shart). A cause directly triggers a ruling; a condition is a prerequisite without being the direct cause.
In the example, the divorce claim against the absent is a condition for the mahr claim against the present.
3
Apply the harm test
If the condition is harmless (e.g., entering a house), representation is valid. If it brings both benefit and harm (e.g., marriage vs. divorce), it is not.
Rashid al-Din states: 'If the absent is not harmed... the present becomes a representative; but not if it is between benefit and harm.'
4
Evaluate the disagreement
Some mashayikh allow representation absolutely when the absent's right is a condition; the majority (including Rashid al-Din) reject this when harm is present.
The hashiya clarifies that the correct position is the harm-dependent view.
5
Conclude the ruling
In the given example, the wife claims mahr from the present and divorce from the absent. Since divorce (the condition) harms the absent, the present cannot represent him for the divorce claim; only mahr is awarded.
The passage says: 'It is fitting that he rules for the mahr against the present and not the separation against the absent.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفرقهم بين سبب وسبب الخ تقدم جوابه قبل نحو أربعة أوراق قوله : (ومن مسائل الشرط ما في جامع الفصولين علق طلاقها الخ) أي معزياً إلى فتاوى رشيد الدين وفيه ثم قال أي رشيد الدين: والصحيح من الجواب فيما لو كان ثبوت الحكم على الغائب شرطاً للمدعي على الحاضر ينظر لو لم يتضرر به الغائب كدخول الدار وغيره يصير الحاضر خصماً عنه لا لو دائراً بين نفع وضرر قوله : (يحكم لها بالمهر على الحاضر لا بالفرقة على الغائب عبارة جامع الفصولين يحكم لها بالمهر على الحاضر وبوقوع الثلاث على الغائب فالمدعى به شيئان بينهما سببية قال (ضد): فيه نظر لأن المدعي على الغائب وهو الفرقة شرط المدعي على الحاضر لا سبب وفي مثله لا ينصب الحاضر عن الغائب عند عامة المشايخ فينبغي أن يقضى بالمهر على الحاضر لا بالفرقة على الغائب (صع) فعلى قياس ما قال (صذ) ينبغي أن يقضى في مسألة (فش) يعني فتاوى رشيد الدين بطلاق المدعية لا بنكاح الغائب . فالحاصل أن المدعي على الغائب إذا كان شرطاً لما يدعي على الحاضر قيل ينتصب الحاضر خصماً . الغائب مطلقاً وهو قول عامة قول بعض المشايخ وقيل لا مطلقاً وهو
His statement: (And their distinction between cause and cause) Its answer preceded about four pages earlier. His statement: (And from the issues of condition is what is in Jami' al-Fusulayn: he suspended her divorce...) i.e., attributed to the fatawa of Rashid al-Din. In it, then he said, i.e., Rashid al-Din: The correct in the answer is that if the establishment of the ruling against the absent is a condition for the claimant against the present, it is considered. If the absent is not harmed by it, like entering a house and similar, then the present becomes a representative for him; but not if it is between benefit and harm. His statement: (He rules for her the mahr against the present, not the separation against the absent.) The expression of Jami' al-Fusulayn is: He rules for her the mahr against the present and the occurrence of triple divorce against the absent. So the claim consists of two things between which there is a causal relationship. He said (ضد): There is consideration, because the claim against the absent, which is the separation, is a condition for the claim against the present, not a cause. In such a case, the present is not appointed as representative for the absent according to the majority of the mashayikh. So it is fitting that he rules for the mahr against the present and not the separation against the absent. (صع) So by analogy with what (صذ) said, it is fitting that he rules in the issue of (فش), meaning the fatawa of Rashid al-Din, for the divorce of the claimant wife and not for the marriage of the absent. The upshot is that if the claim against the absent is a condition for the claim against the present, some say the present becomes a representative for the absent absolutely, which is the view of the majority; some say not absolutely, and that is...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) on the previous discussion. It clarifies the distinction between cause (sabab) and condition (shart) by quoting Rashid al-Din's fatawa. The correct view is that if the condition regarding the absent does not harm the absent (e.g., entering a house), the present can be his representative. However, if the condition brings both benefit and harm (as in the case of marriage vs. divorce), then the present cannot represent the absent. The commentator critiques a previous statement from Jami' al-Fusulayn that seemed to allow judgment for both mahr and divorce. He argues that the divorce (a condition) does not make the present a representative for the absent, so only the mahr should be awarded. He then applies this reasoning to Rashid al-Din's fatwa, suggesting that if the wife claims divorce based on a condition (the absent's marriage), only the divorce claim should be heard, not the marriage claim. The passage ends by summarizing the two positions: some mashayikh say the present becomes a representative absolutely if the absent's right is a condition, others say not absolutely.

Key Terms
تحشية (hashiya): Super-commentary that refines or critiques the main commentary.
سبب (sabab): Cause; a direct legal cause that triggers a ruling.
شرط (shart): Condition; a prerequisite for a ruling without being its direct cause.
خصم (khasm): Representative or litigant in court; one who stands in for another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, regardless of harm.
B) Never, conditions cannot establish representation.
C) Only if the condition does not harm the absent.
D) Only if the absent has appointed the present as agent.

Key Takeaway
The present can represent the absent only if the condition is harmless; when it brings harm, representation fails.

Judge Lending Orphan's Property: Conditions and Prohibitions
States the ruling with conditions and reasoning for lending orphan's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi judge may lend an orphan's property and distinguish the ruling for the father and executor.

Core Idea
A judge may only lend orphan's property as a recommended act under strict conditions; a father and executor are prohibited from lending.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The text distinguishes between the authority of a judge, a father, and an executor regarding lending an orphan's property. Understanding this distinction is crucial for guardians and judges in Islamic finance and estate management.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each party
Read the passage to determine the legal classification for the judge, father, and executor. The judge may lend (mustahabb), while the father and executor are prohibited.
The judge's lending is recommended, not obligatory.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The judge has authority to enforce repayment from the borrower; the father and executor lack this power. Thus lending by them is a voluntary act that makes them personally liable.
This reasoning (talil) is the basis for the difference in rulings.
3
List the conditions for the judge's lending
Identify the conditions from the passage: no executor present, no ghalla (profit) from the orphan's property, borrower must be trustworthy and solvent, and the loan must be documented.
These conditions ensure the orphan's property is safeguarded.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the father
Two narrations exist: one equates the father with the judge (permitting lending), the other with the executor (prohibiting). The more apparent view is prohibition.
The mufta bihi is that the father is like the executor.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi position
The judge may lend only when conditions are met; the father and executor are prohibited from lending orphan's property. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Consider the authority weight: zahir riwayah from Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويقرض القاضي مال اليتيم ويكتب الصك لا الوصي والأب) لأن القاضي يقدر على تحصيله من المستقرض والوصي والأب لا يقدران على ذلك فيضمنان بالإقراض لكونه تبرعاً .ابتداء والمراد ويستحب للقاضي الإقراض ولا يجوز للأب والوصي وإنما استحب منه لأن القاضي لكثرة اشتغاله لا يمكن أن يباشر الحفظ بنفسه فلا بد من الدفع لغيره والدفع بالقرض أنظر لليتيم لكونه مضموناً والوديعة أمانة ولا يقرض إلا من يعرفه بالأمانة والديانة ويكتب عليه ذلك ليحفظه خوف النسيان لكثرة اشتغاله. وفي البناية معزياً إلى تاج الشريعة : يقرض القاضي إلى الثقات والثقة المليء الحسن المعاملة. وفي الأقضية إنما يملك القاضي الإقراض إذا لم تحصل غلة لليتيم أما إذا وجدت فلا يملكه؛ هكذا روي عن محمد ا هـ. وفي المصباح : رجل مليء على فعيل غني مقتدر ويجوز الإبدال والإدغام ا هـ . وينبغي أن يشترط لجواز إقراض القاضي عدم وصي لليتيم فإن كان له وصي ولو منصوب التصرف في ماله من وهو ممنوع منه مع وجود وصيه في بيوع القنية . بین الأب والوصي مع أن في الأب روايتين ولكن أظهرهما أنه كالوصي وهو القاضي لم يجز المصنف وسوی له الصحيح . كما في جامع الفصولين. وفي خزانة الفتاوى الصحيح أن الأب كالقاضي فقد الرملي : وفي جامع الفصولين خلافه قوله : هذا ما ظهر لي (الآن أقول ما ظهر له غير ظاهر لقول الفتح الأصل أن ما كان شرطاً لثبوت الحق للحاضر من غير إبطال حق للغائب قبلت البينة فيه إذ ليس فيه قضاء على الغائب وما تضمن إبطالاً عليه لا تقبل .اهـ. ولا شك أن دخول رمضان ليس فيه إبطال حق على الغائب فلذا قبل بخلاف ثبوت الملك للغائب أو طلاق زوجته ونحو ذلك فإن فيه حكماً على الغائب ابتداء بلا فرق بين كون التعليق بصيغة إن طلق أو إن كانت مطلقة لأن المناط لحوق الضرر، فقياس هذه المسائل على ما في الخلاصة قياس مع الفارق. هذا ما ظهر لي فتدبره قوله : (أما إذا وجد فلا يملكه قال الرملي : كما إذا وجد ما يشتريه له يكون له ربح ويقرض القاضي
His statement: (The judge lends the orphan's property and writes the document; the executor and the father do not.) Because the judge is able to collect it from the borrower, while the executor and father are not able to do so, so they become liable by lending because it is a voluntary act initially. And the meaning: it is recommended for the judge to lend, and it is not permissible for the father and executor. It is recommended from him because the judge, due to his many preoccupations, cannot personally undertake custody, so he must hand it over to someone else, and handing over by loan is better for the orphan because it is guaranteed, whereas a deposit is a trust. And he only lends to one he knows for trustworthiness and religiousness, and he writes it down to preserve it out of fear of forgetfulness due to his many preoccupations. In al-Binaya, attributed to Taj al-Shari'a: The judge lends to trustworthy persons, and the trustworthy person is solvent and good in transactions. In al-Aqdiyya: The judge only has the capacity to lend if no profit (ghalla) accrues to the orphan; if it exists, he cannot lend; thus narrated from Muhammad. In al-Misbah: A solvent man (mali') is on the pattern of fa'il: rich, capable; and substitution and assimilation are permissible. It is necessary to stipulate for the permissibility of the judge's lending that there be no executor for the orphan, for if he has an executor, even if appointed to manage his wealth, he is prohibited from lending in the presence of his executor in the sales of al-Qun'ya. Between the father and the executor, although there are two narrations about the father, the more apparent is that he is like the executor, and the judge... (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage comments on a matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that states: 'The judge lends the orphan's property and writes the document; the executor and the father do not.' The reasoning is that the judge has the authority to enforce repayment from the borrower, while the father and executor do not, so if they lend, they become personally liable (guarantors) because it is a voluntary transfer without authority. The lending is recommended for the judge because he cannot personally safeguard the property due to his duties, and lending is preferable to depositing because it guarantees the capital. The judge must lend only to trustworthy, solvent individuals of good conduct, and document the loan to avoid forgetfulness. Additionally, the judge may only lend if there is no profit (ghalla) from the orphan's property; if there is profit, the orphan's needs are met, so lending is unnecessary and thus not allowed. Another condition is that there be no executor for the orphan; if there is an executor, the judge is prohibited from lending, as the executor is responsible. Regarding the father, there are two narrations: one allows him to lend like the judge, the other treats him like the executor (prohibited). The more apparent view is that the father is like the executor. The passage concludes with a discussion about the previous topic (hiyal) and a critique of applying the method from al-Khulasa to all cases, emphasizing that matters causing harm to the absent are different.

Key Terms
إقراض (iqrad): The act of lending money, here specifically from orphan's property by the judge.
يتيم (yatim): A minor orphan whose father has died and who is under guardianship.
وصي (wasi): An executor appointed by the father or judge to manage the orphan's affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition may the judge lend orphan's property?
A) Any time, as long as he documents the loan.
B) Only if there is no executor for the orphan and no profit (ghalla) from the orphan's property.
C) Only if the orphan's guardian consents.
D) Only if the borrower is a relative of the orphan.

Key Takeaway
The judge may lend orphan's property only if no executor exists and no ghalla, and must document the loan; the father and executor are prohibited from lending under normal circumstances.

Legal Trick to Prove Entry of Ramadan via Suspended Agency
Explaining a legal stratagem to prove the entry of Ramadan and similar facts through suspended agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi stratagem for proving an event via suspended agency and explain its validity despite the deficient nature of acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Suspend agency on an existing fact to prove it indirectly without ruling against an absent party.

This lesson unpacks a clever Hanafi legal stratagem (hila) used in courts to establish the occurrence of an event like the start of Ramadan when one party is absent. The method avoids direct ruling against the absent party by focusing on a suspended power of attorney. We will examine the reasoning behind its validity and its extension to other areas like ownership, marriage, and divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its context
The passage declares the stratagem as the relied-upon view (mu'tamad) in the Hanafi school. Identify the context: proving entry of Ramadan or similar facts without directly ruling against an absent party.
The trick is permissible (mubah) and not sinful.
2
Understand the objection and response
An objection is raised: acknowledgment (iqrar) is a deficient proof (hujja qasira). The author responds that the trick circumvents this deficiency by focusing on the agency itself, not the direct acknowledgment of the event.
The deficiency of iqrar is that it only binds the one who makes it, not the absent party.
3
Trace the method from al-Khulasa
The passage quotes al-Khulasa: one suspends an agency on the entry of Ramadan, the parties dispute the entry, witnesses testify, and the judge rules on the agency and the entry simultaneously. Note that the agency is suspended on an existing condition (entry) not a future act of the absent.
This method avoids the problem of ruling against an absent party because the ruling is on the agency, which is conditional.
4
Apply to other cases
The same trick works for proving ownership, endowment, marriage, or divorce. The key is to suspend agency on an existing status (e.g., 'if so-and-so is the owner') and not on a future act of the absent party (e.g., 'if he marries').
Suspending on a future act would require the absent party's action, which cannot be proven without their presence.
5
Identify the dispute and tarjih
Some scholars object that the trick is invalid because it still indirectly imposes a ruling. The Hanafi response: the opponent can contest the existence of the fact, and the trick does not directly rule against the absent. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi as per al-Khulasa and the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
The dispute is legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi view is relied upon (mu'tamad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو المعتمد. والجواب عما أورد أنه لكون الإقرار حجة قاصرة كما لا يخفى. وفي الخلاصة : الطريق إلى إثبات الرمضانية أن يعلق وكالة بدخوله فيتنازعان في دخوله فيشهد الشهود فيقضي بالوكالة وبدخوله ا هـ . وعلى هذا إذا أريد إثبات طلاق معلق بدخول شهر فالحيلة فيه ذلك ولو كان الزوج غائباً وليس هذا من قبيل الشرط لأنه لا بد أن يكون فعل الغائب، وعلى هذا إذا أريد إثبات شيء من ملك ووقف ونكاح وطلاق فيعلق وكالة بملك فلان ذلك الشيء ويدعي الوكيل فيقول الخصم وكالتك معلقة بما لم يوجد فيقول الوكيل بل هي منجزة لأنها معلقة بأمر كائن ويبرهن على الملك . وكذا في الوقف يعلقها بالوقفية، وفي النكاح بكون فلانة زوجة فلان وفي الطلاق بكونها محرمة عليه ولا يعلقها بفعل الغائب كإن نكح إن وقف إن طلق إن ملك هذا ما ظهر لي الآن والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم وهذا التقرير في هذا المحل كغيره من خواص هذا الشرح ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم.
So it is the relied-upon [view]. And the answer to what was objected is that it is because the acknowledgment (iqrar) is a deficient proof (hujja qasira) as is not hidden. And in al-Khulasa: The way to prove the entry of Ramadan is that he suspends an agency upon its entry, so they dispute about its entry, then the witnesses testify, and the judge rules for the agency and its entry, end. And upon this, if one wants to prove a suspended divorce upon the entry of a month, the trick (hila) is that, even if the husband is absent, and this is not of the type of condition because it must be an act of the absent [party]. And upon this, if one wants to prove something of ownership, endowment, marriage, or divorce, he suspends an agency upon the ownership of so-and-so of that thing, and the agent claims, and the opponent says: 'Your agency is suspended upon something that does not exist,' and the agent says: 'Rather it is immediate (munjaza) because it is suspended upon an existing matter,' and he proves the ownership. Similarly for endowment: he suspends it upon endower status, for marriage: upon so-and-so being the wife of so-and-so, for divorce: upon her being prohibited to him. And he does not suspend it upon an act of the absent, like 'if he marries', 'if endows', 'if divorces', 'if owns'. This is what appeared to me now, and Allah most high knows best. And this explanation in this place, like others, is among the special features of this commentary. There is no power and no strength except with Allah, the Most High, the Magnificent.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) to prove the occurrence of a specific event, such as the entry of Ramadan, in a court case. The method involves suspending a power of attorney (wakala) on the happening of that event. The judge then hears testimony about the event and can rule on the agency, indirectly establishing the event without ruling against an absent party. The passage responds to an objection that acknowledgment (iqrar) is a deficient proof. The trick avoids direct proof of the event against an absent party by proving the agency which is conditional upon it. The author extends this to similar cases: proving a suspended divorce upon entering a month, or proving ownership, endowment, marriage, or divorce. The key is that the agency must be suspended on an existing status (like being a wife) rather than on a future act of the absent person (like 'if he marries'). This distinction preserves the principle that one cannot rule against an absent party. The author notes this as his own contribution to the commentary.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem or trick that uses a permissible mechanism to achieve a lawful goal, such as proving a fact without violating procedural rules.
الوكالة المعلقة (al-wikala al-mu'allaqa): A power of attorney that is suspended upon the occurrence of a specific condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key restriction on the condition used to suspend the agency in the Hanafi stratagem?
A) It must be a future act of the absent party
B) It must be an existing status like ownership or marriage
C) It must be witnessed by two men
D) It must be written down

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a suspended agency can be a valid stratagem to prove an existing fact without ruling directly against an absent party, provided the condition is an existing status.

Judge's Authority to Lend Orphan's Money: The Father's Status Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the father's authority to lend orphan's money.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the judge lending orphan's money, identify the conditions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the father is like the judge or the guardian.

Core Idea
The judge may lend orphan’s money; the father and guardian cannot—but Hanafi scholars debate the father's status.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that the judge is permitted, even recommended, to lend orphan's money under certain conditions, while the guardian and father are generally prohibited. However, there is a significant disagreement within the Hanafi school: is the father like the judge or like the guardian? We will dissect the arguments and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
State who may lend orphan's money and why, based on the passage. The judge may lend; the guardian and father may not, because the judge can recover the debt.
Note the reasoning: the judge has power to collect, the guardian/father do not.
2
List the conditions for the judge's lending
Determine the requirements: no profit (ghalla) from other investments, no guardian exists, borrower must be trustworthy and solvent, and the judge must write a document.
These conditions are derived from the text.
3
Locate the dispute
Identify the two positions regarding the father: one narration treats him like the guardian (cannot lend), another treats him like the judge (can lend).
The author prefers the father like the guardian; al-Ramli prefers the father like the judge.
4
Examine the evidence for each position
Analyze the proofs: the author cites the father's inability to recover; al-Ramli relies on the absolute wording of the texts (mutun).
The author uses qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Understand the practical difference: if the father is like the guardian, lending makes him liable; if like the judge, he is not. The author's view is mufta bihi in this text.
The dispute is unresolved, but the author's view is preferred in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

يملكه قال الرملي : كما إذا وجد ما يشتريه له يكون له ربح ويقرض القاضي اختلف التصحيح والمعتمد ما في المتون. وأطلق في منع إقراض الأب فشمل ما إذا أخذ مال ولده الصغير قرضاً لنفسه وهو مروي عن الإمام، وقيل أما إذا علم فلا لأنه يمكنه بعثه إلى الغائب إذا خاف التلف. قالوا : وله أن يأخذ المال من الأب إذا كان مسرفاً مبذراً ويضعه على يد عدل؛ كذا في القنية. وفي جامع الفصولين : إنما يملك القاضي إقراضه إذا لم يعد خيانة فلا يعزل به ا هـ. وأطلق في الوصي فشمل وصي القاضي كما في جامع الفصولين، وأشار بالوصي إلى أن متولي الوقف ليس له إقراض مال المسجد فلو أقرضه ضمن وكذا يضمن المستقرض ؛ كذا في الخزانة. وليس له إيداعه إلا ممن هو في عياله؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين ثم قال بعده : القيم لو أقرض مال المسجد ليأخذه عند أحرز من إمساكه فلا بأس به وفي العدة للمتولي إقراض ما فضل من الوقف لو أحرز ا.هـ. وقدمنا في كتاب الوقف الحاكم والأصح أن الوصي لا يملك أن يستقرض ماله وقيل يملكه لو ملياً ا هـ . وفي تهذيب القلانسي: ويصدق القاضي فيما قاله من التصرف في الأوقاف وأموال الأيتام والغائبين من أداء وقبض ا هـ. وفي شرح أدب القضاء الأب كالأب الحاجة وهو : يسع غلة مال اليتيم ويكتب الصك لا الوصي والأب أو وجد من يضارب فيه كما سينقله عن جامع الفصولين قوله : (وينبغي أن يشترط الخ قال الرملي : إطلاق المتون يدل على خلافه، وهذا وإن كان من التصرف في ماله لكنه تصرف لا يمكله الوصي وهو أحسن تصرف فا في مال اليتيم وأنظر . فإذا قلنا لم يجز منه والوصي ممنوع من الإقراض امتنع النظر لليتيم في ذلك ولا قائل به تأمل اهـ. وفيه نظر فإن المقصود حفظ ماله وإنما يقرضه القاضي لكثرة اشتغاله وقدرته على التحصيل كما ، فكأن المسوغ له
His saying: (And the judge lends the orphan's property and writes the document, not the guardian and the father) because the judge is able to collect it from the borrower, while the guardian and father are not able to do that, so they are liable (damin) for lending because it is a donation initially. And the meaning is: lending is recommended (mustahabb) for the judge, but not permissible for the father and guardian. It is recommended for him because the judge, due to his many occupations, cannot personally undertake custody, so he must hand over to someone else, and handing over as a loan is better for the orphan because it is guaranteed, while a deposit is a trust. He only lends to those he knows to be trustworthy and religious, and writes it down to preserve it for fear of forgetfulness due to his many occupations. And in al-Binaya, citing Taj al-Shari'a: The judge lends to trustworthy people, and the trustworthy person is solvent (mali'), of good dealing. And in al-Aqdiya: The judge only has the power to lend if there is no profit (ghalla) for the orphan; if profit exists, he does not have that power; thus it is narrated from Muhammad, end. And in al-Misbah: A man mali' (on the pattern of fa'il) means wealthy, capable; substitution and assimilation are permissible, end. And it is appropriate to stipulate for the permissibility of the judge's lending that there be no guardian for the orphan; if he has a guardian, even if appointed to manage his property, he is prevented from it in the presence of his guardian in sales of al-Quniyah. Between the father and the guardian, although there are two narrations concerning the father, the more apparent is that he is like the guardian, and the judge did not permit it according to the author, and equal? Because as for him, the correct is as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in Khizanat al-Fatawa, the correct is that the father is like the judge; al-Ramli said: In Jami' al-Fusulayn there is a contrary opinion. He said: This is what appeared to me (now I say: what appeared to him is not apparent because the text of al-Fath: The principle is that whatever is a condition for establishing a right for the present without invalidating a right of the absent, the evidence is accepted, because it is not a ruling against the absent; whatever involves invalidation of a right upon him is not accepted. End. There is no doubt that the entry of Ramadan does not invalidate a right of the absent, so it is accepted, unlike establishing ownership for the absent or divorce of his wife and the like, because that is a ruling against the absent initially, with no difference between the suspension being in the form 'if he divorces' or 'if she is divorced', because the criterion is the attachment of harm. So analogizing these issues to what is in al-Khulasa is an analogy with a difference. This is what appeared to me, so ponder it. His saying: (As for if profit exists, he does not have that power) al-Ramli said: As if he finds something to buy for him that would yield profit, and the judge lends... The correction differs, and the relied-upon is what is in the texts (mutun). And he made absolute in prohibiting the father's lending, so it includes when he takes his minor child's property as a loan for himself, and that is narrated from Imam [Abu Hanifa], and it is said he may do that. And it is incumbent upon the judge to check on the conditions of those to whom he lent orphan's property, so that if someone becomes insolvent, he takes back the money from them, because the judge, though able to recover, can only recover from the wealthy, not the poor; hence he cannot initially lend to the insolvent, so likewise he should not leave it with him at the end. And the author indicated that the judge has authority to lend endowment (waqf) property, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn, and he may lend found property (luqata) from the finder, and lend the property of an absent person, and he may sell his movable property if he fears loss if he does not know the whereabouts of the absent; but if he knows, he cannot because he can send it to the absent if he fears loss. They said: He may take property from the father if he is a wasteful spendthrift and place it with a trustworthy person; as in al-Quniyah. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: The judge only has the power to lend if he does not find something to buy that would yield profit for the orphan; not if he finds it or finds someone to do mudaraba, because that is more beneficial. Similarly, he only lends to a solvent person, end. And it is restricted to lending because the guardian can sell on credit, as mentioned in the chapters on wills. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the guardian lends, it is not considered betrayal, so he is not dismissed for it, end. And he made it absolute concerning the guardian, so it includes the judge's appointee as guardian, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And he indicated by 'guardian' that the mutawalli (caretaker) of a waqf cannot lend the property of the mosque; if he lends, he is liable, and likewise the borrower is liable; as in al-Khazana. And he may not deposit it except with someone who is in his household; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. Then after it he said: The qayyim (trustee) if he lends the mosque's property to take it when it is safer than keeping it, there is no harm. And in al-'Uddah, the mutawalli may lend what is surplus of the waqf if it is safer, end. And we have presented in the Book of Waqf the ruling if the mutawalli lends waqf property by order of the judge from the imam and dies insolvent. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the guardian borrows the orphan's property and profits with it, then spends on him for a period, he is considered a volunteer if he becomes a guarantor; he does not become free unless he raises his matter to the judge. The more correct view is that the guardian cannot borrow his own property; and it is said he may if he is solvent, end. And in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: The judge is believed regarding what he says about disposition in endowments and the properties of orphans and absent persons, whether payment or receipt, end. And in Sharh Adab al-Qada: The judge's lending is more beneficial for the child and more protective of his property because it is guaranteed and because of his ability to recover. And they said: The guardian may deposit but not lend. And I have not seen the ruling on the grandfather regarding the permissibility of his lending according to the narration of its permissibility for the father; apparently he is like the father because they said: The grandfather is the father of the father, like the father. The need: It suffices (?) the profit of the orphan's property and writes the document, not the guardian and father, or if he finds someone to do mudaraba as he will quote from Jami' al-Fusulayn. His saying: (It is appropriate to stipulate... ) al-Ramli said: The absolute wording of the texts indicates otherwise. And this, though it is a disposition of his property, is a disposition that the guardian cannot do, and it is the best kind of disposition in the orphan's property, so consider. So if we said it is not permitted from him, and the guardian is prohibited from lending, the welfare of the orphan is obstructed in this, and no one says this, so ponder, end. But there is consideration: the purpose is the preservation of his property, and the judge only lends due to his many occupations and his ability to recover as... so it seems the motivator for him...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that the judge may lend the orphan's money and write the document, but the guardian (wasi) and the father (ab) may not. The reason is that the judge has the authority to recover the debt from the borrower, while the guardian and father lack that power, so they would be liable if they lent because it is a donation (tabarru'). Lending is recommended (mustahabb) for the judge because he cannot personally safeguard the money due to his many duties, and a loan is better than a deposit because it is guaranteed. The judge only lends to trustworthy, solvent individuals. He writes a document to avoid forgetfulness. Conditions are discussed: the judge may only lend if there is no profit (ghalla) from other investments like mudaraba or purchasing trade goods. If a guardian exists, the judge cannot lend because the guardian should handle it. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the father: some narrations say he is like the guardian (cannot lend), others say he is like the judge (can lend). The author leans towards the father being like the guardian, but al-Ramli disagrees and relies on the texts that treat the father like the judge when there is no guardian. The passage also covers related matters: the judge's authority to lend waqf property, found property, and property of absent persons; selling movable property if loss is feared; taking property from a wasteful father; and the guardian's inability to lend but ability to sell on credit. The commentary concludes with a note on the grandfather's status, suggesting he is like the father.

Key Terms
الإقراض (al-iqrad): Lending money or property with the expectation of repayment, here specifically of orphan's property by the judge.
الوصي (al-wasi): The guardian appointed by the deceased father or by the judge to manage the orphan's property.
الغلة (al-ghalla): Profit or income generated from the orphan's property, such as from trade or mudaraba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, who may lend orphan's property?
A) The judge only
B) The father only
C) The guardian only
D) Both the judge and the father

Key Takeaway
The judge may lend orphan's money only when no profit alternative exists and the borrower is solvent; the father is generally prohibited from lending, though some Hanafi scholars allow it.

Qada — Judiciary
