الفصل أي فصل المحتجب والمخدرة ؛ أبو السعود قوله : ( لكون المطلق أزيد من المقيد) لأن المطلق يثبت من الأصل حتى يستحق به الزوائد والمقيد بسبب يقتصر على وقت وجوب السبب.
Book of Hawalah, Chapter of Tahkim: The statement: 'And if he adds 'I do not know you' — i.e., he adds his saying 'I do not know you' to his saying 'You never had anything against me' — his evidence is not accepted. The meaning includes similar phrases like 'I have not seen you' or 'no transaction or mixing occurred between us' or 'no taking or giving' or 'I have never met you in any place' as in Fath al-Qadir. It is not accepted because reconciliation between his two statements is impossible, since there cannot be a transaction between two people without acquaintance. It is reported from our companions (al-Quduri) that it is accepted because reconciliation is possible, as a hidden person (muhtajab) from among men or a secluded woman might be harmed by disturbance at his door, so he orders one of his deputies to satisfy the opponent without knowing him. His statement: 'One should ponder the reason for non-remission' — he said in al-Minh: apparently, since the defendant is denying, his liability is not burdened with anything in his view, so how can set-off occur? And Allah knows best. He narrated it from al-Ramli with an addition: 'Or we say: his persistence in denial is a rejection of what the plaintiff admitted, and that is something that is rejected by rejection.' His statement: 'And it is said that evidence for release is accepted in this chapter' — said by the author of al-Kafi as mentioned by al-Ayni, and his statement in this: 'Then he knows him.' He derived in al-Nihayah following Qadi Khan that if the defendant is one who personally conducts his affairs, it is not accepted. So the muhtajab is one who does not personally conduct his affairs; it is said he is one whom not everyone sees due to his grandeur. In al-Qamus: al-shaghab (with fath) is said to be inciting evil. In Islah al-Idah: there is consideration because the basis of possible reconciliation is that one of them is not personally conducting his affairs, not specifically the defendant; and al-Quduri's depiction of possible reconciliation does not indicate that. Its rebuttal is apparent because the whole discussion is about the contradiction of the defendant, not the plaintiff. The author indicated that if reconciliation is not possible, the contradiction is not removed. For example, what is in al-Miraj attributed to al-Shafi: if he said 'I did not pay him anything' then claimed payment, it is not heard because it is impossible for him to say 'I did not pay him anything' when he did pay. But if he claimed his admission of payment or judgment, it should be heard because the self-contradictor is one who combines two statements, and here he did not combine; for this reason if the plaintiff confirmed him explicitly, he would not be contradictory. Mentioned by al-Tamartashi. From here I answered an incident: he gave permission to pay money to his brother, then he claimed against him that he did not pay, so he said 'I paid' then said 'I did not pay'; the court ruled against him; then the brother came and confirmed that he had paid; then he is discharged because the brother's confirmation (who was authorized to receive) is like the plaintiff's confirmation, and you have already learned the case when the plaintiff confirms. And it is said that evidence for release is accepted in this chapter by agreement of narrations because release is realized without acquaintance. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: if he claimed absolute ownership against him, then he claimed against him at that time before the judge due to a cause, it is accepted and his evidence is heard, unlike the reverse unless the reverser says 'I meant by the second absolute the first restricted because the absolute is more than the restricted.' And upon this is the fatwa; stated by Shams al-Aimmah. If he claimed offspring first then restricted ownership; by analogy from what they mentioned, if he claimed offspring and witnesses testified to restricted ownership, it should not be accepted, but it should be? And in Iqrar al-Bazzaziyyah: he confessed to selling his slave to so-and-so, then it becomes valid because confession to a sale without price is a false confession. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: if he guaranteed the price or dowry, then the guarantor proved the invalidity of the sale or marriage, it is not accepted because his undertaking to pay money is an admission of the validity of the cause of obligation; so the claim of invalidity is not heard from him afterwards. But if he proved payment by the principal or his release, it is accepted because it is confirmation of the prior obligation. If he guaranteed for a man who claims a thousand, and the guarantor proved that the claimed thousand is the price of wine, it is not accepted. If the guarantor said the claimed thousand is gambling or price of wine or the like that is not obligatory, his statement is not accepted. If he proved the admission of the creditor while he (the principal) is denying, his statement is not accepted, and he cannot administer an oath to the claimant. If the claimant confessed before the judge, both the principal and guarantor are discharged. I say: it cannot be said that since they are discharged by his confession, the evidence of his confession should be accepted, because evidence is heard when the claim is valid, and here it is invalid due to contradiction, because his guaranty is an admission of its validity. In al-Ikhtiyar: every two contradictory statements emanating from... The chapter: i.e., the chapter on the veiled and secluded woman; Abu al-Su'ud's statement: 'because the absolute is more than the restricted' because the absolute establishes from the beginning including its accessories, while the restricted by a cause is limited to the time of the cause's existence.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the acceptance of evidence (bayyinah) in cases where a party has made contradictory statements. The main issue discussed is when a defendant first denies any debt ('you never had anything against me') and then adds 'I do not know you.' The general Hanafi ruling is that his evidence (e.g., to prove payment) is not accepted because the two statements are contradictory—if there was no debt, there was nothing to pay, and claiming ignorance of the plaintiff contradicts the possibility of a transaction. Reconciliation (tawfiq) is impossible. However, al-Quduri reports a view that it is accepted if the defendant is a muhtajab (a person of high status who does not personally handle his affairs), because he might have delegated the payment to an agent without knowing the plaintiff. The passage also discusses the 'fivefold issue' (al-mas'alah al-mukhamsah): if a plaintiff claims 200 dirhams, proves 150 were paid, and then the defendant proves he paid the remaining 50, the evidence is not accepted unless both parties specify that the 50 is from the 150 claimed (because the 150 already includes a fifty). Further, the passage addresses cases of contradictory statements regarding absolute versus restricted claims, and several rules concerning guarantors (kafalah) presenting evidence after contradicting themselves.

Key Terms
المسألة المخمسة (al-mas'alah al-mukhamsah): The fivefold issue: a specific scenario where a claim of payment is ambiguous due to overlap of amounts.
الكفيل (al-kafil): Guarantor; one who undertakes to pay an obligation if the principal fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fivefold issue, why is the defendant's evidence of paying 50 dirhams not accepted?
A) Because he did not have witnesses.
B) Because the 50 is not distinct from the 150 already admitted.
C) Because he admitted the debt earlier.
D) Because the plaintiff denied receiving the 50.

Key Takeaway
In financial disputes, specificity is crucial; a payment that overlaps with a previously admitted amount must be explicitly distinguished, and a guarantor cannot challenge the validity of the obligation he guaranteed.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Resolving Contradiction: Sharʿī Takdhīb and Abandonment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on when contradictory claims prevent evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi mechanisms for resolving contradictory claims (sharʿī takdhīb and abandonment) and apply them to determine when evidence is accepted.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not final; it is resolved when the Sharia deems the first claim false or the claimant abandons it.

In Hanafi procedural law, a claimant who makes two contradictory statements is not automatically barred from evidence. This lesson examines the core principle that such contradiction can be removed either by the opponent's proof that the first claim is false (sharʿī takdhīb) or by the claimant's explicit retraction. The passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq illustrates these mechanisms through the example of a sale denied and then a defect return claimed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory claims
Read the scenario: the claimant first denies a sale, then later claims a return due to defect. Note that these are legally incompatible – denial of sale negates the possibility of returning for defect.
The contradiction is between 'no sale' and 'sale with defect'.
2
Determine the resolution by sharʿī takdhīb
The opponent proves the sale with evidence. This legally 'deems the first claim false' (sharʿī takdhīb). The contradiction is eliminated because the first claim is now effectively false in the eyes of the law.
The claimant is treated as one who lied only about the denial, not about the defect.
3
Check for explicit abandonment
If the claimant says 'I abandon the first statement and settle on the second', the contradiction is also resolved. This is a voluntary retraction.
Abandonment is accepted as long as the second claim is not itself contradictory to established evidence.
4
Apply to the case of iqālah
Contrast with iqālah (mutual rescission): claiming iqālah after denying the sale is not accepted because iqālah acknowledges the original sale, thus affirming the contract rather than removing contradiction.
Iqālah is a separate contract that confirms the original sale existed.
5
State the ruling
In the Hanafi school, the claimant's evidence for the defect return is heard because the contradiction has been removed. The general principle: contradictory claims do not automatically bar evidence; the context matters.
This is the muftā bihi position.

اشترط أن يكون الثاني عند القاضي يكفي في تحقق التناقض كون الثاني عند الحاكم. ثم قال في فصل الدفع وفي المحيط : ادعى على آخر عند غير الحاكم بالشراء أو الإرث ثم ادعاه عند الحاكم مطلقاً إن ادعى الشراء من معروف لا تقبل، وإن ادعاه من مجهول ثم المطلق عند الحاكم تقبل دلت المسألة أنه لا يشترط في التناقض كون المتدافعين في مجلس الحكم بل يكتفي بكون الثاني في مجلس الحكم اهـ. وقدمنا أنه المعتمد. ثم اعلم أن بالبيع فما معنى قوله «لم يقض القاضي بالبيع؟ وأقول : الجواب النافع إن شاء الله ما يستفاد من كتاب نور العين في غير هذا المحل وفي غير هذه المسألة، وهو أن الكفيل لما التحق زعمه بالعدم وثبت خلافه وهو كونه كفيلاً لم يسع في إعادة زعمه ولم يرد نقض البينة بل رضي بموجبها حتى جعله مبني لدعواه الرجوع على الأصيل، وأما البائع في مسألتنا فقد سعى في إعادة ما آل زعمه وهو براءة ذمته بعد التحاقه بالعدم بثبوت خلافه وأراد نقض ما أثبتته البيئة وهو عدم براءة ذمته، فهذا فرق واضح حق. وكذا يقال في دعوى الإقالة لأنها فسخ للعقد الذي أثبته الخصم بالبينة ففيه تقرير لموجبها، ومثله يقال في مسألة البزازية الأخيرة فاحفظه فإنه ينفعك في كثير من أمثال هذه المسائل.

قوله : (لا بد أن يكون عند القاضي قدمنا الكلام عليه عند قوله «ادعى داراً في يد رجل» فراجعه قوله: (ثم اعلم أن المتناقض إذا قال تركت الكلام الأول الخ) قدم المسألة في شرح قوله

المدعى عليه بأنك كنت ادعيته قبل هذا مقيداً وبرهن عليه فقال المدعي ادعيه الآن بهذا السبب وتركت المطلق يقبل ويبطل الدفع ا هـ. ثم اعلم أن التناقض المانع أما أن يسمع الحاكم الكلامين أو يسمع الثاني فيدفع المدعى عليه أنه قال أولاً كذا يريد دفعه فينكر فيبرهن المدعى عليه على قوله الأول فيثبت التناقض وهذا هو طريق دفع الدعوى وسيأتي بيانه إن شاء الله تعالى في المخمسة من الدعوى. وفي الظهيرية : ادعى عليه أن أباك أوصى لي بثلث ماله فأنكر المدعى عليه الوصية فبرهن المدعي فقال المدعى عليه إن أبى رجع عن هذ الوصية قيل لا هذا الدفع والصحيح أنه صحيح، وكذا لو برهن على جحود أبيه بناء على أن الجحود يصح رجوع ا هـ . وفيها : ادعت امرأة على ورثة زوجها المهر فأنكروا نكاحها فبرهنت فدفعوا بأنها كانت أبرأت أباناً في حياته إن قالوا أبرأته عن المهر لا يصح للتناقض، وإن قالوا أبرأته عن دعوى المهر صح ا هـ . وفي البزازية : ادعى عليه ألف درهم ثمن جارية بشرائط وعجز عن إثباتها فقال كانت الألف وديعة عنده لا تقبل، ولو ادعى كونها وديعة فعجز فادعى كونها قرضاً تقبل ا هـ .

أن ادعى داراً في يد رجل الخ والأولى ما عبر به في فصل الاستحقاق حيث قال: ثم اعلم المتناقض الذي لا تسمع دعواه إذا قال تركت أحد الكلامين فإنه يقبل منه اهـ . لأن قوله هنا إذا قال تركت الكلام الأول الخ ) لا يوافقه كلام البزازية. ثم إن كلام البزازية لا يدل على أن هذا قاعدة كلية كما يقتضيه كلام المؤلف بل في هذه الصورة الجزئية وفي الحقيقة رجوعه عن الإطلاق إلى التقييد من قبيل التوفيق يدل عليه قول الخانية حتى لو قال أردت بهذا الملك المطلق الملك بذلك السبب تسمع دعواه وتقبل بينته فليتأمل.
And from what we have established, it is clear that the author's restriction of the book's issue to the claim of return due to defect after denying the original sale is to exclude the claim of rescission (iqālah). And a distinction between them is needed, as is needed in what is in al-Bazzāziyyah: He claimed against him the purchase of his slave, so he denied, then he proved it, then he claimed that he returned it to him due to a defect—it is heard because he became deemed a liar in denying the purchase, so the contradiction was removed by the shar‘ī takdhīb just as it is removed by the opponent's affirmation. Etc. Jāmi‘ al-Fuṣūlayn: If he said, 'There is no marriage between me and you,' then when she proved the marriage, he proved the khul‘, his evidence is accepted. If he said, 'There was never a marriage between us,' or 'I never married her,' and the rest remains... This and the defect case should be the same. And there, in Ẓāhir al-Riwāya, the evidence of innocence from defect is not accepted because innocence from defect is an acknowledgment of the sale; similarly, khul‘ implies previous marriage, so contradiction is established. Etc. Then know that contradiction between two claims must be before the judge, as indicated by what is in al-Ajnās and al-Ṣughrā: He claimed a limited thing by purchase or inheritance, then claimed it as absolute ownership—it is not heard if the first claim was before the judge. But if it was not before the judge, then this and the first are the same. Al-Bazzāzī said: This is according to the narration that contradiction only occurs if both claims are before the judge. As for the condition that the second must be before the judge, it is sufficient for establishing contradiction that the second is before the ruler. Then he said in the chapter on al-Daf‘ and in al-Muḥīṭ: He claimed against another before a non-judge by purchase or inheritance, then claimed it absolutely before the judge—if he claimed the purchase from a known person, it is not accepted; if he claimed it from an unknown person, then the absolute claim before the judge is accepted. The issue indicates that it is not a condition for contradiction that the two lawsuits be in the same court session; rather, it suffices that the second is in the court session. He said: And we have stated that it is relied upon. Then know that the contradictory person, if he says, 'I abandon the first statement and settle on the second,' it is accepted from him. He said in al-Bazzāziyyah and al-Dhakhīrah: He claimed it absolutely, so he pushed it away from him... said al-Ramlī: You need to reflect on this answer. That is, because the judgment of purchase is a judgment of sale, so what is the meaning of his statement 'the judge did not judge by sale'? I say the beneficial answer, God willing, is what is learned from the book Nūr al-‘Ayn in other places and other issues: that when the guarantor's claim became non-existent and its contrary—that he is a guarantor—was established, he did not strive to revive his claim nor did he seek to nullify the evidence, but rather was satisfied with its implication, even making it the basis for his claim to recourse against the principal. As for the seller in our issue, he strove to revive his claim—the freedom of his liability—after it became non-existent by the establishment of its opposite, and he wanted to nullify what the evidence established, which is the non-freedom of his liability. So this is a clear and correct distinction. Similarly, it is said in the claim of rescission because it is a cancellation of the contract that the opponent established with evidence, so it contains affirmation of its implication. And similarly, it is said in the last issue of al-Bazzāziyyah. So memorize it, for it will benefit you in many such issues.

His statement: (It must be before the judge) we have already discussed it when discussing his statement: 'He claimed a house in the hand of a man,' so refer to it. His statement: (Then know that the contradictory person if he says, 'I abandon the first statement...') I presented the issue in the explanation of his statement.

The defendant: 'You claimed this before as restricted and proved it,' so the plaintiff says, 'I claim it now with this cause and I abandon the absolute,' it is accepted and the defense is invalidated. Then know that the contradiction that prevents: either the ruler hears both statements, or he hears the second and the defendant argues that he said the first initially, intending to refute it, so he denies, then the defendant proves his first statement, so the contradiction is established. This is the method of defending the lawsuit, and its explanation will come, God willing, in the fifth chapter of al-Da‘wā. And in al-Ẓahīriyyah: He claimed against him that your father bequeathed to me one-third of his wealth, so the defendant denied the bequest, so the plaintiff proved it, then the defendant said, 'My father revoked this bequest.' It was said: This defense is not valid, but the correct view is that it is valid. Similarly, if he proved his father's denial, on the basis that denial can be revoked. And in it: A woman claimed against the heirs of her husband the dowry, so they denied her marriage, so she proved it, then they argued that she had released her father during his lifetime—if they say 'she released him from the dowry,' it is invalid due to contradiction; but if they say 'she released him from the claim of dowry,' it is valid. And in al-Bazzāziyyah: He claimed against him a thousand dirhams as the price of a slave girl with conditions, and he was unable to prove it, so he said, 'The thousand was a deposit with him'—it is not accepted. If he claimed it was a deposit, then was unable, then claimed it was a loan—it is accepted.

Then: 'He claimed a house in the hand of a man, etc., and the better is what he expressed in the chapter on istiḥqāq where he said: Then know: the contradictory person whose claim is not heard, if he says, 'I abandon one of the two statements,' then it is accepted from him. Because his statement here 'if he says, 'I abandon the first statement'' does not agree with the statement of al-Bazzāziyyah. Then the statement of al-Bazzāziyyah does not indicate that this is a general rule as the author's statement implies; rather, in this particular case, in reality his return from the absolute to the restricted is of the type of reconciliation, as indicated by the statement of al-Khāniyyah: even if he said, 'By this absolute ownership I meant the ownership by that cause,' his claim is heard and his evidence is accepted. So reflect.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of contradictory claims (tanāquḍ) on the admissibility of evidence. The commentary expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqāʾiq. The author begins by explaining the author's (of Kanz) restriction of the issue to a specific scenario to exclude related cases. He then cites various scenarios from al-Bazzāziyyah, Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn, and others to illustrate when contradiction is considered and when it is resolved. Key points: 1) Contradiction occurs when a claimant makes two incompatible statements about the same subject. 2) If the first claim is proven false by the opponent's evidence, the contradiction is removed and the second claim is heard. 3) The condition for tanāquḍ is that both statements were made before the same judge, or at least the second statement was made before the judge. 4) If the claimant explicitly abandons his first statement and settles on the second, it is accepted. 5) The author provides a detailed analysis of why in some cases (like the guarantor) the contradiction is considered resolved, while in others (like the seller claiming return) it is not, based on the intention to either confirm or challenge the established evidence. 6) The passage concludes with a clarification that the rule of accepting the abandonment of one claim is not a general rule but applies only to certain cases where the second claim is a reconciliation rather than a contradiction.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): Contradiction between two claims that are legally incompatible, such as first denying a sale then claiming a defect return.
المكذب (al-mukadhdhib): A claimant who is deemed a liar by the Sharīʿa due to his own contradictory statements.
التكذيب الشرعي (al-takdhīb al-sharʿī): Legal falsification: when the opponent's evidence proves the first claim false, removing the contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a claimant first denies a sale in court, then the opponent proves the sale, then the claimant claims a return due to defect, the evidence for the return is:
A) Not accepted because of contradiction.
B) Accepted because the opponent's evidence removed the contradiction (sharʿī takdhīb).
C) Accepted only if the claimant explicitly abandons the first statement.
D) Not accepted because the second claim acknowledges the sale.

Key Takeaway
Contradictory claims can be resolved by sharʿī takdhīb or abandonment, allowing evidence to be heard.

The Venue of Contradiction: Both Claims or Only the Second?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on when contradictory claims prevent evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on whether contradictory claims must both be before the same judge, and apply the relied-upon view that only the second claim need be before the judge.

Core Idea
Contradiction only matters when the second claim is before the judge; the first can be anywhere.

A key intra-Hanafi procedural dispute concerns the venue of contradictory claims: must both statements be made before the same judge for tanāquḍ to bar evidence, or is it enough that the later claim is in court? This lesson examines the two narrations and the settled position that only the second claim need be judicial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting views
View 1: Both claims must be before the same judge (some early texts). View 2: Only the second claim need be before the judge; the first may have been made elsewhere.
The first view is from al-Ajnās and al-Ṣughrā; the second is from al-Bazzāzī and al-Muḥīṭ.
2
Trace the authority of each view
The early view is cited as 'al-riwāyah al-ūlā'. The later view is attributed to al-Bazzāzī and supported by al-Muḥīṭ. The author of al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq declares the second view 'muʿtamad' (relied upon).
The passage explicitly says 'wa qaddamnā annahu al-muʿtamad'.
3
Understand the practical consequence
Under the first view, a claimant who made a contradictory statement before a non-judge (e.g., in mediation) cannot have his second claim heard in court if the first is later proven. Under the second view, only the in-court statement matters.
Example: Claimant first says 'I inherited this house' in a private letter, then in court says 'I bought it'. Under the second view, only the in-court claim is considered.
4
Apply the relied-upon view
A claimant who first claimed by purchase before a non-judge, then claims absolute ownership in court, is heard as long as the second claim is before the judge. The first claim is irrelevant for tanāquḍ.
Exception: If the purchase claim was from a known person, the absolute claim is rejected because it is inherently contradictory (known vs unknown).

And from what we have established, it is clear that the author's restriction of the book's issue to the claim of return due to defect after denying the original sale is to exclude the claim of rescission (iqālah). Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of contradictory claims (tanāquḍ) on the admissibility of evidence. The commentary expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqāʾiq. The author begins by explaining the author's (of Kanz) restriction of the issue to a specific scenario to exclude related cases. He then cites various scenarios from al-Bazzāziyyah, Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn, and others to illustrate when contradiction is considered and when it is resolved. Key points: 1) Contradiction occurs when a claimant makes two incompatible statements about the same subject. 2) If the first claim is proven false by the opponent's evidence, the contradiction is removed and the second claim is heard. 3) The condition for tanāquḍ is that both statements were made before the same judge, or at least the second statement was made before the judge. 4) If the claimant explicitly abandons his first statement and settles on the second, it is accepted. 5) The author provides a detailed analysis of why in some cases (like the guarantor) the contradiction is considered resolved, while in others (like the seller claiming return) it is not, based on the intention to either confirm or challenge the established evidence. 6) The passage concludes with a clarification that the rule of accepting the abandonment of one claim is not a general rule but applies only to certain cases where the second claim is a reconciliation rather than a contradiction.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): Contradiction between two claims that are legally incompatible, such as first denying a sale then claiming a defect return.
المكذب (al-mukadhdhib): A claimant who is deemed a liar by the Sharīʿa due to his own contradictory statements.
المعتمد (al-muʿtamad): The relied-upon position; the view that is operative for fatwa.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the relied-upon Hanafi view on the venue requirement for tanāquḍ?
A) Both contradictory claims must be made before the same judge.
B) Only the second claim must be before the judge; the first can be anywhere.
C) Only the first claim must be before the judge; the second can be anywhere.
D) Neither claim needs to be before the judge.

Key Takeaway
For tanāquḍ to bar evidence, only the second claim need be before the judge; the first claim's venue is irrelevant.

Qada — Judiciary

Judge and Amin's Non-Liability in Official Sales
Ruling with proof on non-liability of judge and amin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge and his amin are not liable for the price when property sold for creditors is reclaimed, and explain the proof based on the judge's status as deputy of the caliph.

Core Idea
A judge and his amin are immune from personal liability for official sales; the buyer must recover from the creditors.

This lesson examines the liability of a judge or his appointed amin when they sell property belonging to creditors. According to Hanafi law, if the price is lost and the sold item is reclaimed, the judge and amin are not personally liable. The buyer must seek recourse from the creditors. The rationale is that the judge acts as the deputy of the caliph, and this immunity extends to his amin. We will explore the proof, the intra-Hanafi disagreement on certain wordings, and the buyer's right to recover the price from the beneficiaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The scenario: judge or amin sells a slave for creditors, takes the price, the price is lost, the slave is reclaimed (e.g., due to a prior right). The ruling: the seller (judge/amin) does not guarantee the price to the buyer.
The ruling applies to both judge and his appointed amin.
2
Trace the proof: qiyas on the caliph
The judge is the deputy (khalifa) of the caliph or the state. Just as the caliph has no personal liability for official acts, the judge has none either. The amin, being the judge's appointee, shares this immunity.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) based on the principle that public officials are exempt from personal liability in their official capacity.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
There is a dispute among the mashayikh regarding the case when the judge simply says 'this slave' without specifying it is for the creditors. Does the immunity still apply? The text mentions al-Kharzadeh's view that no liability attaches, but the commentator does not give a definitive tarjih.
This shows the importance of explicit intention in official sales.
4
Understand the buyer's recourse
Since the seller (judge/amin) is not liable, the buyer must return to the creditors (ghurama) to recover the price, because the sale was for their benefit. The liability (uhda) falls on them.
This is analogous to the principal being liable when the agent cannot be held liable in cases of interdiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو باع القاضي أو أمينه عبداً للغرماء وأخذ المال فضاع واستحق العبد لم يضمن أي البائع الثمن للمشتري لأن القاضي قائم مقام الخليفة ولا ضمان عليه فلا ضمان على القاضي وأمين القاضي كالقاضي وهو من يقول له القاضي جعلتك أميناً . في بيع هذا العبد أما إذا قال هذا العبد ولم يزد عليه اختلف المشايخ بع أنه لا تلحقه عهدة ؛ ذكره شيخ الإسلام خواهرزاده كذا في شرح التلخيص للفارسي. وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله إلى أن العبد لو ضاع منه قبل التسليم إلى المشتري لم يضمنا كما ذكره الشارح، وإلى أن أمينه لو قال بعت وقبضت الثمن وقضيت الغريم صدق بلا يمين وعهدة إلحاقاً بالقاضي ؛ كذا في شرح التلخيص أيضاً. وفي البدائع من خيار العيب: إن العيب إذا كان ظاهراً يرد المبيع به ينظر القاضي أو أمينه ا هـ . وفي قضاء الملتقط : إذا وجب يمين على مخدرة وجه القاضي لها ثلاثة من العدول يستحلفها واحد وآخران يشهدان على يمينها أو نكولها ا هـ. فعلى هذا المستحلف ليس بأمينه وإلا قبل قوله في اليمين والنكول وحده. ثم اعلم أن القاضي وأمينه لا ترجع حقوق عقد باشراه لليتيم إليهما بخلاف الوكيل والأب والوصي فلو ضمن القاضي أو أمينه ثمن ما باعاه لليتيم بعد بلوغه صح بخلافهم وتمامه في قضاء العتابية . قوله : (ورجع المشتري على الغرماء لأن البيع وقع لهم فكانت العهدة عليهم عند تعذر جعلها على العاقد كما تجعل العهدة على الموكل عند تعذر جعلها على الوكيل في المحجور
His saying: (And if the judge or his amin sold a slave for the creditors and took the money, and it was lost, and the slave was reclaimed, then he does not guarantee) i.e. the seller does not guarantee the price to the buyer because the judge is the deputy of the caliph and there is no liability upon him; so there is no liability on the judge and the judge's amin is like the judge, and he is the one to whom the judge says 'I appointed you as amin' for the sale of this slave. But if he said 'this slave' and did not add more, the mashayikh differed that no liability attaches to him; mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam Kharzadeh as in Sharh al-Talkhis al-Farisi. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated that if the slave was lost from him before delivery to the buyer, they are not liable, as the commentator mentioned. And that if his amin said 'I sold and took the price and paid the creditor', he is believed without oath and liability, by annexation to the judge; as in Sharh al-Talkhis also. And in al-Bada'i' from the chapter of option of defect: if the defect is apparent, he returns the sold item to him; the judge or his amin looks into it. And in Qada' al-Multaqit: if an oath becomes obligatory upon a secluded woman, the judge sends to her three just men: one administers the oath and the other two witness her oath or refusal. Accordingly, the one who administers the oath is not his amin; otherwise, his word alone in the oath and refusal would be accepted. Then know that the judge and his amin do not have the right to claim back rights of a contract they entered into for an orphan, unlike the agent, father, and executor; so if the judge or his amin personally guaranteed the price of what they sold for the orphan after his maturity, it is valid, contrary to them. And its completion is in Qada' al-Atabiyya. His saying: (And the buyer returns to the creditors because the sale occurred for them, so the liability is upon them when it is not possible to place it upon the contractor, just as liability is placed upon the principal when it is not possible to place it upon the agent in the case of the interdicted.)
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability (daman) of a judge or his appointed amin (trustee) when they sell property belonging to creditors. The matn states that if the judge or his amin sells a slave for the creditors, takes the price, the price is lost, and the slave is subsequently reclaimed (due to a defect or right), the seller (judge/amin) does not guarantee the price to the buyer. The commentator explains the rationale: the judge acts as the deputy of the caliph (or the state) and is thus exempt from personal liability. This immunity extends to the amin appointed by the judge. However, if the judge merely says 'this slave' without specifying the sale for creditors, there is disagreement. The commentator also notes multiple subsidiary rulings: if the slave is lost before delivery, the judge/amin is not liable; the amin's statement about the sale and payment is believed without oath; the judge or amin handles defective returns; and the judge may send representatives to administer oaths to secluded women. Importantly, the judge and his amin do not have the right to reclaim contractual rights for an orphan (unlike a wakil, father, or wasi), but if they personally guarantee the price, it is valid. The final sentence clarifies that the buyer has recourse against the creditors because the sale was for their benefit, and the liability (uhda) falls on them when it cannot be placed on the direct contractor, analogous to the principal being liable when the agent cannot be held liable in cases of interdiction.

Key Terms
أمين القاضي (amīn al-qāḍī): A person appointed by the judge as a trustee to perform specific tasks, such as selling property for creditors.
عهدة (ʿuhda): Liability or responsibility for a sale, particularly if the sold item is reclaimed by a rightful owner or has a defect.
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee or liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a judge sells a slave for creditors, takes the price, the price is lost, and the slave is reclaimed, who must compensate the buyer?
A) The judge
B) The amin of the judge
C) The creditors
D) The buyer bears the loss

Key Takeaway
The judge and his amin are immune from personal liability in official sales; the buyer must recover the price from the creditors.

Subsidiary Rules: The Amin's Credibility, Defects, and Orphan Contracts
Collection of subsidiary rulings on the amin's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the subsidiary rulings that the amin's statement is accepted without oath, that the judge or amin handles defective returns, and that the judge and amin cannot reclaim orphan contract rights unless they personally guarantee the price.

Core Idea
The amin's word is sufficient evidence; the judge oversees returns; and the judge cannot reclaim orphan contract rights without personal guarantee.

This lesson covers additional rulings related to the judge's amin. The amin is trusted regarding his actions: his claim of sale and payment is accepted without oath. The judge or his amin handles the return of defective goods. However, a key limitation is that the judge and his amin, unlike agents and guardians, cannot recover rights from contracts they made for orphans unless they personally guarantee the transaction. These rules highlight the unique status of judicial officials.

Guided Path
1
Identify the amin's evidentiary privilege
The amin's statement that he sold the property, took the price, and paid the creditor is believed without the need for an oath, by analogy to the judge.
This is an exception to the normal rule that a claimant must provide evidence or take an oath.
2
Examine the rule for defective goods
If a buyer returns a sold item due to an apparent defect, the judge or his amin looks into it and handles the return.
This shows the judge's supervisory role even in post-sale disputes.
3
Understand the limitation on orphan contracts
Unlike a wakil, father, or wasi, the judge and amin cannot later reclaim a right from a contract they made for an orphan after the orphan reaches maturity. However, if they personally guarantee the price (i.e., make themselves sureties), the guarantee is valid.
This distinction protects the orphan from the official's potential conflicts of interest.
4
Compare with agents and guardians
The text explicitly contrasts the judge/amin with the wakil, father, and wasi, who have the right to reclaim. This highlights the judge's responsibility is to the court, not personal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Accepting a Judge's Report of His Own Judgment
Ruling on accepting a judge's oral report of his own hadd judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge's report of his own hadd judgment is accepted in the Hanafi school and explain the Hanafi proof from the Quran.

Core Idea
A just, knowledgeable judge's report of his hadd judgment is binding — obedience to him is obligatory.

When a judge states he has passed a hadd punishment like stoning or amputation, may the executioner act on that statement alone? This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explores the Hanafi position, its Quranic proof, and the conditions that must be met. It also addresses disagreements within the school and with other madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The author begins with the general ruling: a just, knowledgeable judge's report of his judgment is accepted. Note the two conditions: 'adl (justice) and 'ilm (knowledge).
Justice means uprightness in religious observance; knowledge means competence in Islamic law.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The ruling is based on Quran 4:59, which commands obedience to 'uli al-amr' (those in authority). The Hanafi usul principle 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub' makes this obligation binding.
The imperative 'أطيعوا' indicates obligation.
3
Analyze the conditions and exceptions
If the judge is just but ignorant, he must be questioned about the conditions of the judgment. If he is sinful (fasiq), his statement is not accepted unless the executor himself sees the evidence.
The possibility of error or treachery justifies non-acceptance.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf accepted the report unconditionally (with justice and knowledge). Muhammad initially agreed, then required an additional just witness, then returned to the original view.
Later scholars adopted Muhammad's intermediate view due to corruption of judges.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and practical effect
The confirmed view of the two shaykhs is the mufta bihi: a just, knowledgeable judge's report is accepted without extra witnesses. However, in corrupt times, some prefer requiring additional verification.
The practical consequence: executioner can act on the judge's word alone under ideal conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال قاض عدل عالم قضيت على هذا بالرجم أو بالقطع أو بالضرب فافعله وسعك فعله لأن طاعة أولي الأمر واجبة بالآية الشريفة وتصديقه طاعة له. قيد بعدالته وعلمه لتنتفي عنه التهمة فإن كان عدلاً جاهلاً يستفسر؛ فإن أحسن الشرائط وجب تصديقه وإلا لا. وكذا إن كان فاسقاً إلا أن يعاين الحجة لاحتمال الخطأ أو الخيانة ولا يمين على القاضي، وما ذكره المصنف قول الماتريدي. وفي الجامع الصغير: لم يقيده بهما ثم رجع محمد فقال: لا يأخذ بقوله إلا أن يعاين الحجة أو يشهد بذلك القاضي مع عدل وبه أخذ مشايخنا لفساد أكثر قضاة زماننا والتدارك غير ممكن؛ كذا في الشرح وفي العناية: لا سيما قضاة زماننا لأن أكثرهم يتولون بالرشا فأحكامهم باطلة ومعناه أن يشهد القاضي والعدل على شهادة الذين شهدوا بسبب الحد لا حكم القاضي وإلا كان القاضي شاهداً على فعل نفسه؛ كذا في فتح القدير واستثنى في الهداية من هذا الكلي كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي لضرورة إحياء الحقوق ولأن الخيانة في مثله قلما تقع اهـ. فظاهر الاقتصار على كتاب القاضي أن القاضي لا يقبل قوله فيما عداه سواء كان قتلاً أو قطعاً أو ضرباً كما في الكتاب أو غيرها، فلو قال قضيت بطلاقها أو بعتقه أو ببيع أو نكاح أو إقرار لم يقبل قوله والحاصل أن الإمام رجع عن القول بقبول قوله إلا أن يعاين الحجة لم يجره المشايخ على إطلاقه فمنهم من زاد أو يشهد بذلك مع القاضي عدل وهو رواية عنه، وقد استبعده في فتح القدير بكونه بعيداً في العادة وهو شهادة القاضي عند الجلاد ومنهم من استثنى كتاب القاضي كما قد علمت والاكتفاء بالواحد على هذه الرواية في حق يثبت بشاهدين وإن كان في زنا فلا بد ثلاثة أخر؛ كذا ذكره الأسبيجابي. وأما الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي فقيده بغير العالم العدل أما كان متصفاً بهما فيقبل قوله لأن عدم الاعتماد إنما علل بالفساد والغلط وهو منتف في العالم العدل. وذكر الأسبيجابي أن المسألة مصورة عند أبي حنيفة في القاضي العالم العدل لأنه إذا كان غير هذا لا يولى القضاء ولا يؤتمر بأمره بالاتفاق ا.هـ. فما قاله أبو منصور عن مذهب الإمام فلهذا اختاره في الكتاب وفي التهذيب ويصدق القاضي فيما قال من التصرف في الأوقاف وأموال اليتامى والغائبين من أداء وقبض، وإذا رفع إلى القاضي أنك حكمت على فلان بكذا وهو غائب لم يصدق فيه ا هـ . وفي جامع الفصولين من الفصل العاشر: وروى ابن سماعة أن القاضي لا يقضي بعلمه أقول: ينبغي أن يفتى به في غير كتاب القاضي لمعنى ظاهر في أكثر قضاة الزمان أصلح الله شأنهم. ورأيت في عيون المذاهب أنه لو قال قاض عدل عالم حكمت على هذا بالرجم أو بالقطع فافعله وسعك أن تفعله إلا عند مالك والشافعي في قول ومحمد في رواية وبه يفتى ا هـ . فقد ثبت أن الفتوى على ما رجع إليه محمد لكن رأيت بعد ذلك في شرح أدب القضاء للصدر الشهيد أنه صح رجوع محمد إلى قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رواه هشام عنه ا.هـ. والحاصل المفهوم من شرح الصدر الشهيد أن الشيخين قالا بقبول إخباره عن إقراره بشيء لا يصح رجوعه عنه مطلقاً، وأن محمداً أولاً وافقهما ثم رجع عنه وقال لا يقبل إلا بضم رجل آخر عدل إليه وهو المراد بقول ثم صح رجوعه إلى قولهما، وأما إذا أخبر القاضي بإقراره عن شيء يصح رجوعه عنه كالحد لم يقبل قوله بالإجماع، وإن أخبر عن ثبوت الحق بالبينة فقال قامت بذلك بينة وعدلوا وقبلت شهادتهم على ذلك تقبل في الوجهين جميعاً ا اعلم أن القاضي إذا قضى بشيء ينبغي له أن يشهد على قضائه، سواء كان ببينة أو بإقرار مطلقاً إلى آخر ما ذكره الصدر الشهيد ولا بد من إشهاده عليه في محل ولايته فلو أشهد على قضائه بعد ما خرج من المصر لم يسع الشاهدين الشهادة وإن بينا لم يقبلا كما ذكره الحصيري في شرح الجامع الكبير . من روي عنه أنه لا يقبل مطلقاً قوله: (إن الشيخين قالا بقبول إخباره عن إقراره أي إخبار القاضي عن إقرار الخصم بما لا
And if a just, knowledgeable judge says, 'I have judged this person to be stoned, or amputated, or flogged,' then do it, and it is permissible for you to do it, because obedience to those in authority is obligatory by the noble verse, and believing him is obedience to him. He restricted it to his justice and knowledge to remove suspicion. If he is just but ignorant, he is questioned; if he fulfills the conditions, it is obligatory to believe him, otherwise not. Likewise if he is sinful, unless he witnesses the evidence, due to the possibility of error or treachery. And there is no oath on the judge. What the author mentioned is the statement of al-Maturidi. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, he did not restrict it to both, then Muhammad retracted and said: 'One does not accept his statement unless he witnesses the evidence, or the judge along with a just person testifies to that.' Our scholars adopted this due to the corruption of most judges of our time, and redress is not possible. This is in al-Sharh and al-Inayah: 'Especially the judges of our time, because most of them obtain their position through bribery, so their judgments are invalid.' Its meaning is that the judge and the just person testify to the testimony of those who testified concerning the cause of the hadd, not the judgment of the judge, otherwise the judge would be a witness regarding his own action. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Hidayah, an exception was made from this general rule for the judge's letter to another judge, due to the necessity of reviving rights, and because treachery in such matters rarely occurs. So the apparent restriction to the judge's letter indicates that the judge's statement is not accepted in other matters, whether it be killing, amputation, or beating as in the book, or other than these. Thus if he said, 'I judged her divorce, or his manumission, or a sale, or a marriage, or an acknowledgment,' his statement is not accepted. In conclusion, the Imam retracted from the view of accepting his statement unless he witnesses the evidence. The scholars did not apply it absolutely; some added, 'or that a just person testifies with the judge,' and this is a narration from him. In Fath al-Qadir it was deemed improbable as being unusual, and it is the judge's testimony before the executioner. And some excepted the judge's letter as already known. Reliance on a single witness according to this narration is in a matter that requires two witnesses, and if it is fornication, three others are necessary. This is what al-Asbijabi mentioned. As for Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, he restricted it to the just, knowledgeable [judge]; if he is described as such, his statement is accepted because the reason for lack of reliance was attributed to corruption and error, and that is absent in the just, knowledgeable person. Al-Asbijabi mentioned that the issue is illustrated according to Abu Hanifah regarding the just, knowledgeable judge, because if he is other than this, he should not be appointed as judge nor obeyed by agreement. So what Abu Mansur said about the school of the Imam, for this reason it was chosen in the book and in al-Tahdhib. And the judge is believed regarding what he says concerning the management of endowments and the property of orphans and absentees, both payment and receipt. And if it is brought to the judge that you judged on so-and-so such-and-such while he was absent, he is not believed in it. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, from the tenth chapter: Ibn Sami'ah narrated that the judge does not judge by his knowledge. I say: It is appropriate to issue a fatwa according to this in other than the judge's letter, due to an apparent reason concerning most judges of the time, may Allah improve their condition. And I saw in 'Uyun al-Madhahib that if a just, knowledgeable judge said, 'I judged this person to be stoned or amputated, so do it,' it is permissible for you to do it, except according to Malik and al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and Muhammad in one narration, and according to that the fatwa is given. So it is established that the fatwa is according to what Muhammad retracted to, but I saw after that in Sharh Adab al-Qada' by al-Sadr al-Shahid that Muhammad's return to the statement of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf is authenticated, narrated by Hisham from him. The conclusion understood from the explanation of al-Sadr al-Shahid is that the two shaykhs said: His report of an acknowledgment from a person concerning something from which he cannot retract is accepted absolutely. Muhammad first agreed with them, then retracted and said: 'It is not accepted unless another just man is joined to him.' That is what is meant by 'then his return to their statement was authenticated.' As for if the judge reports his acknowledgment of something from which he can retract, like hadd, his statement is not accepted by consensus. And if he reports the establishment of a right by evidence, saying, 'Evidence for that was established and they were just, and their testimony was accepted regarding that,' it is accepted in both aspects. Know that if a judge passes a judgment, he should have witnesses to his judgment, whether by evidence or acknowledgment absolutely, up to the end of what al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned. And it is necessary to have witnesses on it in the place of his jurisdiction. If he has witnesses on his judgment after leaving the city, it is not permissible for the two witnesses to testify, and if they make it clear, they are not accepted, as mentioned by al-Husayri in Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir. From those who narrated from him that it is not accepted absolutely. His statement: (Indeed the two shaykhs said accepting his report of his acknowledgment, i.e., the judge's report of the litigant's acknowledgment of what is not...) [text incomplete]
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on whether a judge's report of his own prior judgment is sufficient grounds for execution of a hadd punishment or other legal consequences. The author, al-Marghinani or his commentator, explains that if a judge who is both just ('adl) and knowledgeable ('alim) says, 'I have passed a judgment of stoning, amputation, or flogging,' the executioner may carry it out. This is because obedience to those in authority (ulu al-amr) is obligatory based on the Quranic verse (4:59), and believing the judge is part of that obedience. The qualification of justice and knowledge is crucial to eliminate suspicion of bias or error. If the judge is just but ignorant, he must be questioned about the conditions of the judgment; if they are correct, his word is accepted, otherwise not. If the judge is sinful (fasiq), his statement is not accepted unless the executor directly witnesses the evidence (e.g., the confession or testimony) for himself, due to the possibility of the judge being mistaken or dishonest. No oath is required from the judge. The author notes that this view is that of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. However, in al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad initially did not restrict to justice and knowledge, then retracted and said that the judge's statement is only accepted if the executor sees the evidence or if another just person testifies alongside the judge. Our later scholars (mashayikh) adopted this due to the corruption of most judges in their time. The text then discusses exceptions: the judge's letter to another judge is an exception because of necessity for reviving rights, and treachery rarely occurs in such correspondence. So by implication, the judge's oral report is not accepted for other matters like divorce, manumission, sale, etc. The passage then details the positions of different authorities: some require a just witness with the judge, some except the judge's letter, and some follow al-Maturidi's view that a just and knowledgeable judge's word is accepted. Finally, it mentions that the fatwa was initially on Muhammad's retracted view (requiring another witness), but later it was authenticated that Muhammad returned to the view of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf that the judge's report is accepted without additional witnesses if he is just and knowledgeable. The passage concludes with a note that the judge should have witnesses on his judgment, and those witnesses must be appointed in the place of his jurisdiction; if he appoints them after leaving the city, their testimony is not accepted.

Key Terms
قاض عدل عالم (qadi 'adl 'alim): A judge who is both just (upright in religious observance) and knowledgeable (qualified in Islamic law).
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another regarding a judgment or legal matter.
أولوا الأمر (ulu al-amr): Those in authority, including rulers, judges, and qualified leaders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (after Muhammad's final return), when is a judge's report of a hadd judgment accepted?
A) Only if the judge is just, knowledgeable, and another just witness testifies.
B) If the judge is just and knowledgeable, without additional witnesses.
C) Only if the judge is just, regardless of knowledge.
D) Only if the judgment was passed in writing.

Key Takeaway
A just and knowledgeable judge's report of his own hadd judgment is binding due to the Quranic obligation to obey those in authority, though later scholars added caution due to judicial corruption.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The Judge's Statement About His Past Judgment
Analysis of acceptance of a judge's statement about his judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge's statement about his past judgment is accepted according to the Hanafi school and explain the rational basis (istihsan) for this ruling.

Core Idea
A just and knowledgeable judge's statement about his past judgment is accepted because his office implies regularity.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine a key procedural ruling: when can a judge's own report about a judgment he previously made be taken as conclusive? The Hanafi school, guided by principle and practicality, holds that a qualified judge's statement suffices, unless he reports something from which he could retract. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the cross-madhhab dispute, and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
What is the ruling being discussed? The Hanafi school accepts a judge's statement about his past judgment under certain conditions.
The ruling is about acceptance, not about the original judgment.
2
State the conditions
The two main conditions: the judge must be just and knowledgeable, and he must ascribe the action to his period of office.
If the judge is not just and knowledgeable, his statement is not accepted by consensus.
3
Analyze the dalil
The proof is istihsan: corruption and error are improbable in a just and knowledgeable judge, and his ascription to office negates liability.
This is a rational proof, not a textual one.
4
Map the dispute
Malik, al-Shafi'i (in one narration), and Muhammad (initially) hold that the judge's statement is not accepted without additional evidence.
Muhammad later retracted and agreed with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
5
Apply the ruling
The judge's statement is accepted for acts within his office (e.g., disposal of waqf, payment to creditors). If he reports a retractable matter like hadd, it is not accepted by consensus.
The key distinction is whether the matter can be retracted.

قوله إلا أن يعاين (الحجة الصواب إبدال (عن)) بـ (إلى) كما لا يخفى بأدنى تأمل . رجع قوله : (إن الشيخين قالا بقبول إخباره عن إقراره أي إخبار القاضي عن إقرار الخصم بما لا عزل لرجل أخذت منك ألفاً ودفعته إلى زيد قضيت به عليك فقال الرجل أخذته ظلماً فالقول للقاضي وكذا لو قال قضيت بقطع يدك في حق إذا كان المقطوع يده والمأخوذ منه ماله مقراً أنه فعله وهو قاض. في قوله : (وإن قال قاض عزل لرجل أخذت منك ألفاً ودفعته إلى زيد قضيت به عليك فقال الرجل أخذته ظلماً فالقول للقاضي وكذا لو قال قضيت بقطع يدك في حق إذا كان المقطوع يده والمأخوذ منه ماله مقراً أنه فعله وهو قاض) لأنهما لما توافقا أنه فعل ذلك ، في قضائه كان الظاهر شاهداً له إذ القاضي لا يقضي بالجور ظاهراً ولا يمين عليه لأنه ثبت فعله في قضائه بالتصادق ولا يمين على القاضي. وأشار المؤلف إلى عدم الضمان على القاطع والآخذ لو أقر بما أقر به القاضي. وقيد بإقراره أنه فعله وهو قاض لأن المقطوع يده والمأخوذ ماله لو زعماً أنه فعل قبل التقليد أو بعد العزل ففيه اختلاف، والأصح أن القول للقاضي لأنه أسند فعله إلى حالة معهودة منافية للضمان فصار كما إذا قال طلقت أو أعتقت وأنا مجنون وجنونه معهود ولو أقر القاطع والآخذ في هذا الفصل بما أقر به القاضي يضمنان لأنهما أقرا بسبب الضمان وقول القاضي مقبول في دفع نفسه لا الضمان عن في إبطال سبب الضمان عن غيره بخلاف الأول لأنه ثبت فعله في قضائه بالتصادق. ولو كان المال في يد أنه فعله الآخذ قائماً وقد أقر بما أقر به القاضي والمأخوذ منه المال صدق القاضي في قضائه أو لا يؤخذ منه لأنه أقر أن اليد كان له فلا يصدق في دعوى التملك إلا بحجة وقول المعزول ليس بحجة فيه. ثم اعلم أن الأصل أن المقر إذا أسند إقراره إلى حالة منافية للضمان من كل وجه فإنه لا يلزمه شيء منها ما ذكرناه ومنها لو قال العبد لغيره بعد العتق قطعت يدك وأنا عبد فقال المقر له بل قطعتها وأنت حر فالقول للعبد ومنها ما لو قال المولى لعبد قد أعتقه أخذت منك غلة كل شهر خمسة دراهم وأنت عبد فقال المعتق أخذتها بعد العتق كان القول للمولى. ومنها الوكيل بالبيع إذا قال بعت وسلمت قبل العزل وقال الموكل بعد العزل فالقول للوكيل إن كان المبيع مستهلكاً، وإن كان قائماً فالقول للموكل لأنه أخبر عما لا يملك الإنشاء، وكذا في مسألة الغلة لا يصدق في الغلة القائمة لأنه أقر بالأخذ وبالإضافة يدعي عليه التمليك. ومنها لو قال الوصـ بعد ما بلغ اليتيم أنفقت عليك كذا وكذا من وأنكر اليتيم كان القول للوصي لكونه أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان، وأورد في النهاية على هذا الأصل ما إذا أعتق أمته ثم قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي فقالت هي قطعتها وأنا حرة فالقول لها، وكذا في كل شيء أخذه منها عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف مع أنه منكر للضمان صي المال يصح رجوع المقر عنه كالقصاص وحد القذف والأموال والطلاق وسائر الحقوق قوله : (وذكر الشارح أن هذا الفرق غير مخلص) قال في الحواشي السعدية لعدم جريانه في صورة النزاع في أخذ غلة العبد وقطع يد الأمة كما لا يخفى.
Book of Hawala, Chapter of Arbitration: From Kashf: Ziyada: Or he witnesses that with the judge, and it is a narration from him. It was deemed improbable in Fath al-Qadir as being far in custom, and it is the judge’s testimony before the executioner. Some excepted the judge’s writing as you know. Reliance on a single [witness] according to this narration in a matter established by two witnesses – if it is about fornication then three others are required; so al-Asbijabi mentioned. As for Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, he restricted it to other than the just and knowledgeable [judge]; but if he is just and knowledgeable, his statement is accepted because lack of reliance is attributed to corruption and error, which are absent in the just and knowledgeable judge. Al-Asbijabi mentioned that the issue is framed according to Abu Hanifa regarding a just and knowledgeable judge, because if he is otherwise, he is not appointed to judgeship nor is his command followed by consensus. So what Abu Mansur said about the Imam’s school is why it was chosen in the book and in al-Tahdhib. The judge is believed regarding his disposal of endowments and the wealth of orphans and absentees, both payment and receipt. If it is reported to the judge that you judged against so-and-so while he was absent, he is not believed in that. In Jami‘ al-Fusulayn from the tenth chapter: Ibn Sama‘ah narrated that the judge does not judge by his knowledge. I say: Fatwa should be given according to this concerning other than the judge’s writing (kitab al-qadi) due to an obvious reason concerning most judges of the time. I saw in ‘Uyun al-Madhahib that if a just, knowledgeable judge said, ‘I sentenced this man to stoning or amputation, so do it’, it is permissible for you to do it, except according to Malik and al-Shafi‘i in one opinion and Muhammad in a narration, and fatwa is given according to that. So it is established that fatwa is according to what Muhammad finally held, but later I saw in Sharh Adab al-Qada’ by al-Sadr al-Shahid that it is confirmed that Muhammad returned to the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, narrated by Hisham from him. The conclusion from the commentary of al-Sadr al-Shahid is that the two shaykhs said [the judge’s] report of his acknowledgment of a matter from which he cannot retract is accepted absolutely, and Muhammad initially agreed with them then retracted and said it is not accepted unless another just man is added to him – this is the meaning of ‘then his return to their view is confirmed’. But if the judge reports his acknowledgment of something from which retraction is possible, like prescribed punishment, his statement is not accepted by consensus. If he reports establishment of a right by evidence, saying ‘evidence was presented and they were deemed reliable and I accepted their testimony on that’, it is accepted in both aspects. Know that if a judge passes a judgment, he should have witnesses to his judgment, whether based on evidence or acknowledgment, absolutely, up to what al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned. He must have them witness it in the place of his jurisdiction. If he has them witness his judgment after leaving the city, it is not permitted for the two witnesses to testify, and if they do, it is not accepted, as mentioned by al-Husayri in Sharh al-Jami‘ al-Kabir. From the narration that it is not accepted at all. Then the statement: ‘If he was a wrongdoer’ is a conjunction to the implied negation after ‘except’, meaning: if he does not possess the conditions or is a wrongdoer, he is not to be believed unless he sees the evidence. His statement: ‘He did not restrict it to both’ i.e., justice and knowledge. His statement: ‘for the acceptance of his statement unless he sees the evidence’ – the correct is to change ‘an’ to ‘ila’. He retracted. His statement: ‘Indeed the two shaykhs said acceptance of his report of his acknowledgment’ i.e., the judge’s report of the litigant’s acknowledgment of what cannot be retracted. A removed judge says to a man: ‘I took a thousand from you and paid it to Zayd, and I passed judgment against you based on it.’ The man says: ‘You took it unjustly.’ The statement is the judge’s. Likewise if he said: ‘I passed judgment cutting your hand’ if the one whose hand was cut and the one from whom money was taken acknowledge that he did that while he was a judge. Because when they both agree that he did that in his judgment, the apparent circumstance testifies for him, since a judge does not pronounce unjust judgments apparently, and there is no oath upon him because his action in his judgment is established by mutual acknowledgement, and there is no oath on a judge. The author indicated no liability on the cutter and the taker if they acknowledge what the judge acknowledged. He restricted it to his acknowledgment that he did it while he was a judge, because if the one whose hand was cut and the one from whom money was taken claimed that he did it before appointment or after removal, there is difference. The soundest is that the statement is the judge’s because he ascribes his action to a familiar state that negates liability, so it becomes as if he said ‘I divorced or emancipated while insane’ and his insanity is familiar. If the cutter and taker in this section acknowledge what the judge acknowledged, they are liable because they acknowledged a cause of liability, and the judge’s statement is accepted to defend himself, not to void the cause of liability for others, contrary to the first because his action in his judgment is established by mutual acknowledgement. If the property is in the hand that he did that, the taker has it and acknowledged what the judge acknowledged, and the one from whom the money was taken – is the judge believed in his judgment or is it not taken from him? Because he acknowledged that the hand had it, so he is not believed in a claim of ownership without evidence, and the statement of the removed judge is not evidence. Then know that the principle is: if the person making an acknowledgment ascribes it to a state that negates liability completely, nothing is binding on him from it. From this are: if a slave says to another after emancipation, ‘I cut your hand while I was a slave,’ and the recipient says, ‘Rather you cut it while free,’ the statement is the slave’s. Also if a master says to a slave he emancipated, ‘I took from you monthly rent of five dirhams while you were a slave,’ and the emancipated one says, ‘You took it after emancipation,’ the statement is the master’s. Also an agent for sale if he says, ‘I sold and delivered before removal,’ and the principal says ‘after removal,’ the statement is the agent’s if the sold item is consumed; if it exists, the statement is the principal’s because he informs of what he cannot create anew. Similarly, regarding rent, he is not believed in the existing rent because he acknowledged taking and claims ownership by addition. Also if a guardian, after the orphan reaches majority, says, ‘I spent on you such and such,’ and the orphan denies, the statement is the guardian’s because he ascribes it to a state negating liability. A counterexample was given in al-Nihayah: if he emancipates his slave-girl then says, ‘I cut your hand while you were my slave,’ and she says, ‘You cut it while I was free,’ the statement is hers. Similarly for everything he took from her, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, even though he denies liability. The property from which the person making acknowledgment can retract, like qisas, slander punishment, properties, divorce, and all rights. His statement: ‘The commentator said this distinction is not resolving’ – he said in al-Hawashi al-Sa‘diyyah because it does not apply in the instance of dispute regarding taking the slave’s rent and cutting the hand of the slave-girl, as is obvious.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rulings concerning the acceptance of a judge's statement about his own past judgment. The commentator opens by discussing the condition that the judge must be just and knowledgeable for his statement to be accepted, citing Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and al-Asbijabi who frame the issue according to Abu Hanifa's view that a judge lacking these qualities is not appointed. The passage then clarifies that a judge's statement regarding disposal of waqf and orphan funds is accepted. A significant point is that if a removed judge says 'I took a thousand from you and paid it to Zaid as a judgment,' the man's claim of injustice is not heard; the judge's statement is taken because the apparent circumstance—that judges do not pass unjust judgments—supports him. Similarly, if the judge says he ordered amputation, the statement holds if the parties acknowledge he did so while in office. The author then explains the principle: if a person ascribes an action to a state that negates liability (like slavery, insanity, official capacity), his statement is accepted unless the opposing party proves otherwise. Numerous applications follow: a freed slave's claim of cutting a hand while enslaved, a master's claim of taking rent while the slave was enslaved, an agent's claim of sale before removal, and a guardian's claim of expenses. The passage concludes with a note on a counterexample in al-Nihayah regarding a master and his slave-girl, which generates discussion on the distinction between liability and acknowledgment.

Key Terms
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge appointed by the state to adjudicate disputes; his statement about his own judgment is under discussion.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment of a past action; the judge's report of his own acknowledgment or the acknowledgment of a litigant.
العدالة (al-adalah): Justice; the quality of being upright and trustworthy, a prerequisite for acceptance of the judge's statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a removed judge's statement about having ordered amputation of a hand accepted?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances.
B) Only if the judge is just and knowledgeable and the parties acknowledge he did it while he was a judge.
C) Only if there are two witnesses present.
D) Never, because amputation is a hadd punishment.

Key Takeaway
A just and knowledgeable judge's statement about an action performed during his tenure is accepted based on istihsan, unless the matter is one from which retraction is possible (e.g., hadd).

The Removed Judge's Word and the Principle of Attributing to a State Negating Liability
Explains a removed judge's claim, the principle of attribution, and an intra-Hanafi dispute on its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for accepting a removed judge's claim, apply the principle of attribution to a state negating liability, and distinguish between Abu Hanifah's and Muhammad's positions on the 'from every aspect' requirement.

Core Idea
A judge's word is accepted when both agree the act was judicial, but the school splits on whether the capacity must negate liability from every aspect.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a practical question: when a removed judge claims that his past action was part of a court ruling, should his word be taken over that of the litigant? The Hanafi school builds its answer on a broader principle: attributing an act to a state that negates liability. However, a dispute between the founding imams over the stringency of this condition has significant consequences for how cases are decided. Let's work through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim and ruling
The judge says 'I took the money as a judgment.' The other party says it was unjust. The ruling: the judge's word is accepted. Note the key condition: the other party acknowledges that the act was done while the judge was in office.
This is not an automatic acceptance; the acknowledgment establishes the judicial context.
2
Locate the underlying principle
The judge's word is accepted because he attributed his act to a 'familiar state that negates liability' – being a judge. This is analogous to a crazy person who divorces or emancipates: if his insanity is known, his word is accepted because the state negates the normal consequences.
The principle is called 'isnad al-iqrar ila halah munafiyah li-l-daman'.
3
Recognize the disputed condition: 'from every aspect'
Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf require that the state negate liability 'from every aspect' (min kulli wajh). Muhammad holds that it is enough if the state generally negates liability (fi al-jumlah), even if it may sometimes coincide with liability. This affects the outcome in cases where the state is not universally exempting.
The slave-girl example illustrates the dispute: ownership normally negates liability, but because a slave-girl can be pawned (creating liability), her state does not negate liability from every aspect.
4
Apply to the slave-girl case
A master says he cut his slave-girl's hand while she was his slave. She says it was after freedom. According to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, her word is taken, despite the master's attribution to a state (ownership) that usually negates liability. The reasoning: ownership does not negate liability from every aspect (e.g., if she was pawned, the master could be liable). The commentator notes this exception seems inconsistent with the principle.
This case exposes the tension within the school and leads to refinement of the rule.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
In practice, Muhammad's view makes it harder for a confessor to escape liability – any possible liability in the state prevents him from being cleared. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) appears to favor the stricter 'from every aspect' condition, which aligns with Abu Hanifah, but the mufta bihi is not explicitly resolved.
This disagreement reminds us that even core principles may be applied differently depending on the jurist's weighing of potential harm.

لما توافقا أنه فعل ذلك ، في قضائه كان الظاهر شاهداً له إذ القاضي لا يقضي بالجور ظاهراً ولا يمين عليه لأنه ثبت فعله في قضائه بالتصادق ولا يمين على القاضي. وأشار المؤلف إلى عدم الضمان على القاطع والآخذ
