منافية للضمان فصار كما إذا قال طلقت أو أعتقت وأنا مجنون وجنونه معهود ولو أقر القاطع والآخذ في هذا الفصل بما أقر به القاضي يضمنان لأنهما أقرا بسبب الضمان وقول القاضي مقبول في دفع نفسه لا الضمان عن في إبطال سبب الضمان عن غيره بخلاف الأول لأنه ثبت فعله في قضائه بالتصادق. ولو كان المال في يد أنه فعله الآخذ قائماً وقد أقر بما أقر به القاضي والمأخوذ منه المال صدق القاضي في قضائه أو لا يؤخذ منه لأنه أقر أن اليد كان له فلا يصدق في دعوى التملك إلا بحجة وقول المعزول ليس بحجة فيه. ثم اعلم أن الأصل أن المقر إذا أسند إقراره إلى حالة منافية للضمان من كل وجه فإنه لا يلزمه شيء منها ما ذكرناه ومنها لو قال العبد لغيره بعد العتق قطعت يدك وأنا عبد فقال المقر له بل قطعتها وأنت حر فالقول للعبد ومنها ما لو قال المولى لعبد قد أعتقه أخذت منك غلة كل شهر خمسة دراهم وأنت عبد فقال المعتق أخذتها بعد العتق كان القول للمولى. ومنها الوكيل بالبيع إذا قال بعت وسلمت قبل العزل وقال الموكل بعد العزل فالقول للوكيل إن كان المبيع مستهلكاً، وإن كان قائماً فالقول للموكل لأنه أخبر عما لا يملك الإنشاء، وكذا في مسألة الغلة لا يصدق في الغلة القائمة لأنه أقر بالأخذ وبالإضافة يدعي عليه التمليك. ومنها لو قال الوصـ بعد ما بلغ اليتيم أنفقت عليك كذا وكذا من وأنكر اليتيم كان القول للوصي لكونه أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان، وأورد في النهاية على هذا الأصل ما إذا أعتق أمته ثم قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي فقالت هي قطعتها وأنا حرة فالقول لها، وكذا في كل شيء أخذه منها عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف مع أنه منكر للضمان بإسناد الفعل إلى حالة منافية للضمان فأجاب بالفرق من حيث إن المولى أقر بأخذ مالها ثم ادعى التمليك لنفسه فيصدق في إقراره ولا يصدق في دعواه التمليك له، وكذا لو قال لرجل أكلت طعامك بإذنك فأنكر الإذن يضمن المقر. وذكر الشارح أن هذا الفرق غير مخلص كما قال وقد خرج هذا الفرع ونحوه بما زدناه على القاعدة من قولنا من كل وجه لأن كونها أمة له لا ينفي الضمان عنه من كل وجه لأنه يضمن فيما لو كانت مرهونة أو مأذونة مديونة فلم يرد وأصل المسألة في المجمع من الإقرار قال ولو أقر حربي أسلم بأخذ مال قبل الإسلام أو بإتلاف خمر بعده أو مسلم بمال حربي في دار الحرب أو بقطع يد معتقة قبل العتق فكذبوه في الإسلام أفتى بعدم الضمان في الكل. قال المصنف في شرحه وقالا : يضمن لأنه أسنده إلى حالة قد يجامعها الضمان في الجملة فلا يبرأ بهذا الإسناد. وفي البزازية : دهنا صب لإنسان عند الشهود فادعی مالکه ضمانه فقال كانت نجسة لوقوع فأرة فالقول للصاب لإنكاره الضمان والشهود يشهدون على الصب لا على عدم النجاسة، وكذا لو أتلف لحم طواف فطولب بالضمان فقال كانت ميتة فأتلفتها لا يصدق، وللشهود أن يشهدوا أنه لحم ذكي بحكم الحال وقال القاضي لا يضمن فاعترض عليه بمسألة كتاب الاستحسان أن رجلاً لو قتل رجلاً قال كان ارتد أو قتل أبي فقتلته قصاصاً أو للردة لا يسمع فأجاب وقال : لأنه لو قبل لأدى إلى فتح باب العدوان فإنه يقتل ويقول كان القتل لذلك وأمر الدم عظيم فلا يهمل بخلاف المال فإنه بالنسبة إلى الدم أهون حتى حكم في المال بالنكول وفي الدم حبس حتى يقرأ ويحلف واكتفى باليمين الواحدة في المال وبخمسين يميناً في الدم والله تعالى أعلم.
A removed [judge] said to a man, 'I took 1000 from you and gave it to Zaid and ruled it against you.' The man said, 'He took it unjustly.' Then the word is of the judge. Likewise if he said, 'I ruled to cut off your hand in a right [claim]' when the one whose hand was cut and the one whose property was taken acknowledge that he did that while he was a judge. In his statement: 'And if a removed judge said to a man, I took 1000 from you and gave it to Zaid and ruled it against you, and the man said he took it unjustly, then the word is of the judge. Likewise if he said, I ruled to cut your hand in a right [claim], when the one whose hand was cut and the one whose property was taken acknowledge that he did that while he was a judge.' Because when both agree that he did that in his judging, the apparent circumstance witnesses for him, since the judge apparently does not judge unjustly. And no oath is upon him because his act in his judging is established by mutual confirmation, and no oath is upon the judge. The author indicated that there is no liability on the cutter and the taker if they acknowledge what the judge acknowledged. And he restricted it by their acknowledgment that he did it while being a judge, because if the one whose hand was cut and the one whose property was taken claim that he did it before appointment or after removal, then there is disagreement, and the correct is that the word is of the judge because he attributed his act to a familiar state that negates liability, so it becomes like if he said, 'I divorced or emancipated while I was insane' and his insanity is familiar. And if the cutter and the taker in this chapter acknowledge what the judge acknowledged, they are liable because they admitted a cause of liability. And the word of the judge is accepted for repelling [liability] from himself, not for invalidating a cause of liability for others, unlike the first because his act in his judging is established by mutual confirmation. And if the property is in the hand of the taker, existing, and he has acknowledged what the judge acknowledged, and the one from whom the property was taken, is the judge truthful in his ruling or not? It is not taken from him because he acknowledged that the hand [i.e., possession] was his, so he is not believed in a claim of ownership except with proof, and the word of the removed is not proof in it. Then know that the principle is that when the confessor attributes his confession to a state that negates liability from every aspect, then nothing is incumbent upon him from it, as we mentioned. Among them: if the slave said to another after emancipation, 'I cut your hand while I was a slave,' and the confessee said, 'Rather you cut it while you were free,' then the word is of the slave. Among them: if the master said to his slave whom he had emancipated, 'I took from you a usufruct of every month five dirhams while you were a slave,' and the freedman said, 'You took it after emancipation,' then the word is of the master. Among them: the agent for sale, if he said, 'I sold and delivered before removal,' and the principal said, 'after removal,' then the word is of the agent if the sold item is consumed; if it is existing, then the word is of the principal because he reported about something he does not own to initiate. Likewise in the matter of usufruct, he is not believed regarding the existing usufruct because he acknowledged taking and by addition he claims ownership against him. Among them: if the executor said after the orphan attained majority, 'I spent on you such and such,' and the orphan denied, then the word is of the executor because he attributed it to a state that negates liability. And in an-Nihayah, an objection was raised against this principle: if a man emancipated his female slave, then said to her, 'I cut your hand while you were my female slave,' and she said, 'You cut it while I was free,' then the word is hers. Likewise regarding everything he took from her according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, even though he is denying liability by attributing the act to a state that negates liability. So he answered by the difference that the master acknowledged taking her property, then claimed ownership for himself, so he is believed in his acknowledgment but not believed in his claim of ownership. Likewise if he said to a man, 'I ate your food with your permission,' and the other denied permission, the confessor is liable. And the commentator mentioned that this difference is not salvaging as he said. And this branch and its like are excluded by what we added to the rule from our statement 'from every aspect' because her being his slave does not negate liability from him from every aspect, for he is liable if she was pawned or indebted and permitted, so it was not accepted. And the origin of the issue is in al-Majma' from the chapter of confession. He said: 'And if a Muslim convert from war [state] acknowledged taking property before Islam or destroying wine after it, or a Muslim acknowledged property of a war [person] in dar al-harb, or cutting the hand of an emancipated female slave before emancipation, and they denied [the alleged state] in Islam, he gave fatwa of no liability in all.' The author said in his commentary: 'And they both [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: He is liable because he attributed it to a state that may possibly accompany liability in general, so he is not cleared by this attribution.' And in al-Bazzaziyyah: Oil was spilled by a person in the presence of witnesses; its owner claimed his liability, so he said, 'It was impure from the fall of a mouse.' The word is of the spiller for his denial of liability, and the witnesses testify to the spilling, not to the absence of impurity. Likewise if he destroyed meat of a circumambulator and was asked for liability and said, 'It was carrion, so I destroyed it,' he is not believed. And the witnesses may testify that it was ritually slaughtered meat by the apparent circumstance, and the judge said he is not liable. Then an objection was raised against him with the issue from Kitab al-Istihsan: that if a man killed a man and said, 'He apostatized or killed my father, so I killed him as retaliation or for apostasy,' it is not heard. He answered and said: 'Because if it were accepted, it would lead to opening the door of transgression, for he would kill and say that the killing was for that reason, and the matter of blood is grave, so it is not neglected. Unlike property, because it is lighter compared to blood, such that in property judgment is given by default (nukul), while in blood the defendant is imprisoned until he confesses or takes an oath, and one oath suffices in property while fifty oaths in blood.' And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the acceptance of a removed judge's claim regarding his past judicial acts. It states that if a removed judge says he took money from someone and paid it to another as a judgment, the word of the judge is taken, provided the other party acknowledges that the act was done when the judge was in office. This is because the apparent situation (al-zahir) supports the judge, as judges do not unjustly take property. The same applies to amputation cases. The author qualifies that the acknowledgment must be that the act was done while he was a judge; if the claimant says it was before appointment or after removal, there is disagreement, but the correct view is that the judge's word is accepted because he attributes the act to a state that negates liability (a state of authority). This is analogous to a person who says 'I divorced my wife while I was insane' — if his insanity is known, his word is accepted. The text then elaborates the general principle: when a person makes a confession and attributes it to a state that negates liability from every aspect, he is not held liable. Examples include a slave who, after emancipation, says he cut someone's hand while a slave; a master who took usufruct from his slave before emancipation; an agent who sold before removal; an executor who spent on the orphan before majority. However, an objection is raised from al-Nihayah: if a master says he cut his slave-girl's hand while she was his slave, and she says he did it after freeing her, the word is given to her according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. The response is that the master admitted taking her property then claimed ownership, so he is believed in the admission but not in the claim of ownership. The commentator notes this distinction is insufficient, because her being a slave does not negate liability from every aspect — he could be liable if she was pawned or indebted. The passage then mentions an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf hold that if the state only generally negates liability (not from every aspect), the attribution does not clear him, whereas Abu Hanifah takes the stricter view. Further examples are given from al-Bazzaziyyah about spilled oil and destroyed meat, and a comparison with blood crimes where the principle is not applied due to the gravity of blood. The passage concludes that property matters are treated more leniently than blood matters.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): This chapter's title is al-Ḥawālah, but the passage deals with al-Taḥkīm (arbitration/judgment). It seems the text is from the book of Ḥawālah but the specific section is Bāb al-Taḥkīm.
التحكيم (al-taḥkīm): Arbitration or appointment of a judge. The passage discusses the statements of a removed judge (qāḍin maʿzūl).
حالة منافية للضمان (ḥālah munāfiyah li-l-ḍamān): A state that negates liability, such as being a judge (judicial immunity), being insane, being a slave (limited liability), etc.
المعزول (al-maʿzūl): The removed judge, i.e., one who has been dismissed from office. The passage discusses his claim regarding past judicial acts.
من كل وجه (min kulli wajh): From every aspect; i.e., the state must negate liability in all possible circumstances, not just generally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle discussed, if a removed judge claims he took money as part of a judgment, and the other party says he took it unjustly, whose statement is accepted?
A) The party who says it was unjust, because the judge is no longer in office.
B) The judge, because the other party acknowledges that the act was done while he was a judge, and the apparent circumstance supports the judge.
C) The judge only if he brings witnesses.
D) Neither; the case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that attributing an act to a state negating liability from every aspect exonerates the actor, but the scope of 'every aspect' remains a point of ikhtilaf within the school.

Qada — Judiciary

Appointing a Litigation Agent: The Requirement of Opponent's Consent
Stating the Hanafi rule on appointing a representative for litigation and the condition of opponent's consent, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on appointing a litigation agent, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on bindingness, and identify the exceptions where the appointment is binding without opponent consent.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires opponent consent for a litigation agent to be binding; the Sahibayn disagree; exceptions exist for necessity.

Appointing someone to litigate on your behalf (tawkeel fi al-khusuma) is a practical tool in Islamic legal procedure. But what if the opponent refuses to deal with your agent? The Hanafi school has a nuanced ruling: the appointment is valid (ja'iz) by agreement, but whether it is binding (lazim) on the opponent is disputed. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi debate between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, along with the accepted exceptions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds: appointment for litigation requires the opponent's consent to be binding. Without consent, the opponent may reject the agent.
This is the default rule in the school.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the appointment is binding even without opponent consent, because it is an act in one's exclusive right.
They agree on permissibility (jawaz); the dispute is only on bindingness (luzum).
3
Follow the proofs
Examine the evidence for each side: Abu Hanifa's proof from harm and analogy to a jointly-owned slave; the Sahibayn's proof from analogy to collecting debts.
Note the use of qiyas and istihsan.
4
Analyze the exceptions
Identify the exceptions agreed upon by later scholars: if the principal is sick, traveling, or a secluded woman, the appointment is binding without consent due to necessity.
The exception for a secluded woman was not explicit in early texts but adopted by fatwa.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the opponent can refuse the agent; under the Sahibayn, the opponent must accept. The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa but with the exceptions applied.
This affects how a judge handles a litigant's appointed agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبالخصومة في الحقوق برضا الخصم إلا أن يكون الموكل مريضاً أو غائباً مدة السفر أو مريداً للسفر أو مخدرة) أي وصح التوكيل بالخصومة بشرط رضا الخصم وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يجوز بغير رضاه ولا خلاف في الجواز إنما الخلاف في اللزوم؛ لهما أن التوكيل تصرف في خالص حقه فلا يتوقف على رضا غيره كالتوكيل بتقاضي الديون، وله أن الجواب مستحق على الخصم ولهذا يستحضره والناس متفاوتون في الخصومة، فلو قلنا بلزومه يتضرر به فيتوقف على رضاه كالعبد المشترك إذا كاتبه أحدهما يتخير الآخر بخلاف المريض والمسافر لأن الجواب غير مستحق عليهما هنالك ومريده كهو لتحقيق الضرورة، والمخدرة لو حضرت لا يمكنها أن تنطق بحقها لحيائها فيلزم توكيلها وهذا شيء استحسنه المتأخرون؛ كذا في الهداية. وظاهره أن المخدرة لا نص عليها في المذهب ولهذا قال في فتح القدير: أما على ظاهر إطلاق الأصل وغيره عن أبي حنيفة فلا فرق بين البكر والثيب والمخدرة والمبرزة والفتوى على ما اختاروه من ذلك ا.هـ. والخصومة الجدل، خاصمه مخاصمة وخصومة فخصمه يخصمه غلبه وهو شاذ لأن فاعلته ففعلته يرد يفعل منه إلى الضم إن لم تكن عينه حرف حلق فإنه بالفتح كفاخره فقخره يفخره، أما المعتل كوجدت وبعت فيرد إلى الكسر إلا ذوات الواو فإنها ترد إلى الضم كراضيته فرضوته أرضوه وخاوفني فخفته أخوفه، وليس في كل شيء يقال نازعته لأنهم استغنوا عنه بغلبته . واختصموا تخاصموا والخصم المخاصم والجمع الخصوم، وقد يكون للجمع والاثنين والمؤنث والخصم المخاصم والجمع خصماء؛ في القاموس . هذا معناها لغة . وأما شرعاً فهو الجواب بنعم أو لا كما سيأتي . وفسرها في الجوهرة بالدعوى الصحيحة أو بالجواب الصريح ولو وكله في الخصومة له لا عليه فله كذا قوله : ( وزعم) أي المقرض وقوله وأقر الرسول أي بالقبض رملي قوله : (لا يلزم المستقرض شيء) قال الرملي : وهل يلزم الرسول الجواب؟ لا لأنه أمين يقبل قوله في حق براءة نفسه لا في لزوم الدين ذمة المستقرض كرسول المديون بالدين إلى الدائن إذا أنكر وصوله إليه وادعى الرسول إيصاله إليه يقبل قوله في حق نفسه لا في حق براءة الدائن تأمل. قوله: (ولا خلاف في الجواز إنما الخلاف في اللزوم قال في الجوهرة: يعني هل ترد الوكالة
His statement: '(And [it is valid] to appoint an agent) for litigation in rights with the consent of the opponent, unless the principal is sick, or absent for the duration of travel, or intending to travel, or a secluded woman.' That is, appointment for litigation is valid conditioned on the opponent's consent, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is permissible without his consent, and there is no disagreement on permissibility; the disagreement is only on bindingness (luzum). Their proof is that appointment is an act in one's exclusive right, so it should not depend on the consent of others, like appointment to collect debts. His proof is that the opponent is entitled to a response, and for this he is summoned, and people differ in litigation skills; if we say it is binding, the opponent would be harmed, so it depends on his consent, like the jointly owned slave if one partner grants him kitabah, the other has a choice. Except for the sick and traveler because the response is not owed to them there, and the one intending to travel is like the traveler due to genuine necessity. The secluded woman, if she were to appear, cannot articulate her right due to shyness, so her appointment is binding. This is something the later scholars approved of. Thus in al-Hidayah. Its apparent meaning is that there is no explicit text in the school regarding the secluded woman; therefore, al-Fath al-Qadir said: According to the apparent absolute wording of the original and others from Abu Hanifa, there is no difference between a virgin, a non-virgin, secluded, or unveiled woman, and the fatwa is according to what they chose of that. End. Litigation (khusuma) is argumentation. He disputed with him... (linguistic explanation). This is its lexical meaning. As for its legal meaning, it is answering with 'yes' or 'no' as will come. And it was explained in al-Jawharah as a valid claim or explicit response. If he appoints him for litigation in his favor not against him, then it is... His statement: 'And he claimed' i.e., the lender; and his statement 'and the messenger affirmed' i.e., receipt. Ramli: His statement: 'Nothing is binding on the borrower.' Al-Ramli said: Is the messenger obligated to answer? No, because he is a trustee; his word is accepted regarding his own exoneration, not regarding the debt being binding on the borrower, like the messenger of the debtor with the debt to the creditor if he denies its receipt and the messenger claims delivery; his word is accepted regarding himself, not regarding the exoneration of the debtor. Consider. His statement: 'There is no disagreement on permissibility; the disagreement is only on bindingness.' He said in al-Jawharah: Meaning, is the agency to be returned?
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on a line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It discusses the ruling on appointing an agent for litigation (khusuma). According to Abu Hanifa, such an appointment is valid only if the opponent consents to the representation; without consent, the appointment is not binding on the opponent. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the appointment is valid and binding without the opponent's consent because the principal has the right to delegate his own affairs. However, both agree that the appointment is permissible (ja'iz) in principle; the dispute is about whether it is binding (lazim) on the opponent, i.e., whether the opponent must accept the agent. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the opponent has a right to face his litigant, and people vary in litigation skill; forcing an agent on the opponent could cause harm, just as in the case of a jointly-owned slave, one partner cannot force kitabah on the other. The exception: if the principal is sick, traveling, or a secluded woman (mukhaddara), then the appointment is binding even without consent due to necessity or inability to appear. The passage notes that the ruling for the secluded woman is not explicitly in the early texts; later scholars (mutakaddimun) adopted it by istihsan. The fatwa (operative ruling) follows this exception. The passage also provides linguistic definitions of 'khusuma' (litigation, argumentation) and its legal meaning (answering with yes or no, or a valid claim). It also includes a brief discussion on the liability of a messenger in loan transactions: if a messenger claims delivery, his word is accepted for his own innocence but not for establishing debt on the borrower. This is consistent with the principle that a trustee's word is accepted regarding himself.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): The act of litigation or legal argumentation; legally, it means answering with 'yes' or 'no' (i.e., pleading) or making a valid claim.
الرضا (al-rida): Consent of the opponent for the appointment of a litigation agent to be binding.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Bindingness; whether the opponent is compelled to accept the appointed agent.
المخدرة (al-mukhaddarah): A woman who is secluded and does not appear in public; due to shyness, she cannot articulate her right in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, when is the appointment of a litigation agent binding on the opponent without the opponent's consent?
A) In all cases, because agency is a personal right.
B) Only if the principal is sick, traveling, or a secluded woman.
C) Only if the opponent also appoints an agent.
D) Never; the opponent must always consent.

Key Takeaway
The appointment of a litigation agent is valid without opponent consent, but its bindingness requires consent except in cases of genuine necessity (sickness, travel, seclusion).

Excuses for Appointing an Agent in Court and the Tarjih Between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on excuses and tarjih between Imam and Sahibayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid excuses for a principal to appoint an agent in Hanafi law and explain the tarjih between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the permissibility of agency.

Core Idea
A principal with a valid excuse may appoint an agent; later Hanafis prefer the Sahibayn's view that agency is generally permissible.

In Hanafi qada (judicial procedure), a litigant may need to appoint an agent to appear in court. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses which excuses make such appointment mandatory, and then presents a major intra-madhhab dispute: does the principal always need a personal excuse, or can agency be used freely? The resolution of this dispute through later tarjih is a classic example of how the Hanafi school evolves through scholarly preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses two interrelated issues: (1) what counts as a valid excuse (udhr) for a principal to appoint an agent, and (2) whether agency in litigation is generally permissible even without an excuse. Clarify that the author (Ibn Nujaym) initially follows Abu Hanifa's stricter view, but later tarjih favors the Sahibayn.
The terms 'mukil' (principal) and 'wakil' (agent) are central.
2
List the excuses and their conditions
Extract from the text the three excuses mentioned: menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) of the litigant, and imprisonment (habs) by a judge other than the presiding judge. Note the conditions: menstruation of the defendant is an excuse only if the hearing is in the mosque and the plaintiff does not consent to delay; menstruation of the plaintiff is an excuse absolutely. Imprisonment is an excuse only if by a different judge; if by the same judge, the person can be brought out for the hearing.
The principle is that the excuse must create genuine hardship in personal appearance.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the principal may be compelled to appear personally if he has no valid excuse. Agency without excuse is not automatically permissible. The passage says the author chose Imam's view.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
4
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that agency in litigation is always permissible, regardless of excuse or the opponent's consent. Their view is based on general permissibility of agency and removal of hardship.
Their dalil is the Quranic permission for agency and the principle of easing transactions.
5
Analyze the tarjih among later scholars
The passage cites several later authorities: al-Faqih, al-Ghiyathi, al-Saffar, Khizanat al-Muftiyin, and al-Nihaya all prefer the Sahibayn. Al-Halwani gives the mufti a choice, while al-Bazzaziyya warns about delegating to judges in corrupt times. The mufta bihi today is the Sahibayn's view.
Tarjih is based on practicality and the changing needs of the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومراد المؤلف من الاستثناء استثناء الموكل إذا كان له عذر ولا يختص بالأربعة فشمل حيض المدعى عليها إذا كان الحكم في المسجد ؛ كذا ذكره الشارح وهو مقيد بما إذا كان الطالب لا يرضى بالتأخير فيما إذا كان الحكم في غير المسجد، وأما إذا رضي به فلا يكون عذراً، وأما حيض الطالبة فهو عذر مطلقاً والنفاس كالحيض ؛ كذا في خزانة المفتين. ومن العذر الحبس إذا كان من غير القاضي الذي ترافعوا إليه ؛ ذكره الشارح وفي البزازية : وكونه محبوساً من الأعذار يلزمه توكيله فعلى هذا لو كان الشاهد محبوساً له أن يشهد على شهادته . قال القاضي : إن كان في سجن القاضي لا يكون عذراً لأنه يخرجه حتى يشهد ثم يعيده، وعلى هذا يمكن أن يقال في الدعوى أيضاً كذلك بأن يجيب عن الدعوى ثم يعاد ولو مدعياً يدعي إن لم يؤخر دعواه ثم يعاد ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن المؤلف اختار قول الإمام كما هو دأبه، وقد اختلف ترجيح المشايخ فأفتى الفقيه بقولهما. وقال الغيائي: وهو المختار وبه أخذ الصفار أيضاً. وفي خزانة المفتين : المختار قولهما والشريف وغيره سواء. وفي النهاية والصحيح قولهما. وقال الحلواني : يخير المفتي قال: ونحن نفتي أن الرأي للحاكم. وفي البزازية ومن المعلوم المقرر أن تفويض الخيار إلى قضاة عهد الفساد كما هو المقرر من أن علمهم ليس بحجة. قال شمس الأئمة :
قوله : (وهو مقيد بما إذا كان الطالب لا يرضى بالتأخير الخ قال في الجوهرة: إن كانت هي طالبة قبل منها التوكيل بغير رضا الخصم وإن كانت مطلوبة إن أخرها الطالب حتى يخرج القاضي من المسجد لا يقبل منها التوكيل بغير رضا الخصم الطالب لأنه لا عذر لها إلى التوكيل اهـ.
The author's intent from the exception is to exclude the principal when he has an excuse, and it is not limited to four [excuses]; thus it includes the menstruation of the defendant if the judgment is in the mosque; as the commentator mentioned, and it is restricted to when the plaintiff does not consent to delay if the judgment is outside the mosque; but if he consents, it is not an excuse. The menstruation of the plaintiff is an excuse absolutely, and postpartum bleeding is like menstruation; as in Khizanat al-Muftiyin. Among the excuses is imprisonment if it is by a judge other than the one to whom they have resorted; the commentator mentioned it, and in al-Bazzaziyya: being imprisoned is among the excuses that necessitate appointing an agent; accordingly, if the witness is imprisoned, he may testify on his testimony. The judge said: If he is in the judge's prison, it is not an excuse because he can bring him out to testify and then return him; and accordingly it can be said in litigation as well, that he answers the lawsuit and then is returned, even if the plaintiff claims that his lawsuit not be delayed and then he is returned. Then know that the author chose the statement of Imam [Abu Hanifa] as is his habit, and the preference of the mashayikh differed; al-Faqih gave fatwa based on their statement. Al-Ghiyathi said: it is the chosen one, and al-Saffar also adopted it. In Khizanat al-Muftiyin: the chosen is their statement, and al-Sharif and others are equal. In al-Nihaya: the correct is their statement. Al-Halwani said: the mufti is given choice, and he said: we issue fatwa that the decision is up to the judge. In al-Bazzaziyya: it is known and established that delegating choice to judges in an age of corruption is as established that their knowledge is not a proof. Shams al-A'imma said: His statement: 'It is restricted to when the plaintiff does not consent to delay...' He said in al-Jawhara: If she is the plaintiff, her agency is accepted without the opponent's consent; if she is the defendant and the plaintiff delays until the judge leaves the mosque, her agency is not accepted without the plaintiff's consent because she has no excuse for agency.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and a ruling on valid excuses (a'dhar) that allow a principal to appoint an agent in litigation instead of appearing in person. The author (likely of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) made an exception for principals with excuses. The commentary expands on this: menstruation of the defendant is an excuse only if the hearing is in the mosque and the plaintiff does not consent to delay; if outside the mosque, consent matters. Menstruation of the plaintiff is always an excuse, as is postpartum bleeding. Imprisonment is also an excuse, but only if by a different judge; if by the same judge, he can bring the person out for the hearing and then return him. The second part discusses tarjih (preference) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding whether the principal must personally appear or may appoint an agent. The author (Ibn Nujaym) favored Abu Hanifa's view that the principal may be required to appear if no excuse, but many later scholars (like al-Faqih, al-Ghiyathi, al-Saffar) favored the Sahibayn's view that agency is generally permissible. Al-Halwani gave the mufti choice, while al-Bazzaziyya cautioned that delegating to judges in corrupt times is problematic. Then a quote from al-Jawhara clarifies that for a female plaintiff, her agency is accepted without opponent's consent; for a female defendant, consent is needed if she has no excuse.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A legally recognized excuse that exempts a person from certain obligations or allows delegation.
ترجيح (tarjih): The process of preferring one scholarly opinion over another within the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, whose view on the permissibility of appointing an agent without an excuse is preferred (mufta bihi) by later Hanafi scholars?
A) Imam Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn)
C) Al-Halwani's choice for the mufti
D) Al-Bazzaziyya's delegation to the judge

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a litigant may appoint an agent even without an excuse, as later scholars preferred the Sahibayn's view over Imam Abu Hanifa's stricter requirement.

Agent for Litigation Does Not Authorize Receipt of Debt: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling differentiating the scope of agency for litigation and receipt of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school's operative fatwa holds that an agent for litigation does not have authority to receive the debt, and distinguish the positions of Imam Zufar and the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).

Core Idea
An agent for litigation owns receipt by default but later fatwa follows Zufar due to widespread betrayal.

This lesson examines a ruling from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the scope of agency for litigation. While the majority of Hanafi imams held that an agent for litigation implicitly owns receipt because ownership of something includes its completion, Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that the principal only consented to litigation. Due to the prevalence of betrayal among agents, later scholars adopted Zufar's view as the operative fatwa, making the agent's receipt unauthorized. We will analyze the proofs and the reasoning behind this shift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is the appointment of an agent for litigation. The ruling according to the current fatwa (mufta bihi) is that this agent does not have authority to receive the debt (qabd).
The default position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is the opposite, but the operative fatwa has changed.
2
Locate the dalil for the majority
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) use a qiyas based on the principle 'whoever owns something owns its completion'. They argue that litigation's completion is receipt, so the agent for litigation owns receipt.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Examine Zufar's objection
Imam Zufar argues that the principal only consented to litigation, not to receipt, which is a separate act. Therefore, the agent does not own receipt.
Zufar's view is based on limited consent, more restrictive in scope.
4
Understand the tarjih (preference)
Later scholars, as stated in al-Hidayah, declared the fatwa to be on Zufar's view due to the prevalence of betrayal (khayanah) among agents. This is an application of istihsan overriding the original qiyas.
The change in circumstances (zuhur al-khiyanah) justified the shift.
5
Note the scope of agency for taqadi
The passage also discusses the agent for demanding (taqadi). Originally, such an agent owns receipt because the word 'taqadi' linguistically includes taking, but later custom overrides, making fatwa depend on local merchant custom.
This shows the role of 'urf (custom) in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض الوكيل بالخصومة والتقاضي لا يملك القبض وهذا قول زفر لأنه رضي بخصومته والقبض غيرها ولم يرض به، وعندنا هو وكيل بالقبض لأن من ملك شيئاً ملك إتمامه، وتمام الخصومة وانتهاؤها بالقبض والفتوى اليوم على قول زفر لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء، وقد يؤتمن على الخصومة من لا يؤتمن على المال ونظيره الوكيل بالتقاضي يملك القبض على أصل الرواية لأنه في معناه وضعاً لما في الأساس تقاضيته ديني وبديني واقتضيته ديني واستقضيته، واقتضيت منه حقي أي أخذته إلا أن العرف بخلافه وهو قاض على الوضع والفتوى على أنه لا يملك؛ كذا في الهداية. وفي الفتاوى الصغرى التوكيل بالتقاضي يعتمد العرف إن كان في بلدة كان العرف بين التجار أن المتقاضي هو الذي يقبض الدين كان التوكيل بالتقاضي توكيلاً بالقبض وإلا فلا ذكره عن الفضل ا هـ . قيد بالوكيل لأن الرسول بالتقاضي يملك القبض لأنه بمنزلة الرسول في القبض ولا يملك الخصومة إجماعاً ؛ كذا في الصغرى أيضاً. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن الوكيل بالخصومة لا يصالح وإلى أن الوكيل بالملازمة لا يملك الخصومة والقبض. وفي البزازية وهنا عشر مسائل : الوكيل بقبض الدين أو العين وسيأتي، وبالخصومة أو التقاضي أو بالملازمة ،وقدمناها وبالقسمة وبالأخذ بالشفعة وبالرجوع في الهبة يملك الخصومة والقبض وبالرد بالعيب يخاصم ويحلف والوكيل يحفظ العين لا يخاصم، ولو وكله بطلب كل حق له على الناس أو بكل حل له بخوارزم يدخل القائم لا الحادث. وذكر شيخ الإسلام أنه إذا وكله بقبض كل حق له على فلان يدخل القائم والحادث أيضاً فليتأمل عند الفتوى. وفي المنتقى : وكله بقبض كل دين له يدخل الحادث أيضاً كما لو وكله بقبض غلته يقبض الغلة الحادثة أيضا وقد فاته الوكيل بالصلح فإنه لا يخاصم كما في كافي الحاكم من باب الوكالة بالدم. وفي منية المفتي : ادعى أن فلاناً وكله بطلب كل حق بالكوفة وبقبضه هـ. . باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض الأب في حال كبرهم قوله : (وفي الفتاوى الصغرى الخ) نقل في المنح عن السراجية أن عليه الفتوى، وفي القهستاني عن المضمرات والآن يحكم عرف التجار وبه يفتى.
Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Receipt: The agent for litigation and lawsuits does not own receipt. This is the saying of Zufar because he is satisfied with his litigation, and receipt is other than it, and he is not satisfied with it. And according to us, he is an agent for receipt because whoever owns something owns its completion, and the completion of litigation and its termination is by receipt. And the fatwa today is on the saying of Zufar due to the prevalence of betrayal among agents. Sometimes one is entrusted with litigation who is not entrusted with wealth. Its parallel: The agent for demanding (taqadi) owns receipt according to the original transmission because it is in its meaning by convention, as in al-Asas: 'I demanded my debt and my debt, and I collected my debt, and I collected from him my right' meaning I took it, except that custom is contrary to it and it is a judge on the convention, and the fatwa is that he does not own it; thus in al-Hidayah. And in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: Agency for demand (taqadi) depends on custom; if in a town the custom among merchants is that the demander (mutaqadi) is the one who receives the debt, then agency for demand is agency for receipt; otherwise not; mentioned from al-Fadl. End. It is qualified by 'agent' because the messenger for demand owns receipt because he is like a messenger in receipt, and he does not own litigation by consensus; thus in al-Sughra as well. And the author indicated that the agent for litigation does not make a settlement, and that the agent for attachment (mulazama) does not own litigation or receipt. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: Here are ten cases: The agent for receipt of debt or specific property will come later, and for litigation or demand or attachment—we have presented them—and for division, and for taking by preemption, and for retraction of gift: he owns litigation and receipt. And for return due to defect: he litigates and takes oath. The agent for custody of an object does not litigate. If he appoints him to claim every right he has over people, or every legal claim he has in Khwarazm, it includes existing but not future rights. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that if he appoints him to receive every right he has over so-and-so, it includes both existing and future rights as well; let caution be taken when giving fatwa. And in al-Muntaqa: If he appoints him to receive every debt he has, it also includes future debt, just as if he appoints him to receive his produce, he receives the future produce as well. And the agent for settlement: he does not litigate as in Kafi al-Hakim from the chapter of agency in blood claims. And in Munyat al-Mufti: If he claims that so-and-so appointed him to claim every right in Kufa and to receive it. End. Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Receipt: The father in their majority. He said: (And in al-Fatawa al-Sughra etc.) It is transmitted in al-Minhaj from al-Sirajiyyah that fatwa is upon it, and in al-Qahistani from al-Mudmarat: And now the custom of merchants prevails and fatwa is given by it.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on agency for litigation and receipt. It presents the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'The agent for litigation and demand (taqadi) does not own receipt.' The commentary then explains that this is the view of Imam Zufar, who reasons that the principal consented only to litigation, not to receipt. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that he does own receipt because ownership of a thing includes its completion; litigation ends with receipt. However, the operative fatwa today (later practice) is on Zufar's view due to widespread betrayal (khayanah) among agents. The text then details exceptions: an agent for demanding (taqadi) owns receipt by default according to the original transmission because the term 'taqadi' linguistically includes taking, but custom now overrides, so fatwa is that he does not own receipt unless local custom among merchants says otherwise. A messenger (rasul) for demand does own receipt by consensus. The passage then lists many scenarios from al-Bazzaziyyah, specifying which types of agents own litigation and receipt. It ends with a note from later sources that the custom of merchants is now the basis for fatwa.

Key Terms
وكيل بالخصومة (wakil bi al-khusumah): An agent appointed specifically to conduct litigation (defend or sue) on behalf of the principal.
تقاضي (taqadi): Demanding or claiming a debt; linguistically includes taking receipt, but custom may differentiate.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive receipt of the debt or object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa today (al-yawm) in the Hanafi school, does an agent for litigation (الوكيل بالخصومة) have the authority to receive the debt?
A) Yes, because he owns the completion of litigation
B) No, because Zufar's view is followed due to prevalence of betrayal
C) Yes, if the principal does not object
D) No, because he is not a messenger

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative fatwa is that an agent for litigation does not have authority to receive the debt, based on Zufar's view due to prevalence of betrayal, overriding the original qiyas of the majority.

Proving Agency in Court: The Requirement of a Present Opponent
Conditions for judge to accept evidence of agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedural rule for proving agency in court when the principal is absent and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on accepting simultaneous evidence for both agency and the right.

Core Idea
A judge cannot hear evidence of agency without a present opponent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a fundamental procedural rule in Hanafi qada: an agent must bring a specific opponent (khasm) before the judge will hear witnesses to confirm the agency. Without an opponent, there is no adversary to contest or admit the claim, so the judge has no basis to adjudicate. The text also addresses what happens when an agent brings evidence for both the agency and the underlying right simultaneously, revealing an internal disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The judge does not hear evidence of agency until the agent brings a present opponent, whether that opponent denies or admits the right.
This is the default rule; no opponent means no hearing.
2
Understand the exception for subsequent opponents
Once the agency is proved with one opponent, the agent does not need to re-prove it for later opponents.
The agency is established once and applies to all claims against that specific opponent or others.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
When evidence is presented simultaneously for both agency and the right, Imam Abu Hanifa accepts it only for agency, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) accept it for both.
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the agent must bring separate evidence for the right; under the Sahibayn, one set of witnesses suffices for both.
4
Examine additional conditions from Munyat al-Mufti and al-Qunyah
If the principal appears personally and appoints an agent in court but without an opponent, the appointment is valid only if the judge knows the principal's identity. Also, if a judge rules on agency without a present opponent, the ruling is still valid according to one view, because it was a procedural matter heard without an adversary.
These nuances show that the requirement of an opponent is procedural, not substantive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالخصومة فيه وجاء بالبينة على الوكالة والموكل غائب ولم يحضر الوكيل أحداً قبله للموكل حق فالقاضي لا يسمع من شهوده حتى يحضر خصماً جاحداً لذلك أو مقراً به فحينئذ يسمع وينقذ له الوكالة، فإن أحضر بعد ذلك غريماً آخر لم يحتج إلى إعادة البينة، ولو ادعى الوكالة بطلب كل حق له قبل إنسان بعينه يشترط حضوره ،بعينه وإذا ثبت بحضوره فجاء بخصم آخر يقيم البينة على الوكالة مرة أخرى ادعى أنه وكله بقبض كل حق له ولموكله على هذا كذا وأقام بينة شهدوا على الوكالة والحق على المدعى عليه دفعة واحدة تقبل على الوكالة لا غير ويؤمر بإعادة البينة على الحق عند الإمام، وعندهما تقبل على الأمرين يقضي بالوكالة أو لا ثم بالمال كذا لو ادعى به وصي الميت ا هـ . وفي منية المفتي أيضاً ولو حضر الموكل إلى القاضي ووكل الوكيل وليس معه خصم جاز وكان وكيلاً إن كان يعرف القاضي الموكل، وإن لم يعرف القاضي لا يجوز لأن الموكل وقت القضاء بالوكالة غائب والغائب إنما يصير معلوماً بالاسم والنسب، فإذا كان القاضي يعرف اسم الموكل ونسبه أمكن القضاء بالوكالة وإلا لو قضى بها قضى لمعلوم على مجهول، فإن قال الموكل أنا أقيم البينة على أني فلان بن فلان منه لأن شرط سماعها على النسب الخصومة فيه ولم يوجد ا هـ . وفي القنية : لا يقبل من الوكيل بالخصومة بينة على وكالته من غير خصم ،حاضر، ولو قضى بها صح المختلف اهـ . لأنه قضاء في لم يسمع
And regarding litigation in it, he came with evidence of agency while the principal is absent and the agent did not bring anyone against whom the principal has a right, so the judge does not hear his witnesses until he brings an opponent who denies that or admits it, then he hears and executes the agency for him. If after that he brings another opponent, he does not need to repeat the evidence. If he claims agency to claim every right he has against a specific person, his presence is stipulated. And when it is established by his presence, then if he comes with another opponent to present evidence of agency again, claiming that he appointed him to take possession of every right he has and for his principal so-and-so is owed, and he presents witnesses who testify to the agency and the right against the defendant all at once, it is accepted only regarding the agency, and he is ordered to repeat evidence on the right according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions, it is accepted for both matters: he rules by the agency or not, then by the property. Similarly, if the executor of a deceased claims it. (End). And in Munyat al-Mufti also: If the principal appears before the judge and appoints an agent while there is no opponent with him, it is permissible and he becomes an agent if the judge knows the principal. If the judge does not know, it is not permissible because the principal at the time of ruling on the agency is absent, and an absent person is only known by name and lineage. If the judge knows the name and lineage of the principal, ruling on the agency is possible; otherwise, if he rules, he would rule for a known person against an unknown one. If the principal says, 'I will present evidence that I am so-and-so son of so-and-so,' it is not accepted because the condition for hearing evidence on lineage is that the opponent is present in it, and it does not exist. (End). And in al-Qunyah: The evidence of agency from an agent in litigation is not accepted without a present opponent. And if he rules by it, it is valid according to the difference. (End). Because it is a ruling in a [matter] that was not heard.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage addresses the procedural rule for proving agency in court when the principal is absent. The author states the general ruling: if an agent appointed for litigation comes to court without having brought a specific opponent (i.e., someone against whom the principal has a claim), the judge will not hear the agent's witnesses to prove the agency until he brings an opponent, whether that opponent denies or admits the right. Once the agent brings such an opponent and the judge confirms the agency, if the agent later brings another opponent, he does not need to re-prove the agency. The text then discusses a scenario where the agent claims agency over all rights against a specific person, and then brings evidence both for the agency and the right simultaneously. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the evidence is accepted only for the agency, and the agent must repeat evidence for the right; according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the evidence is accepted for both. A similar rule applies to the executor of a deceased. Next, from Munyat al-Mufti: if the principal himself appears before the judge and appoints an agent in the judge's presence without an opponent, the agency is valid only if the judge knows the principal by name and lineage. If the judge does not know him, the appointment is invalid because the principal is effectively absent at the time of ruling (since the judge cannot identify him), and one cannot rule for a known person against an unknown. Finally, from al-Qunyah: the judge should not accept evidence of agency without a present opponent, but if he rules based on such evidence, the ruling is valid (according to one difference of opinion), because the ruling was in a matter that was not heard (i.e., the opponent was absent and thus the ruling was procedural rather than substantive). The overall principle is that the presence of an opponent is necessary for the judge to hear evidence of agency, except when the principal is personally known to the judge.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): An opponent in litigation, someone against whom a claim is made or who disputes the claim.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony, presented to prove a claim in court.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; a contract whereby one party (principal) appoints another (agent) to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what must an agent do before the judge will hear his witnesses regarding the agency?
A) Bring the principal to court
B) Bring a present opponent (خصم حاضر)
C) Provide a written contract of agency
D) Swear an oath that he is the agent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi procedural law, a judge cannot hear evidence of agency without a present opponent, but if the opponent is present, the agency can be established and applied to other opponents later.

Agent's Acknowledgment in Litigation: Ruling and Dispute
Expansion probe on agent's acknowledgment with internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on an agent's acknowledgment in litigation and explain the internal dispute among Abu Hanifa's companions, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment in court is valid; outside court it is invalid by istihsan, and the agent exits the agency.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling for an agent's acknowledgment in litigation. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute about the validity of such acknowledgment when made outside the courtroom. We will explore the positions of Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and identify the relied-upon view that governs fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its scope
The passage begins by stating that an agent's acknowledgment in litigation is valid if made before a judge; otherwise it is not valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Note that the ruling is restricted to non-hadd and non-qisas cases.
The agent is appointed specifically for litigation (khusumah).
2
Analyze the qiyas argument (Zufar's position)
Zufar invalidates acknowledgment absolutely based on qiyas: the agent is commanded to litigate (contend), but acknowledgment is the opposite (conciliation), so the command does not include its opposite. Therefore, acknowledgment is never within the agent's authority.
This is the pure analogical reasoning.
3
Analyze the istihsan argument (the two's position)
The two argue that the authorization is for a general response. Acknowledgment in court is metaphorically considered litigation because it appears as a response in the litigation context. Istihsan favors validity in court to give effect to the authorization, but outside court it is invalid and the agent exits the agency.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
4
Evaluate Abu Yusuf's alternative and the tarjih
Abu Yusuf holds acknowledgment valid absolutely because the agent stands in the place of the principal. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two, as preferred in al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.
The tarjih is based on avoiding contradiction and preserving the integrity of the agency.
5
Apply the ruling to the five scenarios of authorization
The passage lists five possible authorizations: (1) full litigation agency, (2) excepting acknowledgment, (3) excepting denial, (4) permitting both, (5) excepting both. The ruling varies accordingly. For example, if acknowledgment is excepted, the agent can only deny; if both are excepted, there is disagreement among later scholars.
Understanding these scenarios is crucial for practical fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو) أقر الوكيل بالخصومة عند القاضي صح وإلا (لا) أي وإن أقر على موكله عند غير القاضي لا يصح عندهما استحساناً وخرج به عن الوكالة، وصحح أبو يوسف إقراره مطلقاً، وأبطله زفر مطلقاً وهو القياس لكونه مأموراً بالخصومة وهي منازعة والإقرار ضدها لأنه مسالمة، فالأمر بالشيء لا يتناول ضده ولذا لا يملك الصلح والإبراء. وجه الاستحسان أن التوكيل صحيح وصحته تتناول ما يملك وذلك مطلق الجواب دون أحدهما عيناً فيصرف إليه تحرياً للصحة، فأبو يوسف يقول هو قائم مقام الموكل فلا يختص إقراره بمجلس القضاء، وهما يقولان إن التوكيل يتناول جواباً يسمى خصومة حقيقة إن أنكر أو مجازاً إن أقر، والإقرار في مجلس القضاء خصومة مجازاً لأنه خرج في مقابلة الخصومة أو لأنه له لأن الظاهر إتيانه بالمستحق وهو الجواب في مجلس القضاء فيختص به لكن إذا أقيمت البينة على إقراره في غير مجلس القضاء يخرج من الوكالة حتى لا يؤمر بدفع المال إليه لأنه صار مناقضاً وصار كالأب والوصي إذا أقرا في مجلس القضاء لا يصح ولا يدفع المال إليهما؛ كذا في الهداية. أطلقه وهو مقيد بغير الحد والقود فلا يصح إقرار الوكيل على موكله بهما للشبهة . وقيد بالخصومة لأن الوكيل بغيرها لا يصح إقراره مطلقاً، ومنه الوكيل بالصلح كما في كافي الحاكم كالوكيل بالخصومة لا يملك الصلح والصلح عقد من العقود فالوكيل بعقد لا يباشر عقداً آخر. وقيد بالتوكيل بالخصومة من غير استثناء لأنه لو وكله بها إلا الإقرار فعن أبي يوسف لا يصح وصححه محمد، وعنه أنه فصل بين الطالب والمطلوب فلم يصححه في المال فقال نعم تأمل قوله : وصار كالأب والوصي إذا أقر أي على اليتيم أنه استوفى حقه في مجلس القضاء لا يصح إقرارهما ولكن لا يدفع المال إليهما لزعمهما بطلان حق الأخذ. وإنما لا . إقرارهما لأن ولايتهما نظرية ولا نظر في الإقرار على الصغير، وأما التفويض من الموكل حصل مطلقاً غير مقيد بشرط النطر فيدخل تحته الإقرار والإنكار جميعاً غير أن الإقرار صحته تختص بمجلس القضاء على ما ذكرنا؛ كذا في الكفاية . الثاني؛ كذا في الهداية وفي النهاية يصح استثناء الإقرار في ظاهر الرواية. وفي البزازية : ولو وكله بالخصومة غير جائز الإقرار صح ولم يصح الإقرار في الظاهر لو موصولاً. وفي الأقضية ومفصولاً أيضاً ولو وكله غير جائز الإنكار يصح عند محمد، ولو غير جائز الإقرار والإنكار قيل لا يصح الاستثناء لعدم بقاء فرد تحته وقيل يصح لبقاء السكوت ا هـ . فالحاصل أنها على خمسة أوجه كما في الذخيرة : الأول أن يوكله بالخصومة فيصير وكيلاً بهما . الثاني أن يستثني الإقرار فيكون وكيلاً بالإنكار فقط . الثالث عكسه فيصير وكيلاً بالإقرار فقط في ظاهر الرواية لأن الموكل ربما يضره الإنكار بأن كان المدعي به أمانة، ولو جحدها الوكيل يصح دعوى الرد بعده ويصح قبله ففيه فائدة الرابع أن يوكله بالخصومة جائز الإقرار فيكون وكيلاً بهما. الخامس أن يوكله بها غير جائز الإقرار والإنكار ففيه اختلاف المتأخرين الخلاصة: ولو كان التوكيل بسؤال الخصم واستثني الإقرار موصولاً صح وفي ومفصولا لا ا هـ. ويصح التوكيل بالإقرار ولا يصير به مقراً؛ كذا في النهاية. وفي منية المفتي : إذا استثنى إقراره فأقر خرج عن الوكالة . اهـ.
His statement: (And if) the agent acknowledged in litigation before the judge, it is valid; otherwise (not), meaning if he acknowledged on behalf of his principal before a non-judge, it is not valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) by istihsan, and he exits the agency. Abu Yusuf declared his acknowledgment valid absolutely, and Zufar invalidated it absolutely, and that is qiyas because he is commanded to litigate, which is contention, and acknowledgment is its opposite because it is conciliation. The command to a thing does not encompass its opposite, hence he does not have the right to compromise or release. The basis of istihsan is that the authorization is valid and its validity encompasses what he has, which is absolute response without specifying one of them, so it is directed towards validity. So Abu Yusuf says he stands in the place of the principal, so his acknowledgment is not restricted to the court. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that the authorization encompasses a response called litigation in reality if he denies, or metaphorically if he acknowledges. Acknowledgment in the court is litigation metaphorically because it appears in contrast to litigation, or because of it, since the apparent is that he brings what is due, which is the response in the court, so it is restricted to it. But if evidence is established of his acknowledgment outside the court, he exits the agency so that he is not ordered to pay the money to him because he became contradictory, and he becomes like the father and executor when they acknowledge in the court: it is not valid and the money is not paid to them. This is in al-Hidayah. He made it absolute, and it is restricted to other than hadd and qisas, so the agent's acknowledgment on behalf of his principal regarding them is not valid due to doubt. And it is restricted to litigation because the agent for other than it, his acknowledgment is not valid absolutely. From this is the agent for compromise, as in Kafi al-Hakim: like the agent for litigation, he does not have the right to compromise, and compromise is a contract among contracts, so the agent for a contract does not perform another contract. And it is restricted to authorization for litigation without exception, because if he authorized him for it except for acknowledgment, then from Abu Yusuf it is not valid, and Muhammad declared it valid. And from him (Abu Yusuf) that he distinguished between the claimant and the defendant: he did not declare it valid in property, so he said: yes, ponder his statement: and he becomes like the father and executor when they acknowledge, i.e., on behalf of the orphan that he received his right in court: their acknowledgment is not valid, but the money is not paid to them because they claim the invalidity of the right to take. And the reason their acknowledgment is not valid is because their guardianship is based on consideration (nazar), and there is no consideration in acknowledging against a minor. As for the delegation from the principal, it is obtained absolutely without being conditioned by consideration, so acknowledgment and denial both fall under it, except that the validity of acknowledgment is restricted to the court as we mentioned. This is in al-Kifayah. The second: likewise in al-Hidayah; in al-Nihayah, it is valid to except acknowledgment in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya). And in al-Bazzaziyyah: if he authorized him for litigation without permission to acknowledge, it is valid, but the acknowledgment is not valid in the apparent ruling if it is connected. And in al-Aqdiyyah: and also if disconnected. And if he authorized him without permission to deny, it is valid according to Muhammad. And if without permission to acknowledge and deny, it is said the exception is not valid due to no individual remaining under it, and it is said it is valid due to the remaining of silence. End. So the conclusion is that there are five aspects as in al-Dhakhirah: First, that he authorizes him for litigation, so he becomes agent for both. Second, that he excepts acknowledgment, so he becomes agent for denial only. Third, the opposite, so he becomes agent for acknowledgment only in the apparent transmission, because the principal might be harmed by denial if the claim is a trust, and if the agent denies it, the claim of return after it is valid, and before it is valid, so there is benefit. Fourth, that he authorizes him for litigation with permission to acknowledge, so he becomes agent for both. Fifth, that he authorizes him without permission to acknowledge and deny, and there is difference among the later scholars. The summary: if the authorization was by the request of the opponent and he excepts acknowledgment connected, it is valid; disconnected, it is not. End. And it is valid to authorize for acknowledgment, and he does not become an acknowledger by it. This is in al-Nihayah. And in Munyat al-Mufti: if he excepted his acknowledgment and he acknowledged, he exits the agency. End.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe of the ruling on an agent's acknowledgment (iqrar) during litigation. The base ruling is that an agent appointed for litigation (khusumah) who acknowledges a claim on behalf of his principal is valid only if done before a judge; otherwise, it is invalid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) by istihsan. Abu Yusuf holds it valid absolutely, while Zufar invalidates it absolutely, which is the qiyas because acknowledgment is the opposite of litigation. The commentary explains the istihsan basis: the authorization is for a general response, and acknowledgment in court is considered litigation metaphorically. Further details include: the ruling is restricted to non-hadd and non-qisas cases due to doubt; the agent for compromise cannot acknowledge; if the principal excepts acknowledgment, there are differences; the five possible scenarios of authorization are summarized from al-Dhakhirah. Finally, it is confirmed that authorizing someone to make an acknowledgment is valid, but the agent does not become an acknowledger by the mere appointment.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment by a person of a right or claim against himself; in litigation, it is an admission of the opponent's claim.
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Litigation; a dispute between parties in a court setting, involving claim and defense.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; a person appointed by another (the principal) to perform a specific legal act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) In the presence of the principal.
B) Before a judge in court.
C) In writing.
D) After consulting the principal.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that an agent's acknowledgment in litigation is valid only in court; outside court it is invalid and the agent exits the agency.

The Legal Definition of Da'wa: Attribution in Dispute
Defines da'wa linguistically and then legally, excluding peaceful assertions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of da'wa and distinguish it from the linguistic definition by identifying the essential element of dispute.

Core Idea
A claim is only legally valid when made in a state of dispute; peaceful assertions are not claims.

The opening of Kitab al-Da'wa in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the foundational definition of a claim. This definition separates everyday speech from formal litigation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the rules of evidence and procedure that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal definition
Extract the author's exact words: 'هي إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه حالة المنازعة' (It is the attribution of something to oneself in a state of dispute). This is the legal (shar'i) definition.
The word 'حالة المنازعة' is the key qualifier that sets legal claims apart.
2
Understand the linguistic definitions
Review the citations from al-Misbah, al-Qamus, and al-Kafi. Notice that linguistically, da'wa can mean any assertion, invitation, or supplication. It does not require a dispute.
Linguistic da'wa is broader; legal da'wa is a subset.
3
Note the exclusion: peaceful attribution
The author explicitly excludes 'الإضافة حالة المسالمة' (attribution in a state of peace). Such utterances are valid linguistically but not legally as a claim in court.
This is the essential difference: a dispute (munaza'a) must exist.
4
Apply the example from al-Bazzaziyya
Consider the case: a man holds an item and says 'it is not mine' without any opponent. His negation is not a valid legal claim because there is no dispute. But if he later claims it for himself, that becomes a valid claim because a dispute arises.
This illustrates that a legal claim requires an opposing party or contested right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى هي إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه حالة المنازعة المدعي من إذا ترك ترك والمدعى عليه بخلافه زعم ويضم كتاب الدعوى مناسبتها ظاهرة لأن الوكيل بالخصومة وغيرها يحتاج إليها والكلام فيها في مواضع : الأول في معناها لغة ففي المصباح : ادعيته طلبته لنفسي والاسم الدعوى ودعوى فلان كذا أي قوله. والدعوة المرة وبعض العرب يؤنثها بالألف فيقول الدعوى. وقد يتضمن الادعاء معنى الإخبار فتدخل الباء جوازاً فيقال فلان يدعي بكرم فعاله أي يخبر بذلك عن نفسه . وجمع الدعوى الدعاوى بكسر الواو وفتحها، وبعضهم قال الفتح أولى، وبعضهم الكسر أولى، ومنهم من سوى بينهما ومثله الفتوى والفتاوى وتمامه فيه. وفي القاموس : ادعى بكذا له حقاً أو باطلاً والاسم الدعوة والدعاوة ويكسران والدعوة الحلف والدعاء إلى الطعام كالمدعاة، وبالكسر الادعاء في النسب ا هـ . وفي الكافي : يقال ادعى زيد على عمرو مالاً فزيد المدعي وعمرو المدعى عليه والمال المدعي والمدعى به خطاً والمصدر الادعاء افتعال من دعا والدعوى على فعلى اسم منه وألفها للتأنيث فلا تنون يقال دعوى باطلة وصحيحة وجمعها دعاوى بفتح الواو لا غير كفتوى وفتاوى والدعوى في الحرب أن يقول الناس يا لفلان وأما قوله تعالى دعواهم فيها سبحانك اللهم ﴾ [يونس : ١٠] فمعناها الدعاء وحقيقتها في جميع المواضع أن تدعو إلى نفسك أو لنفسك والدعوى بالفتح المدعاة وهي المأدبة ، وبالكسر في النسب والمدعي من يقصد ايجاب الحق على نفسه ولا حجة له ا هـ. الثاني في معناها شرعاً وهو ما أفاده المؤلف بقوله : (هي إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه حالة المنازعة) فخرج الإضافة حالة المسالمة فإنها دعوى لغة لا شرعاً، ونظيره ما في البزازية : عين في يد رجل يقول هو ليس لي وليس هناك منازع لا يصح نفيه، فلو ادعاه بعد ذلك لنفسه صح، وإن كان قوله : (فخرج عنه دعوى إيفاء الدين والإبراء منه قال بعض الفضلاء: رده العلامة المقدسي
Book of Claims. It is the attribution of something to oneself in a state of dispute. The claimant is the one who, if he leaves [the claim], it is left; and the defendant is opposite. It is said to be included. Book of Claims: Its relevance is clear because the agent for litigation and other matters needs it. The discussion contains topics: First: its meaning linguistically. In al-Misbah: 'I claimed him' means I sought him for myself; the noun is da'wa. 'Da'wa of so-and-so' means his statement. 'Da'wa' (with taa) is one time, and some Arabs make it feminine with alif, saying 'ad-da'wa'. The word claim may include the meaning of informing, so the letter baa may optionally enter; one says 'He claims with generosity of his actions' i.e., he informs about himself. The plural of da'wa is da'awa with kasra or fatha of the waw; some say fatha is better, some say kasra, and some equalize them; similarly fatwa and fatawa. End. In al-Qamus: 'He claimed something, rightfully or wrongfully', the noun is da'wa and da'awa, both with kasra; and da'wa means oath and invitation to food like al-mad'a; and with kasra, claim in lineage. End. In al-Kafi: It is said 'Zayd claimed property from Amr', so Zayd is the claimant (mudda'i) and Amr is the defendant (mudda'a alayhi), and the property is the claimed property (mudda'a) or the claimed-by (mudda'a bihi) incorrectly; the verbal noun is al-iddi'a from da'a; and da'wa is on the pattern of fu'la, a noun, its alif is for femininity so it is not declinable; one says 'a false da'wa' and 'a valid da'wa'; its plural is da'awa with fatha of the waw only, like fatwa and fatawa. Da'wa in war means people saying 'O so-and-so'. As for the saying of Allah Most High 'Their da'wa therein is 'Glory be to You, O Allah'' (Yunus:10), its meaning is supplication; its reality in all contexts is to call to yourself or for yourself. Da'wa with fatha means al-mad'a which is the feast; and with kasra, in lineage. And the claimant (mudda'i) is one who intends to establish a right against himself and has no proof. End. Second: its meaning legally, which is what the author expressed by saying: 'It is the attribution of something to oneself in a state of dispute.' So it excludes attribution in a state of peace; that is da'wa linguistically, not legally. An example from al-Bazzaziyya: An item in a man's hand; he says 'It is not mine' and there is no disputant; his denial is not valid. If he then claims it for himself, it is valid, even if... He said: 'Thus excluded is the claim of repayment of debt and discharge from it.' Some scholars said: al-Maqdisi responded to it.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This opening of the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) defines the term both linguistically and legally. The author begins by stating the legal definition: 'the attribution of something to oneself in a state of dispute' (إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه حالة المنازعة). This is the core definition around which the rest of the discussion revolves. The passage then delves into linguistic definitions from various lexicons. Al-Misbah explains that da'wa derives from calling or seeking something for oneself. Al-Qamus adds nuances including the meaning of oath and invitation. Al-Kafi gives a practical example: when Zayd claims property from Amr, Zayd is the claimant (mudda'i), Amr is the defendant (mudda'a alayhi), and the property is the subject of claim (mudda'a). The Qur'anic verse from Yunus (10:10) uses da'wa in the sense of supplication. The legal definition excludes peaceful assertions—such as simply stating something without a dispute—which are only linguistic claims. An example from al-Bazzaziyya illustrates: if a person holds an item and says 'it is not mine' without any opponent, his negation is not considered a valid claim; but if he later claims it for himself, it becomes a valid claim because now there is a dispute. The passage also mentions that claiming repayment of debt or discharge is excluded from the legal definition, as these are not true claims in the sense of initiating a dispute. This careful delineation sets the stage for the procedural rules that follow in the book.

Key Terms
الدعوى (al-da'wā): Linguistically: any assertion or claim. Legally: the attribution of something to oneself in a state of dispute.
المدعي (al-mudda'ī): The claimant or plaintiff; the person who makes a claim. The text defines him as 'one who, if he leaves [the claim], it is left,' meaning if he stops pursuing it, the matter ends because he is the one asserting a right.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'ā 'alayhi): The defendant; the person against whom the claim is made. The text says he is the opposite of the claimant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of da'wa?
A) Any assertion of right, whether disputed or not
B) The attribution of something to oneself in a state of dispute
C) A claim made in a peaceful setting
D) A denial of ownership

Key Takeaway
A legal claim in Hanafi fiqh requires a state of dispute; without it, the utterance is merely a linguistic assertion and carries no procedural effect.

Conditions for a Valid Claim (Da'wa)
Lists and explains the conditions that make a claim valid in court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) in Hanafi law and apply them to determine whether a given claim is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
A claim in court must meet eight conditions; if any is missing, the claim is invalid.

Before a claim can be heard in a Hanafi court, it must satisfy specific conditions. These ensure the claim is serious, possible, and properly presented. The following passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates these conditions, which you will learn to identify and apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Legal Nature
Recognize that da'wa is a procedural act in qada. Its validity depends on external conditions (shurut), not pillars (arkan).
Da'wa is a litigant's formal assertion of a right.
2
List the Conditions from the Text
Go through each condition stated: sanity, known claimant, possibility, claimant's own tongue, court session, presence of opponent, no contradiction, and claimant as litigant.
The text mentions eight conditions, though it groups the seventh as 'absence of contradiction' with an exception.
3
Understand Rationale and Exceptions
For each condition, ask why it is required. Note exceptions: e.g., the agent can claim with opponent's consent; contradiction is allowed in lineage and freedom claims.
Impossible claims are void; customarily impossible claims are likely not heard.
4
Apply to Scenarios
Given a factual scenario, check each condition. If any is missing, the claim is invalid (batil).
Example: claiming paternity of a child who cannot be born to you is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع في شروطها المصححة لها فمنها عقل المدعي والمدعى عليه، ومنها معلومية المدعي كما سيأتي بيانه ومنها كون المدعي مما يحتمل الثبوت فدعوى ما يستحيل وجوده باطلة كقوله لمن لا يولد مثله لمثله هذا ابني أو قال ذلك لمعروف النسب، ولم أر حكم المستحيل عادة كدعوى فقير أموالاً عظيمة على غني أنه غصبها منه، والظاهر عدم سماعها ثم كتبت بعد ذلك في آخر باب التحالف ما يفيده فليراجع . ومنها كونها بلسان المدعي فلا تصح بلسان وكيله إلا برضا خصمه عند الإمام إذا لم يكن به عذر. ومنها مجلس القضاء فلا تسمع هي والشهادة إلا بين يدي الحاكم. ومنها حضرة الخصم فلا يسمعان إلا على خصم حاضر إلا إذا التمس المدعي ذلك بالكتاب الحكمي للقضاء. ومنها عدم التناقض في الدعوى إلا في النسب والحرية وهو أن لا يسبق منه ما يناقض دعواه كما لو أقر بالملك له ثم ادعى الشراء منه قبله لا بعده أو مطلقاً، وهذه السبعة في البدائع. ومنها كون المدعي ملزماً على الخصم فلا تصح دعوى التوكيل على موكله الحاضر لإمكان عزله كما في العناية
Fourth: Regarding the conditions that validate it. Among them is the sanity of the claimant and the defendant. Among them is the knownness of the claimant as will come. Among them is that the claim be of something that can be proven; thus a claim of something impossible is invalid, like saying to one whose like cannot be born to him 'this is my son' or saying that to one of known lineage. I have not seen the ruling of something customarily impossible, like a poor man claiming great wealth from a rich man that he seized it from him; the apparent is that it is not heard. Then I wrote later at the end of the chapter of mutual swearing what benefits, so refer to it. Among them is that it be in the tongue of the claimant, so it is not valid in the tongue of his agent except with the consent of his opponent according to Imam Abu Hanifa if there is no excuse. Among them is the court session; it and the testimony are not heard except before the judge. Among them is the presence of the opponent; they are not heard except against a present opponent, unless the claimant requests that by judicial letter to the judge. Among them is the absence of contradiction in the claim except in lineage and freedom, meaning that there should not precede from him what contradicts his claim, like if he acknowledged ownership to him then claimed purchase from him before, not after, or absolutely. These seven are in al-Bada'i'. Among them is that the claimant must be a litigant against the opponent; thus a claim of agency against his present principal is not valid due to the possibility of his revocation as in al-'Inaya.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage lists the conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) in Hanafi law. The author enumerates several conditions: (1) Sanity of both parties; (2) The claimant must be known; (3) The claim must be of something possible – impossible claims like paternity of a child who cannot be born are void; customarily impossible claims are also likely not heard; (4) The claim must be made by the claimant himself, not through an agent, unless the opponent agrees (according to Abu Hanifa) unless there is an excuse; (5) The claim must be made in the court session (in the presence of the judge); (6) The opponent must be present unless a judicial letter is issued; (7) No contradiction in the claim, except in lineage and freedom – if the claimant previously acknowledged ownership to the opponent, he cannot subsequently claim purchase from him before that acknowledgment; (8) The claimant must be a litigant – a claim of agency against the principal is invalid because the principal can revoke. These conditions ensure the seriousness and feasibility of the claim.

Key Terms
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The claimant: the person who brings the claim.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant: the person against whom the claim is brought.
دعوى مستحيلة (da'wa mustahila): An impossible claim that is invalid because it cannot be proven.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction in the claim, which invalidates unless it relates to lineage or freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the conditions for a valid claim according to the text?
A) Sanity of claimant
B) Claim must be in Arabic
C) Claim must be possible
D) Presence of opponent

Key Takeaway
A valid da'wa requires eight conditions; any missing renders the claim invalid, but exceptions exist for lineage and freedom.
