The Primary Ruling of a Claim: Obligation to Respond
Defines the ruling of a claim as the defendant's obligation to respond and critiques the addition of attendance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary ruling of a valid claim according to the Hanafi school and explain why the obligation to attend is not included in the ruling.

Core Idea
The ruling of a claim is the obligation of the defendant to respond, not necessarily to attend court.

In this lesson, we examine the primary legal effect of a claim (da'wa) in Hanafi fiqh. The text from Bahr al-Raiq states clearly that the ruling is the obligation of the defendant to answer. Some commentators added the requirement to attend, but the author critically examines this expansion. We will clarify the distinction between a condition (shart) for the claim and its ruling (hukm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text says the ruling of a claim is that the defendant must respond.
This is the explicit statement: 'its ruling is the obligation of the defendant to respond.'
2
Note the additional claim by some scholars
Some commentators added 'obligation to attend' as part of the ruling.
This expansion is found in some commentaries on al-Kafi.
3
Analyze the objection
The author objects that presence is a condition (shart) for the claim, not a ruling that follows from it.
A condition must exist before the ruling can take effect.
4
Conclude the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi is that only responding is the hukm; attending is a separate requirement for the process.
The judge may require attendance, but it is not part of the ruling of the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في حكمها وهو وجوب الجواب على المدعى عليه، واقتصر عليه في الكافي وزاد الشارح وجوب الحضور على الخصم وفيه نظر، لأن حضوره شرطها كما قدمناه فكيف يكون وجوبه حكمها المتأخر عنها؟ وحاصله كما في منية المفتي أن المدعي إذا طلب من القاضي إحضار الخصم أحضره بمجرد الدعوى إن كان في المصر أو كان قريباً بحيث لو أجاب يبيت في منزله، وإن كان قيل يأمره بإقامة البينة على موافقة دعواه لإحضار خصمه والمستور في هذا يكفي، فإذا أقام يأمر إنساناً ليحضر خصمه، وقيل يحلفه القاضي؛ فإن نكل أقامه عن مجلسه، وإن حلف يأمر بإحضاره ا.هـ. وقدمنا في أدب القاضي حكم ما إذا امتنع عن الحضور وأجرة الرسول لإحضاره وما إذا اختفى في بيته وحكم الهجوم عليه
Fifth: Regarding its ruling, and it is the obligation of the defendant to respond. It was limited to that in al-Kafi, and the commentator added the obligation of the opponent to attend, but there is consideration, because his presence is a condition for it as we presented, so how can its obligation be its ruling after it? The summary is as in Munyat al-Mufti: When the claimant asks the judge to bring the opponent, he brings him by the mere claim if he is in the city or near such that if he answers he would spend the night in his home. If he is far, it is said that he orders him to establish evidence that his claim is valid to bring his opponent; the concealed in this suffices. When he establishes it, he orders a person to bring his opponent. It is said that the judge makes him swear; if he refuses (nukul), he removes him from his session; if he swears, he orders him to bring him. And we have presented in Adab al-Qadi the ruling when he refuses to attend, the wage of the messenger to bring him, and when he hides in his house, and the ruling of descending upon him.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal consequence of a claim (da'wa). The primary ruling is that the defendant is obligated to respond. Some scholars also argue that the defendant must appear in court, but the author notes a problem: the opponent's presence is a condition for hearing the claim, so how can it be a ruling of the claim itself? The author summarizes from Munyat al-Mufti the procedure: if the claimant requests the judge to summon the defendant, the judge will do so immediately if the defendant is in the city or nearby. If the defendant is far, the claimant may need to provide evidence that his claim is valid, and then the judge will send someone to bring the defendant. Alternatively, the judge may swear the claimant; if he refuses, he is dismissed; if he swears, the judge orders the defendant to be brought. The author refers to the earlier section on judicial conduct for details on non-attendance and forced entry.

Key Terms
حكم الدعوى (hukm al-da'wa): The ruling of a claim: the obligation of the defendant to respond.
وجوب الجواب (wujub al-jawab): Obligation to respond.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is considered the ruling (hukm) of a valid claim?
A) The defendant must appear in court
B) The defendant must respond to the claim
C) The claimant must provide evidence
D) The judge must issue a verdict

Key Takeaway
The primary ruling of a claim is the defendant's duty to answer; attendance is a separate condition, not part of the hukm.

Procedure for Summoning the Defendant in Hanafi Qada
Describes the judge's procedure for summoning a defendant based on distance and verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi procedure for bringing a defendant to court, distinguishing between nearby and distant defendants, and identify the role of evidence and oaths.

Core Idea
The judge summons a nearby defendant immediately; for a distant one, the claimant must first provide evidence of a valid claim.

This lesson details the practical steps a judge takes when a claimant requests the presence of a defendant. Based on the summary from Munyat al-Mufti, the procedure depends on the defendant's proximity. If the defendant is nearby, the judge brings him simply upon the claim. If far, the claimant must first establish evidence to justify the summons. This reflects the Hanafi concern for preventing harassment of innocent parties.

Guided Path
1
Determine the defendant's location
If the defendant is in the city or nearby (within a one-day return trip), the judge acts immediately.
Nearby means the defendant can return home the same night if summoned.
2
For a nearby defendant, summons directly
The judge brings him just by the mere claim, without further proof.
No additional evidence required.
3
For a distant defendant, require evidence
The claimant must establish evidence (bayyina) that his claim is valid.
The evidence need not be fully disclosed; it suffices to show validity.
4
After evidence, send a messenger
Once evidence is presented, the judge orders a person to bring the defendant.
Alternatively, the judge may administer an oath to the claimant; if sworn, the defendant is brought.
5
Consequences of refusal to swear
If the claimant refuses the oath, he is dismissed from the session.
If he swears, the judge orders the defendant's attendance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في حكمها وهو وجوب الجواب على المدعى عليه، واقتصر عليه في الكافي وزاد الشارح وجوب الحضور على الخصم وفيه نظر، لأن حضوره شرطها كما قدمناه فكيف يكون وجوبه حكمها المتأخر عنها؟ وحاصله كما في منية المفتي أن المدعي إذا طلب من القاضي إحضار الخصم أحضره بمجرد الدعوى إن كان في المصر أو كان قريباً بحيث لو أجاب يبيت في منزله، وإن كان قيل يأمره بإقامة البينة على موافقة دعواه لإحضار خصمه والمستور في هذا يكفي، فإذا أقام يأمر إنساناً ليحضر خصمه، وقيل يحلفه القاضي؛ فإن نكل أقامه عن مجلسه، وإن حلف يأمر بإحضاره ا.هـ. وقدمنا في أدب القاضي حكم ما إذا امتنع عن الحضور وأجرة الرسول لإحضاره وما إذا اختفى في بيته وحكم الهجوم عليه
Fifth: Regarding its ruling, and it is the obligation of the defendant to respond. It was limited to that in al-Kafi, and the commentator added the obligation of the opponent to attend, but there is consideration, because his presence is a condition for it as we presented, so how can its obligation be its ruling after it? The author refers to the earlier section on judicial conduct for details on non-attendance and forced entry.

Key Terms
إقامة البينة (iqamat al-bayyina): Establishing evidence to prove the validity of the claim.
المستور (al-mastur): The concealed evidence; it suffices even if not openly displayed.
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal to take an oath.

Key Takeaway

The Plausibility Requirement: Impossibility Bars a Valid Claim
Defining conditions for a claim to be heard by a judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a claim is barred due to rational or habitual impossibility, and apply the rule that such claims are dismissed without summoning the defendant.

Core Idea
A claim that is rationally or habitually impossible is rejected without calling the defendant.

In Islamic procedural law, not every claim must be heard by a judge. A fundamental condition is that the subject matter must be plausible—capable of being proven. This lesson examines two types of impossibility—rational and habitual—that bar a claim from even being entertained, saving judicial time and preventing frivolous or clearly false lawsuits.

Guided Path
1
1. State the condition for a valid claim
The subject matter (المدعى به) must be possible to prove (يحتمل الثبوت). That means it cannot be impossible, either rationally or habitually.
This is the first and most fundamental filter before a judge hears any case.
2
2. Distinguish rational from habitual impossibility
Rational impossibility (مستحيل عقلاً) goes against reason—like claiming to fly without any means. Habitual impossibility (مستحيل عادة) is something that does not normally happen, though it may be possible in rare cases—like a known pauper suddenly claiming to have lent a fortune.
The text says rational impossibility yields 'certain falsehood', while habitual yields 'apparent falsehood'.
3
3. Apply the rule to the given example
A man known for poverty and receiving zakat claims he lent someone 100,000 gold dinars cash at once. This is habitually impossible. The judge disregards the claim (لا يلتفت إليها) and does not ask the defendant to respond.
The judge does not even call the defendant; the case is dismissed immediately.
4
4. Understand the procedural consequence of a valid claim
If the claim is plausible (i.e., not impossible), the judge must then summon the defendant and demand a yes-or-no answer. The defendant's obligation to appear arises only after the claim is deemed valid.
Al-Zayla'i clarifies that the judge does not summon the defendant merely upon the plaintiff's request; he first hears the claim and checks its validity.
5
5. Note the transmission status of the ruling
The text notes that the rule dismissing habitually impossible claims is not based on an explicit transmission from early scholars (لم يستند إلى نقل عن المشايخ). This suggests it may be based on general legal reasoning (ra'y) or custom.
This is important for understanding the epistemological weight of the ruling—it is not from the nusus but from considered opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروط صحة الدعوى أن يكون المدعى به مما يحتمل الثبوت بأن لا يكون مستحيلاً عقلاً أو عادة فإن الدعوى والحال ما ذكر ظاهرة الكذب في المستحيل العادي يقينية الكذب في المستحيل العقلي. مثال الدعوى بالمستحيل العادي دعوى من هو معروف بالفقر والحاجة وهو أن يأخذ الزكاة من على آخر أنه أقرضه مائة ألف دينار ذهباً نقداً دفعة واحدة وأنه تصرف فيها بنفسه ويطالبه برد بدلها، فمثل هذه الدعوى لا يلتفت إليها القاضي ولا يسأل المدعى عليه عن جوابها اهـ . لكنه لم يستند في منع دعوى المستحيل العادي إلى نقل عن المشايخ ؛ كذا في المنح . قوله : (وزاد الشارح وجوب الحضور على الخصم الخ) عبارة الزيلعي : وحكمها وجوب الحواب على الخصم إذا صحت ويترتب على صحتها وجوب إحضار الخصم والمطالبة بالجواب بلا أو نعم وإقامة البينة أو اليمين إذا أنكر اهـ. فليس في كلام الزيلعي ما يفيد أنه جعل وجوب الحضور حكماً، وغاية ما استفيد من كلامه أن القاضي لا يحضره بمجرد طلب المدعي بل بعد سماعه دعواه فإن رآها صحيحة أحضره لطلب وإلا فلا فتدبر . أبو السعود.
Conditions for the validity of a lawsuit: That the subject matter of the claim is capable of being proven, meaning it must not be impossible rationally or habitually. For the lawsuit, given the stated condition, is apparently false in the case of habitual impossibility and certainly false in rational impossibility. An example of a claim that is habitually impossible: a claim by someone known for poverty and need, who takes zakat, against another that he lent him one hundred thousand gold dinars cash at once, and he disposed of them himself, and demands their return. Such a claim is not entertained by the judge, and he does not ask the defendant for a response, etc. But this prohibition of a habitually impossible claim is not based on a transmission from the early scholars; so in al-Munah. His saying: (And the commentator added the obligation of the defendant's presence, etc.) Al-Zayla'i's expression: Its ruling is the obligation of the defendant to respond if it is valid, and upon its validity follows the obligation of bringing the defendant, demanding a yes-or-no answer, and presenting evidence or oath if he denies, etc. So in al-Zayla'i's words, there is nothing indicating he made attendance obligatory as a ruling; the most derived from his words is that the judge does not bring him merely upon the plaintiff's request, but after hearing his claim; if he sees it as valid, he brings him upon request, otherwise not. So reflect. Abu al-Su'ud.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage outlines the first condition for a valid lawsuit: the subject matter must be plausible. Two types of impossibility are recognized: rational (e.g., claiming to fly without means) and habitual (e.g., a very poor man claiming to have lent a huge sum). Such claims are rejected outright; the judge does not even call the defendant. The commentator notes that this ruling lacks explicit support from early authorities. Then a discussion on the ruling of a valid suit: upon hearing a valid claim, the judge must summon the defendant, who is obliged to respond. The commentator clarifies that the defendant's presence is not an initial obligation but is consequential to the validity of the claim.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A claim or lawsuit in court.
مستحيل عقلاً (mustahil 'aqlan): Intrinsically impossible, contrary to reason.
مستحيل عادة (mustahil 'ādatan): Habitually impossible; something that does not occur normally.
إحضار الخصم (ihdar al-khasm): Bringing the defendant to court.
جواب (jawab): The defendant's plea or response.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a known pauper claims that he lent someone 100,000 gold dinars?
A) The judge hears the case and asks for witnesses.
B) The judge asks the defendant to respond.
C) The judge disregards the claim and does not call the defendant.
D) The judge presumes the claim is true.

Key Takeaway
A claim that is rationally or habitually impossible is dismissed without hearing the defendant; only plausible claims trigger the obligation to summon and respond.

Defining Plaintiff and Defendant in Hanafi Law
Explaining the definition of plaintiff and defendant in Hanafi law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of plaintiff and defendant and distinguish them based on the criterion of compulsion to continue litigation, applying the distinction in form vs. meaning.

Core Idea
The plaintiff is not forced to continue; the defendant is.

Knowing who is the plaintiff and who is the defendant is fundamental to courtroom procedure in Islamic law. The Hanafi school offers multiple definitions, but the core distinction lies in who is compelled to continue the lawsuit if they abandon it. This lesson unpacks these definitions and shows how they apply even when the surface roles seem reversed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition from the matn
Read the opening statement: the plaintiff is not forced to continue if he abandons; the defendant is forced. This is the primary criterion.
This definition is simple but may not cover all cases.
2
Examine alternative definitions
The text presents two other definitions: one based on need for proof (plaintiff needs evidence, defendant's word suffices) and another based on what is apparent (plaintiff seeks the non-apparent, defendant holds the apparent). Understand how these relate to the base definition.
These definitions are not contradictory but highlight different aspects.
3
Apply the example of the depositor
Consider the scenario: a depositor claims he returned the deposit. In form, he is a plaintiff (seeking to establish return), but in meaning he is a defendant (denying liability). The Hanafi ruling: his statement is accepted with an oath, and he is forced to litigate. This shows that the real classification follows meaning, not form.
This example illustrates the principle of al-'ibrah lil-ma'ani la lil-suwar.
4
Note the nuance about the defendant's choice of judge
The passage concludes that the defendant is forced to original litigation, but this does not contradict Muhammad's statement that the defendant can choose the judge. This nuance is important for procedural understanding.
This point will be elaborated later; for now, recognize that force to litigate does not mean loss of all procedural rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (المدعي من إذا ترك ترك والمدعى عليه بخلافه) أي المدعي من لا يجبر على الخصومة إذا تركها والمدعى عليه من يجبر على الخصومة إذا تركها ومعرفة الفرق بينهما من أهم ما يبتنى عليه مسائل الدعوى وقد اختلفت عبارات المشايخ فيه فمنها ما في الكتاب وهو حد عام صحيح، وقيل المدعي من لا يستحق إلا بحجة كالخارج، والمدعى عليه من يكون مستحقاً بقوله من غير حجة كذي اليد، وقيل المدعي من يلتمس غير الظاهر، والمدعى عليه من يتمسك بالظاهر. وقال محمد في الأصل: المدعى عليه هو المنكر وهذا صحيح لكن الشأن في معرفته والترجيح بالفقه عند الحذاق من أصحابنا لأن الاعتبار للمعاني دون الصور فإن المودع إذا قال رددت الوديعة فالقول قوله من اليمين وإن كان مدعياً للرد صورة لأنه ينكر الضمان؛ كذا في الهداية وحاصله أن المدعي يدعي فراغ ذمته عن الضمان ولهذا تقبل بينته اعتباراً للصورة ويجبر على الخصومة ويحلف اعتباراً للمعنى؛ كذا في الكافي. وفي المجتبى : الصحيح ما في الكتاب والمراد أن المدعى عليه يجبر على أصل الخصومة، ولا ينافيه قول محمد إن الخيار للمدعى عليه في تعيين القاضي كما لا يخفى.
His statement: (The plaintiff is the one who if he abandons, abandons; and the defendant is the opposite) i.e. the plaintiff is the one who is not forced to litigation if he abandons it, and the defendant is forced to litigation if he abandons it. Knowing the difference between them is among the most important foundations of the cases of claims. The statements of the shaykhs have differed on it. Among them is what is in the book, and it is a correct general definition. And it is said: the plaintiff is the one who does not deserve (the claim) except with proof, like the outsider (al-kharij); and the defendant is the one who is deserving by his statement without proof, like the possessor (dhu al-yad). And it is said: the plaintiff is the one who seeks the non-apparent, and the defendant is the one who holds on to the apparent. And Muhammad said in al-Asl: the defendant is the denier (al-munkir). This is correct, but the issue is in knowing it; and the preference is by jurisprudence according to the intelligent among our companions, because consideration is for meanings, not forms. For if the depositor says, 'I returned the deposit,' then his statement is taken with an oath, even though he is a plaintiff in form regarding the return, because he is denying liability. Thus in al-Hidayah. Its gist is that the plaintiff claims his liability is free from guarantee; therefore his evidence is accepted considering form, and he is forced to litigation and sworn considering meaning. Thus in al-Kafi. And in al-Mujtaba: the correct is what is in the book, and the intent is that the defendant is forced to the original litigation, and it does not contradict Muhammad's statement that the defendant has the choice in appointing the judge, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the plaintiff and defendant. It opens with the definition from the matn: the plaintiff is not forced to continue if he abandons the lawsuit, while the defendant is forced. Then it presents other definitions from scholars: one based on the need for evidence (plaintiff needs proof, defendant's word suffices), another based on what is apparent. It then quotes Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Asl that the defendant is the denier. The commentary emphasizes that the real distinction is based on meaning, not form. The example given is of a depositor who claims he returned the deposit: he is a plaintiff in form but in meaning he is denying liability, so his statement is accepted with an oath. The passage concludes by noting that the defendant is forced to litigation, but this does not mean he cannot choose the judge (as will be discussed later).

Key Terms
المدعي (al-mudda‘ī): The plaintiff: the one who does not need to be forced to continue the lawsuit if he abandons it; he is the one who seeks a claim against another.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda‘ā ‘alayh): The defendant: the one who is forced to continue the lawsuit if he abandons it; he is the one against whom the claim is made.
الخارج (al-khārij): The outsider: one who does not possess the property in question, so he must bring evidence to establish his claim.
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The possessor: one who holds the property, so his statement is accepted regarding his possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of the defendant (المدعى عليه) in the matn?
A) The one who is forced to litigation if he abandons it.
B) The one who is not forced to litigation if he abandons it.
C) The one who brings the claim.
D) The one who has the burden of proof.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the key distinction between plaintiff and defendant is whether the party is compelled to continue the litigation if they abandon it.

A Third Party Taking Debt Without Permission: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on third party debt collection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on a third party taking the debt item from the debtor without permission and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
A third party who takes the debt item without permission is an assistant, not a usurper; the debt is settled immediately.

In Hanafi law, when a creditor cannot collect directly, a third party may intervene. But what is the legal status of that third party? This passage records a disagreement between two Hanafi authorities: Ibn Salamah and Nasir ibn Yahya. Ibn Salamah considers the taker a usurper liable until the debtor consents, while Nasir views him as an assistant whose action immediately settles the debt. The fatwa follows Nasir's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
The act is: a third party takes from the debtor (without permission) the exact item owed and gives it to the creditor. This is the core scenario.
2
State Ibn Salamah's position
Ibn Salamah says both the taker and the debtor are usurpers (ghāṣib). The transfer only becomes a valid set-off if the debtor later guarantees (i.e., gives permission). Until then, the taker is liable.
3
State Nasir ibn Yahya's position
Nasir says the taker is not a usurper; rather, he is an assistant (muʿīn) to the debtor. The debt is immediately settled (qiṣāṣ), and the taker is not liable for the property.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The text states 'wa bihi yufatā' (and with it the fatwa is given), referring to Nasir's view. This is the operative ruling.
5
Determine the practical difference
Under Ibn Salamah, the taker is liable as a usurper if the property perishes before the debtor's consent. Under Nasir, no liability, and the debt is extinguished immediately.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قياساً. ولو أخذ من الغريم غيره ودفعه إلى الدائن قال ابن سلمة : هو والغريم غاصب، فإن قصاصاً بدينه، وإن ضمن الغريم صار قصاصاً. وقال نصير بن يحيى : ضمن الآخذ لم يصر صار قصاصاً بدينه والآخذ معين له و به يفتي . ولو غصب جنس الدين من المديون فغصبه منه المديون فالمختار هنا قول ابن سلمة ا.هـ. وظاهر قول أصحابنا أن له الأخذ من جنسه مقراً كان أو منكراً، له بينة أو لا، ولم أر حكم ما إذا لم يتوصل إليه إلا بكسر الباب ونقب الجدار وينبغي أن له ذلك حيث لا يمكنه الأخذ بالحاكم، وإذا أخذ غير الجنس بغير إذنه فتلف في يده ضمنه ضمان الرهن كما في غصب البزازية، ولم أر حكم ما إذا ظفر بمال مديون مديونه والجنس واحد فيهما، وينبغي أن يجوز الثامن في دليلها الكتاب والسنة والإجماع وهي شهيرة. والتاسع في أنواعها العاشر في وجوده دفعها وسيأتيان.
By analogy. And if he took from the debtor other than him (i.e., a different person) and gave it to the creditor, Ibn Salamah said: He and the debtor are usurpers. Then it becomes a set-off for his debt, and if the debtor guarantees, it becomes a set-off. And Nasir ibn Yahya said: The taker is not liable; it becomes a set-off for his debt, and the taker is his assistant, and that is the fatwa. And if he seized the same type of debt from the debtor, then the debtor seized it from him, the chosen view here is Ibn Salamah's statement. And the apparent statement of our companions is that he may take from the same type, whether the debtor admits or denies, whether he has evidence or not. And I have not seen the ruling if he cannot reach it except by breaking the door or digging the wall, and it is appropriate that he may do so where he cannot take through the judge. And if he takes other than the type without his permission and it perishes in his hand, he is liable with the liability of a pledge (damān al-rahn), as in Ghāsb al-Bazzāziyyah. And I have not seen the ruling if he comes across the property of his debtor's debtor and the type is the same in both, and it is appropriate that it is permissible. The eighth (principle) in its proof is the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma‘, which are well-known. And the ninth and tenth will come later.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a third party takes payment from a debtor without the debtor's permission and gives it to the creditor. Two positions are presented: Ibn Salamah considers both the taker and the debtor as usurpers, but if the debtor later agrees (guarantees), it becomes a valid set-off. Nasir ibn Yahya, whose view is the fatwa, says the taker is not a usurper but an assistant, and the debt is settled. The passage then extends this to situations where the debtor seizes back the same type from the taker—the chosen view is Ibn Salamah's. The dominant Hanafi view allows taking the same type of goods regardless of whether the debtor admits or denies the debt. If force is needed to access the goods (e.g., breaking a door), it is permissible if the judge cannot enforce. However, taking a different type of goods without permission incurs liability as if it were a pawn. The passage ends by noting that the full proofs (Book, Sunnah, Ijma‘) will follow later.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper: one who takes another's property wrongfully without permission.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Set-off: here meaning the debt is cancelled by the transfer of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi view regarding the third party who takes the debt without permission?
A) He is a usurper and liable for the property.
B) He is an assistant and not liable; the debt is settled.
C) He is a guarantor of the debt.
D) He is a trustee until the debtor agrees.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school is that a third party who takes the debt item from the debtor without permission is an assistant, not a usurper, and the debt is settled immediately.

Extensions: Taking by Force, Different Type, and Property of the Debtor's Debtor
Extension of Hanafi ruling on debt collection to force, different goods, and subdebt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on taking debt without permission to scenarios involving force, different types of goods, and subdebt.

Core Idea
Taking the same type is always allowed; force is permitted if the judge cannot act; taking a different type incurs pawn-like liability.

The passage extends the earlier core dispute to further cases. It clarifies that the creditor may take the same type of goods regardless of the debtor's admission, and that force (breaking doors, digging walls) is permissible when judicial enforcement is unavailable. However, taking a different type without permission creates strict liability akin to a pawn. The text also suggests that taking from the debtor's debtor is permissible if the types match.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of taking the same type
The apparent view of the Hanafi companions: the creditor may take the same type of goods from the debtor whether the debtor admits or denies, with or without evidence. This is the default permission.
2
Analyze the use of force
The commentator notes he did not see a prior ruling on breaking doors or walls. He extrapolates that it is permissible if the judge cannot enforce the debt. This is an extension by necessity.
3
Analyze taking a different type
If the taker takes a different type of goods and they perish in his hand, he is liable with the liability of a pawn (ḍamān al-rahn). This is a ruling of strict liability, analogous to a pawn but with the key difference that the taker acts without permission.
4
Analyze taking from the debtor's debtor
If the creditor finds property belonging to his debtor's debtor (i.e., someone who owes a debt to his debtor) and the type is the same, it is permissible to take it. This is an analogy to the original permission, though the commentator notes he has not seen a direct ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قياساً. ولو أخذ من الغريم غيره ودفعه إلى الدائن قال ابن سلمة : هو والغريم غاصب، فإن قصاصاً بدينه، وإن ضمن الغريم صار قصاصاً. وقال نصير بن يحيى : ضمن الآخذ لم يصر صار قصاصاً بدينه والآخذ معين له و به يفتي . ولو غصب جنس الدين من المديون فغصبه منه المديون فالمختار هنا قول ابن سلمة ا.هـ. وظاهر قول أصحابنا أن له الأخذ من جنسه مقراً كان أو منكراً، له بينة أو لا، ولم أر حكم ما إذا لم يتوصل إليه إلا بكسر الباب ونقب الجدار وينبغي أن له ذلك حيث لا يمكنه الأخذ بالحاكم، وإذا أخذ غير الجنس بغير إذنه فتلف في يده ضمنه ضمان الرهن كما في غصب البزازية، ولم أر حكم ما إذا ظفر بمال مديون مديونه والجنس واحد فيهما، وينبغي أن يجوز الثامن في دليلها الكتاب والسنة والإجماع وهي شهيرة. والتاسع في أنواعها العاشر في وجوده دفعها وسيأتيان.
By analogy. And if he took from the debtor other than him (i.e., a different person) and gave it to the creditor, Ibn Salamah said: He and the debtor are usurpers. Then it becomes a set-off for his debt, and if the debtor guarantees, it becomes a set-off. And Nasir ibn Yahya said: The taker is not liable; it becomes a set-off for his debt, and the taker is his assistant, and that is the fatwa. And if he seized the same type of debt from the debtor, then the debtor seized it from him, the chosen view here is Ibn Salamah's statement. And the apparent statement of our companions is that he may take from the same type, whether the debtor admits or denies, whether he has evidence or not. And I have not seen the ruling if he cannot reach it except by breaking the door or digging the wall, and it is appropriate that he may do so where he cannot take through the judge. And if he takes other than the type without his permission and it perishes in his hand, he is liable with the liability of a pledge (damān al-rahn), as in Ghāsb al-Bazzāziyyah. And I have not seen the ruling if he comes across the property of his debtor's debtor and the type is the same in both, and it is appropriate that it is permissible. The eighth (principle) in its proof is the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma‘, which are well-known. And the ninth and tenth will come later.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a third party takes payment from a debtor without the debtor's permission and gives it to the creditor. Two positions are presented: Ibn Salamah considers both the taker and the debtor as usurpers, but if the debtor later agrees (guarantees), it becomes a valid set-off. Nasir ibn Yahya, whose view is the fatwa, says the taker is not a usurper but an assistant, and the debt is settled. The passage then extends this to situations where the debtor seizes back the same type from the taker—the chosen view is Ibn Salamah's. The dominant Hanafi view allows taking the same type of goods regardless of whether the debtor admits or denies the debt. If force is needed to access the goods (e.g., breaking a door), it is permissible if the judge cannot enforce. However, taking a different type of goods without permission incurs liability as if it were a pawn. The passage ends by noting that the full proofs (Book, Sunnah, Ijma‘) will follow later.

Key Terms
ضمان الرهن (ḍamān al-rahn): Liability of a pledge: the taker is liable for the property if it perishes, similar to a pawned item.
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper: one who takes another's property wrongfully without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what liability does a person incur if he takes a different type of goods from the debtor without permission and they perish?
A) No liability at all.
B) Liability as a pawn (ḍamān al-rahn).
C) Liability as a usurper (ghāṣib).
D) Liability as a guarantor (ḍāmin).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, while taking the same type of debt is broadly permitted, force is only allowed as a last resort, and taking a different type creates strict liability akin to a pawn.

Jurisdiction Dispute with Multiple Judges
Intra-Hanafi dispute with expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on jurisdiction when plaintiff and defendant are from different districts and apply it to cases with multiple judges from the same or different madhhabs.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the defendant chooses the judge, not the plaintiff.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on which judge hears a case when the two parties belong to different judicial districts. The ruling, which gives the choice to the defendant, is further extended by the commentator to cases involving judges from different madhhabs. Mastering this ruling is essential for understanding Hanafi procedural law and its emphasis on protecting the defendant from bias.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two judges, two districts, a plaintiff and a defendant from different districts. The plaintiff wants his judge, the defendant objects.
The scenario is from al-Khāniyyah.
2
State both positions
Abu Yusuf: the plaintiff's judge has jurisdiction. Muhammad: the defendant's judge has jurisdiction.
3
Analyze the rationales
Abu Yusuf: plaintiff is the initiator (munshi’). Muhammad: defendant is the defender (dāfi‘) and should not be forced away from his own judge.
Rationales are from al-Muhīt.
4
Identify the tarjih
Muhammad’s view is the mufta bihi, as stated in al-Bazzāziyyah and confirmed by al-Ramli.
The phrase 'وعليه الفتوى' indicates the operative position.
5
Apply the expansion
The same rule applies when judges belong to different madhhabs (e.g., Shafi‘i and Maliki) as in Cairo. The defendant still chooses.
Al-Ramli gives his own fatwa on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : ولو كان في البلدة قاضيان كل واحد منهما في محلة على حدة فوقعت الخصومة بين رجلين أحدهما من محلة والآخر من محلة أخرى والمدعي يريد أن يخاصمه إلى قاضي محلته والآخر يأبى ذلك اختلف فيها أبو يوسف ومحمد والصحيح أن العبرة لمكان المدعى عليه. وكذا لو كان أحدهما من أهل العسكر والآخر من أهل البلدة فأراد العسكري أن يخاصمه إلى قاضي العسكر فهو على هذا ا هـ. وعلله في المحيط بأن أبا يوسف يقول إن المدعي منشئ للخصومة فيعتبر قاضيه، ومحمد يقول إن المدعى عليه دافع لها وفي البزازية قاضيان في مصر طلب كل واحد منهما أن يذهب إلى قاض فالخيار للمدعى عليه عند محمد وعليه الفتوى ا هـ. وهو بإطلاقه شامل كما إذا أراد المدعي قاضي محلة المدعى عليه وأراد المدعي عليه قاضي محلة المدعي، وما إذا تعدد القضاة في المذاهب الأربعة وكثروا كما في القاهرة فأراد المدعي قاضياً شافعياً مثلاً وأراد الآخر مالكياً مثلاً ولم يكونا من محلتهما فإن الخيار للمدعى عليه وهذا هو الظاهر وبه أفتيت مراراً كثيرة.
And in al-Khāniyyah: And if in a town there are two judges, each in a separate district, and a dispute occurs between two men, one from one district and the other from another district, and the plaintiff wants to litigate before the judge of his district and the other refuses, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differed on this. The correct is that the consideration is for the location of the defendant. Likewise, if one is from the military and the other from the town, and the military man wants to litigate before the military judge, the ruling is the same. He (the commentator) rationalized in al-Muhīt that Abu Yusuf says the plaintiff initiates the litigation, so his judge is considered; Muhammad says the defendant is the one defending against it. And in al-Bazzāziyyah: Two judges in a city, each one of the two disputants asks to go to a judge, the choice is for the defendant according to Muhammad, and the fatwa is on it. It is general, including when the plaintiff wants the judge of the defendant's district and the defendant wants the judge of the plaintiff's district, and when there are multiple judges from the four madhhabs (e.g., in Cairo), the plaintiff wants a Shafi‘i judge and the other wants a Maliki judge, and they are not from their respective districts, the choice is for the defendant. This is the apparent view, and I have given fatwa many times.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage addresses the conflict of jurisdiction when there are multiple judges in different districts. It presents a dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad. Abu Yusuf considers the plaintiff as the initiator of the lawsuit, so the judge of the plaintiff's district should hear the case. Muhammad considers the defendant as the one being forced to defend, so he should choose the judge to protect his interests. The passage concludes that the correct view is to consider the defendant's location, and the fatwa is on Muhammad's opinion. The commentator, al-Ramli, extends this to situations where judges belong to different madhhabs, affirming that the defendant has the right to choose the judge.

Key Terms
قاضي (qāḍī): Judge: the person authorized to adjudicate disputes.
محلة (maḥalla): District or neighborhood: the jurisdiction of a particular judge.
الخيار للمدعى عليه (al-khiyār lil-mudda‘ā ‘alayh): The choice is for the defendant: the defendant has the right to choose the judge in a multi-judge scenario.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding which judge should hear a case when plaintiff and defendant are from different districts, whose view is the mufta bihi?
A) Abu Yusuf's view: the plaintiff's judge.
B) Muhammad's view: the defendant's judge.
C) Both are equally valid.
D) It depends on the value of the lawsuit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi judicial procedure, the defendant has the right to choose the judge when multiple judges exist, protecting him from potential bias.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Choosing the Judge: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad on Plaintiff and Defendant
Intra-Hanafi dispute on jurisdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding which judge has jurisdiction when the plaintiff and defendant are from different localities and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The defendant chooses the judge because he is the defender and the principle of innocence protects him.

When two parties live in different districts, a dispute arises: which judge should hear the case? Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the two leading students of Abu Hanifa, gave conflicting answers. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the official fatwa position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Abu Yusuf says the plaintiff's judge should hear the case; Muhammad says the defendant's judge should hear it.
Both views are based on the role of each party in litigation.
2
Understand the reasoning
Abu Yusuf: the plaintiff initiates the case, so his judge is appropriate. Muhammad: the defendant defends, and the principle of innocence (istishab al-bara'ah) gives him the right to choose a judge who is not biased against him.
Muhammad's reasoning emphasizes protecting the defendant from potential injustice.
3
Note the mufta bihi
According to al-Bazzaziyya, the fatwa is on Muhammad's view: the defendant chooses the judge.
This is the relied-upon position for restricted jurisdiction.
4
Recognize the practical consequence
The defendant has the right to select the forum, preventing the plaintiff from forum-shopping.
This applies when each judge has authority only over his own quarter.

الخ) قال الرملي : عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله المدعي إذا ترك ترك فهو منشئ فيتخير إن شاء أنشأ الخصومة عند قاضي محلته، وإن شاء أنشأها عند محلة خصمه ومحمد رحمه الله المدعى عليه دافع له والدافع يطلب سلامة نفسه عليه فتواه بل على ما قيده من أن كلاً. المتداعيين يطلب المحاكمة عند قاضي محلته، وعلى تقدير أن في نسخته إطلاقاً فهو محمول على التقييد يعني يريد من المصرح به في العمادية والخانية ،وغيرهما فإن الذي ولاه لقاضي العسكر على غير الجندي اهـ. فهذا دليل واضح على أن المعتبر هو ا الولاية فالسلطان لما ولى قاضياً ببلدة أو محلة مخصوصة خصه بأهل تلك البلدة فليس له أن يحكم على غيرهم، ومعلوم أن قاضي مصر لما ولي لم يخص حكمه بأهل مصر بل بمن هو فيها من مصري وشامي وحلبي وغيرهم فينبغي التعويل على قول أبي يوسف لموافقته لتعريف المدعي والمدعى عليه، وإن ما ذكره المتأخر يعني العلامة زين وجه له. حموي عن المقدسي ؛ كذا في ولاية لقاضي العسكر على غير الجندي». أما إذا كان كل منهما مأذوناً بالحكم على أي من حضر عنده فينبغي تصحيح قول أبي يوسف لأن المدعي هو الذي له الخصومة فيطلبها عند أي قاض أراد، ولا يخفى أن قضاة مصر والشام إذنهم عام وهذا كلام متجه . ونقل مثله في الدر المختار عن خط صاحب التنوير على هامش البزازية حيث قال : وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا كان كل قاض على محلة على حدة، أما إذا كان في المصر حنفي وشافعي ومالكي وحنبلي في مجلس واحد والولاية واحدة فلا ينبغي أن يقع
A quarter and another from another quarter, and the plaintiff wants to bring him to the judge of his quarter and the other refuses that. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differed on this, and the correct view is that consideration is given to the place of the defendant. Likewise, if one of them is from the military and the other from the town, and the military person wants to bring him to the military judge, it is according to this. He explained in al-Muhit that Abu Yusuf says the plaintiff initiates the litigation, so his judge is considered; Muhammad says the defendant defends against it. In al-Bazzaziyya: two judges in a city, each one wants to go to a judge; the choice is for the defendant according to Muhammad, and upon it is the fatwa. This includes, as when the plaintiff wants the judge of the defendant's quarter and the defendant wants the judge of the plaintiff's quarter, and when there are many judges from the four schools as in Cairo, and the plaintiff wants a Shafi'i judge and the other wants a Maliki, and they are not from their quarters, then the choice is for the defendant. This is the apparent view, and I have given fatwa upon it many times. His statement: (And he explained it in al-Muhit...): al-Ramli said: According to Abu Yusuf, the plaintiff, if he leaves, leaves; he is the initiator, so he may choose to initiate the litigation before the judge of his quarter or before the judge of his opponent. Muhammad: the defendant is the defender, and the defender seeks safety for himself, and the principle is the innocence of his liability, so taking him to someone who refuses due to a suspicion or doubt that may cause him to prove what was not established against him, is more appropriate because he wants to defend himself, and his opponent is obligating him to respond to the claim. And whoever seeks safety is more deserving of consideration than one who seeks its opposite. His statement: (This is the apparent view and I have given fatwa upon it many times) was refuted by al-Maqdisi as being incorrect: first, the famous copies of al-Bazzaziyya are not as absolute as he claimed and based his fatwa upon, but rather as restricted by that each of the two litigants requests trial before the judge of his quarter; and if his copy is absolute, it is interpreted as restricted, meaning what is expressly stated in al-Imadiyya and al-Khaniyya and others: indeed, the one who was appointed specifically to that town or that quarter, and therefore he said... In Jami' al-Fusulayn: two strangers disputed before the judge of a town; his judgment is valid as arbitration. I say: There is no need for this because judges are delegated generally to judge over everyone in their town or village that they are appointed to. Hence the statement in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya after mentioning the issue restricted as we mentioned: Likewise, if one is from the military and the other from the town, and the military person wants to bring him to the military judge, it is according to this, and the military judge has no authority over non-military. This is clear evidence that consideration is given to authority. When the Sultan appoints a judge to a particular town or quarter, he restricts him to the people of that town, so he cannot judge over others. It is known that the judge of Cairo, when appointed, is not restricted to the people of Cairo but to those within it, whether Egyptian, Syrian, Aleppan, etc. So one should rely on the view of Abu Yusuf because it agrees with the definition of plaintiff and defendant. And what the later scholar (meaning al-Allamah Zayn) said is supported. Humawi from al-Maqdisi; as in the margin of Abu al-Su'ud. I say: The gist is that what they mentioned of correcting Muhammad's view that consideration is given to the place of the defendant is only when there are two judges each in a quarter and each is commanded to judge only the people of his quarter, as evidence by al-Imadi's statement: 'the military judge has no authority over non-military.' But when each is authorized to judge anyone who appears before him, then the view of Abu Yusuf should be corrected because the plaintiff is the one who has the litigation and seeks it before whichever judge he wants, and it is no secret that the judges of Egypt and Syria have general authorization, and this is sound. A similar view is transmitted in al-Durr al-Mukhtar from the handwriting of the author of al-Tanwir on the margin of al-Bazzaziyya: this disagreement is when each judge is over a separate quarter; but when in the city there is a Hanafi, Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali in one council and authority is one, then this disagreement should not occur.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding which judge has jurisdiction when the plaintiff and defendant live in different localities. The author opens by stating the positions: Abu Yusuf considers the plaintiff's judge because the plaintiff initiates the case, while Muhammad considers the defendant's judge because the defendant is the one defending. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's view, giving the defendant the choice. The author (likely Ibn Nujaym, the commentator) affirms this and notes that he has given fatwa accordingly many times. He then provides extensive commentary from al-Ramli explaining the reasoning: the defendant seeks to protect himself and the presumption of innocence, so taking him to a judge who might be biased against him is improper. Later, al-Maqdisi objects that the copies of al-Bazzaziyya are not absolute but restricted to cases where each litigant requests his own judge. The author then discusses a scenario from Jami' al-Fusulayn about two strangers and argues that the jurisdiction depends on the scope of the judge's authority. He concludes that when judges have general authority (as in Egypt), Abu Yusuf's view is preferable because the plaintiff has the right to choose. This final tarjih is supported by al-Durr al-Mukhtar and other authorities. The passage is an expansion probe, explaining and refining the original ruling.

Key Terms
مدعي (mudda'i): Plaintiff: the one who initiates the claim and seeks to prove a right against another.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayhi): Defendant: the one against whom the claim is made; he defends and denies the claim.
ولاية (wilayah): Judicial authority: the jurisdiction granted by the ruler to a judge over a specific area or people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in al-Bazzaziyya, who has the right to choose the judge when the plaintiff and defendant are from different quarters with separate judges?
A) The plaintiff
B) The defendant
C) The ruler
D) Both parties must agree

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the defendant's choice of judge is preferred due to the principle of innocence.

Refining the Rule: General Jurisdiction and the Tarjih of Abu Yusuf
Refinement of intra-Hanafi dispute based on scope of jurisdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and restricted judicial jurisdiction in Hanafi law and determine which imam's view applies in each case.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi shifts to Abu Yusuf when judges have general authority because the plaintiff then has the right to choose.

The earlier fatwa giving the defendant the choice assumed judges had limited, quarter-specific authority. But what if judges are appointed generally for the entire city? Later Hanafi scholars refined the rule: under general jurisdiction, the plaintiff may choose any judge. This lesson explores that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the condition for Muhammad's view
Muhammad's view applies when each judge has a restricted jurisdiction, such as over a specific quarter or group (e.g., military personnel).
The source text gives examples: quarter judges and military judges.
2
Understand the criticism by al-Maqdisi
Al-Maqdisi objected that the absolute wording in al-Bazzaziyya was misinterpreted; the fatwa actually assumes each litigant requests his own judge.
The issue is not absolute but conditional on the scope of authority.
3
Identify the scenario for general jurisdiction
In large cities like Cairo, judges are appointed with general authority over all residents regardless of locality or school.
Here the judge's authority is not limited to a specific quarter.
4
Apply Abu Yusuf's view
Under general jurisdiction, the plaintiff may choose the judge because he is the one initiating the case and there is no restriction on the judge's authority.
This aligns with Abu Yusuf's original reasoning.
5
Note the tarjih by later scholars
Later authorities like al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Ibn Nujaym prefer Abu Yusuf's view for general jurisdiction, as it agrees with the legal definitions of plaintiff and defendant.
Thus the earlier fatwa is not absolute but conditional.

قريتهم التي تولوا القضاء ولهذا قال في الفصول العمادية بعد ذكر المسألة مقيدة بما ذكرنا : وكذا لو كان من أهل العسكر والآخر من أهل البلد فأراد العسكري أن يخاصمه إلى قاضي العسكر فهو على هذا، ولا ولاية لقاضي العسكر على غير الجندي اهـ. فهذا دليل واضح على أن المعتبر هو ا الولاية فالسلطان لما ولى قاضياً ببلدة أو محلة مخصوصة خصه بأهل تلك البلدة فليس له أن يحكم على غيرهم، ومعلوم أن قاضي مصر لما ولي لم يخص حكمه بأهل مصر بل بمن هو فيها من مصري وشامي وحلبي وغيرهم فينبغي التعويل على قول أبي يوسف لموافقته لتعريف المدعي والمدعى عليه، وإن ما ذكره المتأخر يعني العلامة زين وجه له. حموي عن المقدسي ؛ كذا في حاشية أبي السعود. أقول: وحاصله أن ما ذكروه من تصحيح قول محمد بأن العبرة لمكان المدعى عليه إنما هو فيما إذا كان قاضيان كل منهما في محلة وقد أمر كل منهما بالحكم على أهل محلته فقط بدليل قول العمادي «ولا ولاية لقاضي العسكر على غير الجندي». أما إذا كان كل منهما مأذوناً بالحكم على أي من حضر عنده فينبغي تصحيح قول أبي يوسف لأن المدعي هو الذي له الخصومة فيطلبها عند أي قاض أراد، ولا يخفى أن قضاة مصر والشام إذنهم عام وهذا كلام متجه . ونقل مثله في الدر المختار عن خط صاحب التنوير على هامش البزازية حيث قال : وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا كان كل قاض على محلة على حدة، أما إذا كان في المصر حنفي وشافعي ومالكي وحنبلي في مجلس واحد والولاية واحدة فلا ينبغي أن يقع
A quarter and another from another quarter, and the plaintiff wants to bring him to the judge of his quarter and the other refuses that. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differed on this, and the correct view is that consideration is given to the place of the defendant. Likewise, if one of them is from the military and the other from the town, and the military person wants to bring him to the military judge, it is according to this. He explained in al-Muhit that Abu Yusuf says the plaintiff initiates the litigation, so his judge is considered; Muhammad says the defendant defends against it. In al-Bazzaziyya: two judges in a city, each one wants to go to a judge; the choice is for the defendant according to Muhammad, and upon it is the fatwa. This includes, as when the plaintiff wants the judge of the defendant's quarter and the defendant wants the judge of the plaintiff's quarter, and when there are many judges from the four schools as in Cairo, and the plaintiff wants a Shafi'i judge and the other wants a Maliki, and they are not from their quarters, then the choice is for the defendant. This is the apparent view, and I have given fatwa upon it many times. His statement: (And he explained it in al-Muhit...): al-Ramli said: According to Abu Yusuf, the plaintiff, if he leaves, leaves; he is the initiator, so he may choose to initiate the litigation before the judge of his quarter or before the judge of his opponent. Muhammad: the defendant is the defender, and the defender seeks safety for himself, and the principle is the innocence of his liability, so taking him to someone who refuses due to a suspicion or doubt that may cause him to prove what was not established against him, is more appropriate because he wants to defend himself, and his opponent is obligating him to respond to the claim. And whoever seeks safety is more deserving of consideration than one who seeks its opposite. His statement: (This is the apparent view and I have given fatwa upon it many times) was refuted by al-Maqdisi as being incorrect: first, the famous copies of al-Bazzaziyya are not as absolute as he claimed and based his fatwa upon, but rather as restricted by that each of the two litigants requests trial before the judge of his quarter; and if his copy is absolute, it is interpreted as restricted, meaning what is expressly stated in al-Imadiyya and al-Khaniyya and others: indeed, the one who was appointed specifically to that town or that quarter, and therefore he said... In Jami' al-Fusulayn: two strangers disputed before the judge of a town; his judgment is valid as arbitration. I say: There is no need for this because judges are delegated generally to judge over everyone in their town or village that they are appointed to. Hence the statement in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya after mentioning the issue restricted as we mentioned: Likewise, if one is from the military and the other from the town, and the military person wants to bring him to the military judge, it is according to this, and the military judge has no authority over non-military. This is clear evidence that consideration is given to authority. When the Sultan appoints a judge to a particular town or quarter, he restricts him to the people of that town, so he cannot judge over others. It is known that the judge of Cairo, when appointed, is not restricted to the people of Cairo but to those within it, whether Egyptian, Syrian, Aleppan, etc. So one should rely on the view of Abu Yusuf because it agrees with the definition of plaintiff and defendant. And what the later scholar (meaning al-Allamah Zayn) said is supported. Humawi from al-Maqdisi; as in the margin of Abu al-Su'ud. I say: The gist is that what they mentioned of correcting Muhammad's view that consideration is given to the place of the defendant is only when there are two judges each in a quarter and each is commanded to judge only the people of his quarter, as evidence by al-Imadi's statement: 'the military judge has no authority over non-military.' But when each is authorized to judge anyone who appears before him, then the view of Abu Yusuf should be corrected because the plaintiff is the one who has the litigation and seeks it before whichever judge he wants, and it is no secret that the judges of Egypt and Syria have general authorization, and this is sound. A similar view is transmitted in al-Durr al-Mukhtar from the handwriting of the author of al-Tanwir on the margin of al-Bazzaziyya: this disagreement is when each judge is over a separate quarter; but when in the city there is a Hanafi, Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali in one council and authority is one, then this disagreement should not occur.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding which judge has jurisdiction when the plaintiff and defendant live in different localities. The author opens by stating the positions: Abu Yusuf considers the plaintiff's judge because the plaintiff initiates the case, while Muhammad considers the defendant's judge because the defendant is the one defending. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's view, giving the defendant the choice. The author (likely Ibn Nujaym, the commentator) affirms this and notes that he has given fatwa accordingly many times. He then provides extensive commentary from al-Ramli explaining the reasoning: the defendant seeks to protect himself and the presumption of innocence, so taking him to a judge who might be biased against him is improper. Later, al-Maqdisi objects that the copies of al-Bazzaziyya are not absolute but restricted to cases where each litigant requests his own judge. The author then discusses a scenario from Jami' al-Fusulayn about two strangers and argues that the jurisdiction depends on the scope of the judge's authority. He concludes that when judges have general authority (as in Egypt), Abu Yusuf's view is preferable because the plaintiff has the right to choose. This final tarjih is supported by al-Durr al-Mukhtar and other authorities. The passage is an expansion probe, explaining and refining the original ruling.

Key Terms
مدعي (mudda'i): Plaintiff: the one who initiates the claim and seeks to prove a right against another.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayhi): Defendant: the one against whom the claim is made; he defends and denies the claim.
ولاية عامة (wilayah 'ammah): General jurisdiction: the judge's authority extends over all residents of a city or region without restriction to a specific quarter or group.
ولاية خاصة (wilayah khassah): Restricted jurisdiction: the judge's authority is limited to a specific quarter, tribe, or class (e.g., military personnel).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a city where judges have general jurisdiction over the entire city, which imam's view applies regarding who chooses the judge?
A) Muhammad's view: the defendant chooses
B) Abu Yusuf's view: the plaintiff chooses
C) Both views are invalid
D) The ruler decides

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school reconciles the dispute by conditioning the choice of judge on the scope of the judge's authority.

Plaintiff Cannot Be Forced to Sue
Fatwa that a plaintiff cannot be forced to sue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a plaintiff cannot be forced to sue and distinguish it from a claim to repel harassment (daf' al-ta'arud).

Core Idea
You cannot be forced to sue; the right to claim is yours alone.

This lesson explores a fundamental principle in Islamic judicial procedure: the plaintiff's autonomy in deciding whether to bring a lawsuit. Based on a fatwa from the Hanafi tradition, we examine why a court cannot compel someone to sue, even if they have a valid claim. We also distinguish this from a special type of claim known as daf' al-ta'arud (repelling harassment), which is admissible without a specific underlying right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the fatwa statement: 'لا يجبر المدعي على الدعوى' (the plaintiff cannot be forced to sue). This is the central legal principle.
The reason given is 'لأن الحق له' (because the right belongs to him).
2
Understand the basis of the ruling
The right to sue is a personal right (haqq). The plaintiff may choose to exercise it or not. The court has no authority to compel.
This applies to all types of claims unless otherwise specified.
3
Distinguish from daf' al-ta'arud
The fatwa contrasts a regular claim (e.g., for debt) with a claim to repel harassment. In daf' al-ta'arud, the plaintiff does not assert a right but only seeks to stop unjust interference. This type is admissible and valid even without a specific right.
The difference is clear: in the first case, the plaintiff asserts a right or else risks testifying to discharge; in the second, he only demands removal of harassment.
4
Apply to practical scenarios
Consider a creditor who does not wish to sue his debtor. The court cannot force him. Conversely, a person being harassed can file a daf' al-ta'arud claim without needing to prove a debt or other right.
This protects the plaintiff's choice and prevents abuse of judicial process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيره فأجاب : لا يجبر المدعي على الدعوى لأن الحق له ا هـ . ولا يعارضه ما نقلوه في وبين صحة الدعوى بدفع التعرض وهي مسموعة كما في البزازية والخزانة، والفرق بينهما ظاهر فإنه في الأول إنما يدعي أنه إن كان له شيء عليه يدعيه وإلا يشهد على نفسه بالإبراء، وفي الثاني إنما يدعي عليه أنه يتعرض له في كذا بغير حق ويطالب بدفع التعرض فافهم ا هـ.
Someone else replied: The plaintiff cannot be forced to file a claim because the right is his. This is not contradicted by what they transmitted about the validity of a claim to repel harassment, for it is admissible as in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khazana. The difference between them is apparent: in the first, he only claims that if he has something against him, he would claim it; otherwise he would testify to his own discharge. In the second, he only claims that the other harasses him unjustly and demands the removal of harassment. Understand this.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This short passage is a fatwa clarifying that a plaintiff cannot be forced to initiate a lawsuit because the right to sue belongs to him. The author distinguishes between two types of claims: in a normal debt claim, the plaintiff must assert a right or else he might be forced to declare discharge; but in a claim for defense against harassment (daf' al-ta'arud), the plaintiff is only seeking to stop unjust interference. The latter is admissible even without a specific basis of right. This distinction is important to understand that the court does not compel someone to sue unless they voluntarily choose to.

Key Terms
دفع التعرض (daf' al-ta'arud): Claim to repel harassment: a lawsuit seeking to stop someone from unjustly interfering with one's property or person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this fatwa, is a plaintiff ever forced to bring a claim?
A) Yes, if the debt is proven
B) No, because the right to sue is his
C) Yes, in cases of harassment
D) No, but he must testify about the debt

Key Takeaway
A plaintiff cannot be forced to sue because the right to claim belongs to him; however, a claim to repel harassment (daf' al-ta'arud) is admissible even without a specific underlying right.

Identifying the Proper Defendant in Claims
Definitional list of proper defendants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proper defendant (khasm) in different types of claims and apply the Hanafi rules to determine who must be present in court.

Core Idea
The proper defendant depends on the nature of the claim and the relationship of the parties, not merely possession.

Determining the correct defendant is a foundational procedural issue in Islamic court proceedings. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq fills a gap left by earlier commentators by listing who must be summoned in various property-related claims. As you work through these rules, you will see that the Hanafi school's approach balances the rights of ownership, possession, and contractual relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of claim
Determine what the claimant is asserting: absolute ownership, usurpation, prior right (istihqaq), pre-emption (shuf'a), or a defective sale (fasid). The defendant varies by claim type.
2
Examine the relationship to the property
Identify who currently possesses the property and what legal relationship they have (owner, lessee, pledgee, muzari', musta'ir, buyer, heir, etc.).
3
Apply the specific Hanafi rule
For each combination of claim type and possession relationship, recall the rule from the passage. For example: for a claim of absolute ownership on a leased item, both owner and lessee must be present unless the claimant claims purchase before the lease.
Memorize the rules for common scenarios.
4
Note exceptions and special cases
Be aware of exceptions such as the borrower (musta'ir) not being required, or the change of defendant before and after delivery in a fasid sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد من بيان من يكون خصماً في الدعوى ليعلم المدعى عليه، وقد أغفله الشارحون وهو مما لا ينبغي فأقول في دعوى الخارج ملكاً مطلقاً في عين في يد مستأجر أو أو فلا مرتهن بد من حضرة المالك وذي اليد إلا إذا ادعى الشراء منه قبل الإجارة، فالمالك وحده يكون خصماً، وتشترط حضرة المزارع إن كان البذر منه أو كان الزرع نابتاً وإلا مستعير لا. وفي دعوى الغصب عليه لا تشترط حضرة المالك وفي البيع قبل التسليم لا بد في دعوى الاستحقاق والشفعة من حضرة البائع والمشتري والمشتري فاسداً بعد القبض خصم لمن يدعي الملك فيه، وقبل القبض الخصم هو البائع وحده وأحد الورثة ينتصب خصماً عن الكل، فالقضاء عليه قضاء على الكل وعلى الميت، وقيده في الجامع بكون الكل في يده وأن البعض في يده فبقدره والموصى له ليس بخصم في إثبات الدين إنما هو خصم في إثبات الوصية
It is necessary to explain who is a defendant in a claim so that the defendant is known. The commentators have neglected this, which should not be. So I say: In a claim by an outsider of absolute ownership in an object in the hand of a lessee or pledgee, the presence of the owner and the possessor is necessary unless he claims purchase from him before the lease, then the owner alone is defendant. The presence of the sharecropper (muzari') is required if the seed is from him or if the crop is growing; otherwise, the borrower (musta'ir) is not required. In a claim of usurpation against him, the presence of the owner is not required. In a sale before delivery, in a claim of prior right (istihqaq) and pre-emption (shuf'a), the presence of the seller and buyer is necessary. In a defective sale after possession, the buyer is a defendant for anyone claiming ownership; before possession, the defendant is the seller alone. One of the heirs stands as a defendant for all, so judgment against him is judgment against all and against the deceased. It is restricted in al-Jami' to the case where all (the property) is in his hand; and if some is in his hand, then to that extent. The legatee is not a defendant in proving a debt; he is only a defendant in proving the bequest.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive list of rules for identifying the proper defendant (khasm) in various civil claims, a topic the author says previous commentators neglected. Key points: For an outsider claiming ownership of an item in the hands of a lessee or pledgee, both the owner and the possessor must be present to defend; but if the claimant claims he bought from the owner before the lease, only the owner is needed. For a sharecropper (muzari'), his presence is required if he owns the seed or the crop is growing. For a borrower (musta'ir), he is not required. In a claim of usurpation against the possessor, the owner need not be present. In a sale before delivery, if someone claims prior right or pre-emption, both seller and buyer must be present. In a defective (fasid) sale, after possession, the buyer is the proper defendant for a claim of ownership; before possession, only the seller. Among co-heirs, one can represent all, so a judgment against him applies to all and to the deceased, but only if all property is in his possession; if only a part, then only to that extent. The legatee (musa lahu) is not a defendant in a claim to prove a debt against the deceased; he is only a defendant when proving the bequest itself. These rules help determine who must be summoned in a lawsuit.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): Defendant or party to a lawsuit: the person against whom the claim is made and who can defend.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Prior right or ownership claim: a third party claiming that the property in suit rightfully belongs to him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim by an outsider that he owns an item currently leased, who must be present as defendants?
A) Only the lessee
B) Only the owner
C) Both the owner and the lessee
D) Neither, the outsider must prove ownership

Key Takeaway
The proper defendant in a claim is identified by the nature of the claim and the relationship of the parties, not merely by possession.
