Jurisdiction Dispute Between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: Plaintiff or Defendant's Judge?
Resolving the dispute on plaintiff vs. defendant venue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on determining the competent judge, identify the condition that resolves it, and apply the ruling to concrete scenarios.

Core Idea
Jurisdiction depends on the judge's authorization: general allows plaintiff's choice, restricted mandates defendant's location.

This lesson addresses a key procedural question in Hanafi qada: when plaintiff and defendant reside in different jurisdictions, which judge has competence? The two leading disciples of Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, gave conflicting answers. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq not only states their views but provides a sophisticated reconciliation based on the scope of judicial authority. Understanding this resolution is essential for any mufti or judge handling multi-jurisdiction cases.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify what Abu Yusuf and Muhammad each held regarding the competent judge when plaintiff and defendant are from different jurisdictions.
Abu Yusuf: plaintiff may choose any judge. Muhammad: defendant's location determines the judge.
2
Identify the key evidence
Find the proofs each imam relied on as stated in the text. Abu Yusuf: plaintiff is the rights-holder and initiates the claim. Muhammad: judge's authority is territorial over the defendant.
Note that both proofs are aqli (rational) based on the nature of judicial authority.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The author shows that the dispute is not absolute. Muhammad's view applies when judges have restricted district authority; Abu Yusuf's when authority is general over all persons in a city.
The example of Cairo's judge and al-Imadi's statement about army judge are used as evidence.
4
Apply the condition to a case
Given a scenario (e.g., separate district judges vs. general city judges), determine which view applies and why.
This tests whether you can identify the decisive factor: the scope of wilayah.
5
Derive the mufta bihi
State the operative ruling for fatwa: in major cities with general authorization, plaintiff may choose; in restricted districts, defendant's location controls.
The author explicitly leans toward Abu Yusuf for general authorization cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في جامع الفصولين اختصم غريبان عند قاضي بلدة صح قضاؤه على سبيل التحكيم. أقول: ولا يحتاج إلى هذا لأن القضاة يفوض لهم الحكم على العموم في كل من هو في بلدهم أو قريتهم التي تولوا القضاء ولهذا قال في الفصول العمادية بعد ذكر المسألة مقيدة بما ذكرنا : وكذا لو كان من أهل العسكر والآخر من أهل البلد فأراد العسكري أن يخاصمه إلى قاضي العسكر فهو على هذا، ولا ولاية لقاضي العسكر على غير الجندي اهـ. فهذا دليل واضح على أن المعتبر هو الولاية فالسلطان لما ولى قاضياً ببلدة أو محلة مخصوصة خصه بأهل تلك البلدة فليس له أن يحكم على غيرهم، ومعلوم أن قاضي مصر لما ولي لم يخص حكمه بأهل مصر بل بمن هو فيها من مصري وشامي وحلبي وغيرهم فينبغي التعويل على قول أبي يوسف لموافقته لتعريف المدعي والمدعى عليه، وإن ما ذكره المتأخر يعني العلامة زين وجه له. حموي عن المقدسي ; كذا في حاشية أبي السعود. أقول: وحاصله أن ما ذكروه من تصحيح قول محمد بأن العبرة لمكان المدعى عليه إنما هو فيما إذا كان قاضيان كل منهما في محلة وقد أمر كل منهما بالحكم على أهل محلته فقط بدليل قول العمادي «ولا ولاية لقاضي العسكر على غير الجندي». أما إذا كان كل منهما مأذوناً بالحكم على أي من حضر عنده فينبغي تصحيح قول أبي يوسف لأن المدعي هو الذي له الخصومة فيطلبها عند أي قاض أراد، ولا يخفى أن قضاة مصر والشام إذنهم عام وهذا كلام متجه . ونقل مثله في الدر المختار عن خط صاحب التنوير على هامش البزازية حيث قال : وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا كان كل قاض على محلة على حدة، أما إذا كان في المصر حنفي وشافعي ومالكي وحنبلي في مجلس واحد والولاية واحدة فلا ينبغي أن يقع الخلاف في إجابة المدعي لما أنه صاحب الحق .اهـ. قلت : وذكر نحوه في المنح ولكن رده الرملي في حاشيته عليها وبالغ فيه حتى جعله بالهذيان أشبه ولم يأت لرده بوجه يقويه والظاهر أنه لم يظهر له المراد وهو الذي ذكرناه في الحاصل فقال ما قال وذكر شيخ مشايخنا السائحاني بعد كلام قال في قضاء البزازية : فوض قضاء ناحية إلى رجلين لا يملك أحدهما القضاء، ولو قلد رجلين على أن ينفرد كل منهما بالقضاء لا رواية فيه. وقال الإمام ظهير الدين: ينبغي أن يجوز لأن القاضي نائب السلطان ويملك التفرد .اهـ. فتحصل أن الولاية لو لقاضيين فأكثر كل واحد في محلة فتفرد القاضي صحيح والعبرة للمدعى عليه، وإن كانوا في محل واحد على السواء فقد سمعت أنه لا يملك أحدهم التفرد فلا فائدة في اختيار أحدهم، وإن أمر كل واحد بالتفرد جاز، وحينئذ فلا يظهر فرق بين كون كل واحد في محلة أو مجتمعين فما فهمه صاحب التنوير ليس على إطلاقه بل على هذا التفصيل اهـ. وكان عليه أن يذكر بعد قوله جاز والعبرة للمدعي وقد اتضح المرام من هذه المسألة على أتم وجه والله تعالى الحد قوله : (أو دائناً) فائدته إثبات المحاصصه تأمل.
In Jami' al-Fusulayn: Two strangers brought a claim before the judge of a town; his judgment is valid as arbitration. I say: There is no need for this because judges are delegated to judge generally over everyone who is in their town or village for which they are appointed. For this reason, al-Fusul al-Imadi stated after mentioning the case restricted as we have said: Likewise if one is from the army and the other is from the town, and the army man wants to take him to the army judge, it follows this; the army judge has no authority over non-soldiers. This is clear evidence that what matters is jurisdiction: when the sultan appoints a judge for a specific town or district, he restricts him to its people; he cannot judge others. It is known that when the judge of Cairo was appointed, his judgment was not restricted to the people of Cairo but to everyone present there—Egyptian, Syrian, Aleppan, etc. Hence one should rely on Abu Yusuf's opinion because it accords with the definitions of plaintiff and defendant. What the later scholar (i.e., Allamah Zayn) mentioned is explained by Himawi from al-Maqdisi; likewise in Hashiyat Abi al-Su'ud. I say: The gist is that what they mentioned—correcting Muhammad's view that the criterion is the defendant's location—applies only when there are two judges each in a separate district and each was ordered to judge only its people, as al-Imadi's statement indicates. But if each is authorized to judge anyone who appears before him, then Abu Yusuf's view should be corrected because the plaintiff initiates the claim and can take it to any judge he wishes. It is clear that the judges of Egypt and Syria have general authorization, and this reasoning is sound. Similar is transmitted in al-Durr al-Mukhtar from the margin of al-Bazzaziyya by the author of al-Tanwir: This difference applies when each judge is in a separate district; but if in a city there are Hanafi, Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali judges in one council with unified jurisdiction, then the difference should not arise regarding answering the plaintiff because he is the rights-holder. I say: Al-Manh mentioned similar, but al-Ramli refuted it in his Hashiyah and went so far as to compare it to delirium, but his refutation is weak. It appears he did not understand the intended meaning—which we have summarized. Then the shaykh of our shaykhs, al-Saihani, said after some comments: In Qadha al-Bazzaziyya: If one delegated the judiciary of a district to two men, one of them does not have independent authority; if he appointed two with the condition that each holds independent authority, there is no transmitted report. Imam Zahir al-Din said: It should be allowed because the judge is the sultan's deputy and can act independently. So the result is that if there are two or more judges, each in a separate district, then independent judgment is valid and the criterion is the defendant's location. If they are in the same place equally, then none can act independently, and there is no benefit in choosing one. If each is ordered to act independently, it is valid, and then no difference appears between being in separate districts or together. What the author of al-Tanwir understood is not absolute but subject to this detail. He should have mentioned after 'it is valid' that the criterion is the plaintiff. The objective is now fully clear. And Allah knows best. (His statement: 'or a creditor' — its benefit is establishing pro rata sharing; ponder this.)
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage addresses a key dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding which judge has competence when the plaintiff and defendant are in different jurisdictions. The base case from Jami' al-Fusulayn states that if two strangers litigate before a town judge, the judgment is valid as arbitration. The author argues that the need for arbitration rationale is unnecessary because judges are generally delegated to judge all persons present in their territory. He supports this with al-Imadi's example: a military judge has no authority over civilians, implying the judge's authority is geographically defined. For Cairo's judge, authority extended to all residents, not just natives. The author concludes that one should rely on Abu Yusuf's opinion—that the plaintiff can choose the judge—because the plaintiff is the one who initiates the claim. However, the later scholar Allamah Zayn's interpretation, as explained by Himawi, reconciles Muhammad's view (criterion is defendant's location) by limiting it to cases where each judge is restricted to a specific district. In situations like major cities where judges have general authorization, Abu Yusuf's view is adopted. The author then critiques Ramli's rejection of this reconciliation. The passage concludes with a detailed resolution: if judges are in separate districts with limited jurisdiction, the defendant's location determines competence; if they are in the same place with equal jurisdiction, independent action is only valid if explicitly authorized; if authorized, the plaintiff's choice prevails. The final line references a phrase 'or a creditor' from the earlier lesson, explaining its benefit for establishing pro rata distribution.

Key Terms
الولاية (al-wilāyah): Judicial authority or jurisdiction granted by the ruler to a judge over a specific territory or group of people.
المدعي (al-mudda‘ī): The plaintiff who brings a claim. Hanafi definition: the one who would be left if he did not claim.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda‘ā ‘alayh): The defendant against whom the claim is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the resolution in the text, when does Imam Muhammad's opinion (criterion is defendant's location) apply?
A) When both judges are in the same city
B) When each judge is assigned to a specific district with limited jurisdiction
C) When the plaintiff is from the army
D) When the property is in dispute

Key Takeaway
The competent judge is determined by the scope of his authority: general authority allows plaintiff's choice; restricted authority requires defendant's location.

The Five Opponents in Lineage Lawsuits and Conditions for Minors and Slaves
Defines the five opponents and conditions for minors and slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five proper opponents in a lineage lawsuit and explain when the presence of a minor or slave is required in court.

Core Idea
Only five specific parties are proper opponents in lineage cases; minors and slaves are represented by guardians, and their presence is often not required.

In Hanafi court procedure, proving lineage (ithbat al-nasab) is a sensitive matter that requires suing the correct party. This lesson identifies the five proper opponents and clarifies when a minor or slave must physically appear in court. Understanding these rules ensures that lawsuits are properly directed and not dismissed for procedural errors.

Guided Path
1
List the five opponents
Memorize the five parties: heir, executor, legatee, creditor of deceased, and debtor to deceased. These are the only proper defendants in a lineage claim.
2
Identify the special waqf case
When a waqf is for a minor with an executor, claims against the waqf must be brought against the trustee (mutawalli), not the executor, because the executor does not handle waqf assets.
This clarifies who is the proper opponent when waqf property is involved.
3
Determine when a minor's presence is required
Generally, a minor need not appear if a guardian (father or executor) is present. Exception: if the minor is sued for a tort (e.g., usurpation) and the plaintiff has a witness present, the minor must appear with the guardian; otherwise the judge appoints a guardian.
Torts require stricter procedure to protect the minor's rights.
4
Apply the slave rule
When a master claims compensation (arsh) for injury to his slave or dower for a female slave, the slave's presence is not required; the master's presence suffices.
Slaves are not independent litigants.
5
Note the exception for nursing infants
The correct (sahih) view is that nursing infants need not appear in court at all, even when they are plaintiffs or defendants.
This is an exception to the general rule that minors must appear when suing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخصم في إثبات النسب خمسة: الوارث، والوصي، والموصى له والغريم للميت أو على الميت وقف على صغير له وصي ولرجل فيه دعوى يدعيه على متولي الوقف لا على الوصي لأن الوصي لا يلي القبض، ولا تشترط حضرة الصبي عند الدعوى عليه وتكفي حضرة وصيه ديناً أو عيناً، باشره الوصي أو لا، ولا يشترط حضرة العبد والأمة عند دعوى المولى أرشه ومهرها، ولو ادعى على صبي محجور عليه استهلاكاً أو غصباً وقال لي بينة حاضرة تسمع دعواه وتشترط حضرة الصبي مع أبيه أو وصيه وإلا نصب القاضي له وصياً. وتشترط حضرته عند الدعوى مدعياً أو مدعى عليه، والصحيح أنه لا تشترط حضرة الأطفال الرضع عند الدعوى.
And the opponent in proving lineage are five: the heir, the executor, the legatee, the creditor of the deceased or the debtor to the deceased. And if there is a waqf for a minor who has an executor, and a man has a claim against the trustee of the waqf, the claim is against the trustee, not the executor, because the executor does not take possession. And the presence of the minor is not required when suing him; the presence of his executor is sufficient, whether for debt or property, whether the executor directly handled it or not. And the presence of a male or female slave is not required when the master claims compensation for injury (arsh) or her dower. And if someone sues a minor under interdiction for consumption or usurpation and says, 'I have a witness present,' his claim is heard, and the presence of the minor is required with his father or executor; otherwise, the judge appoints an executor for him. And his presence is required when suing as plaintiff or defendant, but the correct view is that the presence of nursing infants is not required.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage lists the five parties who can be opponents (خصم) in a lawsuit to establish lineage (نسب): heir, executor, legatee, creditor of the deceased, and debtor to the deceased. The text then addresses specific cases: If a waqf is established for a minor who has an executor, someone with a claim against the waqf must sue the trustee (متولي), not the executor. When suing a minor, the presence of the minor is not required; the guardian's presence suffices. Similarly, when a master claims injury compensation or dower for a slave, the slave's presence is not required. However, if a minor is sued for a tort like consumption or usurpation and the plaintiff has a witness, the claim is heard but the minor must be present with his guardian; otherwise, the judge appoints one. The presence of the minor is needed when he is a plaintiff or defendant, except for nursing infants, whose presence is not required according to the correct view (الصحيح).

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): Opponent in a lawsuit; the proper party to be sued
إثبات النسب (ithbat al-nasab): Proving lineage or paternity
وصي (wasi): Executor appointed in a will or by the court
متولي الوقف (mutawalli al-waqf): Trustee or manager of a waqf (endowment)
حضرة (hadrah): Presence (in court or before the judge)
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a slave or free person (specific amounts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many opponents are listed for a lineage lawsuit?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi court procedure for lineage cases, only five specific parties are proper opponents, and minors and slaves are represented by guardians, with limited exceptions for tort claims and infants.

Delegation of Judicial Authority to Multiple Judges and Benefit of 'Or Creditor'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple judges and explanation of a phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when two judges appointed for the same district may exercise independent authority and identify the benefit of the phrase 'or creditor' in establishing proportional distribution among creditors.

Core Idea
Two judges in the same locality cannot individually judge unless explicitly ordered; the phrase 'or creditor' ensures proportional distribution among creditors.

When the sultan appoints multiple judges to the same district, a key question arises: can each judge act independently? The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer based on the terms of delegation and the locality assignment. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the correct view, and also clarifies why the phrase 'or creditor' was included in earlier rulings—it secures the proportional right of creditors in debt distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core dispute: if a ruler delegates the judiciary of a district to two judges, does each have the right to judge independently?
The baseline ruling from Qada al-Bazzaziyyah is that neither can judge alone.
2
Analyze the default Hanafi position
Without specific permission, two judges in the same locality cannot individually act. This is based on the principle that the delegation is limited; the judge is the sultan's deputy, not an absolute independent authority.
The text states 'لا يملك أحدهما القضاء' when no condition is stated.
3
Examine the exception
If the ruler explicitly orders each to judge independently, or if each judge is assigned a separate locality (muhallah), then independent judgment is valid. In the latter case, the relevant party is the defendant (العبرة للمدعى عليه).
The detail depends on the terms of delegation.
4
Consider the opposing view
Imam Zahir al-Din argued that each judge inherently has independent authority as the sultan's deputy. The correct view rejects this absolute interpretation; strict terms of delegation govern.
The passage notes that this view is not accepted; the delegation must be interpreted strictly.
5
Understand the benefit of 'or creditor'
The phrase 'أو دائناً' in the earlier matn serves to establish proportional distribution (المحاصصه) among creditors. Without it, the ruling might allow one creditor to claim full payment before others; with it, all creditors share proportionally.
This is a practical fiqh benefit that affects debt recovery cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في إجابة المدعي لما أنه صاحب الحق .اهـ. قلت : وذكر نحوه في المنح ولكن رده الرملي في حاشيته عليها وبالغ فيه حتى جعله بالهذيان أشبه ولم يأت لرده بوجه يقويه والظاهر أنه لم يظهر له المراد وهو الذي ذكرناه في الحاصل فقال ما قال وذكر شيخ مشايخنا السائحاني بعد كلام قال في قضاء البزازية : فوض قضاء ناحية إلى رجلين لا يملك أحدهما القضاء، ولو قلد رجلين على أن ينفرد كل منهما القضاء لا رواية فيه. وقال الإمام ظهير الدين: ينبغي أن يجوز لأن القاضي نائب السلطان ويملك التفرد .اهـ. فتحصل أن الولاية لو لقاضيين فأكثر كل واحد في محلة فتفرد القاضي صحيح والعبرة للمدعى عليه، وإن كانوا في محل واحد على السواء فقد سمعت أنه لا يملك أحدهم التفرد فلا فائدة في اختيار أحدهم، وإن أمر كل واحد بالتفرد جاز، وحينئذ فلا يظهر فرق بين كون كل واحد في محلة أو مجتمعين فما فهمه صاحب التنوير ليس على إطلاقه بل على هذا التفصيل اهـ. وكان عليه أن يذكر بعد قوله جاز والعبرة للمدعي وقد اتضح المرام من هذه المسألة على أتم وجه والله تعالى الحد قوله : (أو دائناً) فائدته إثبات المحاصصه تأمل.
The disagreement is in responding to the plaintiff because he is the holder of the right. End. I say: A similar point was mentioned in Al-Munah, but al-Ramli refuted it in his gloss, overdoing it until he made it similar to delirium, and he did not bring a strong basis for his refutation. It appears that the intended meaning was not clear to him, which is what we mentioned in Al-Hasil, so he said what he said. And our teacher al-Saihani mentioned after some words: In Qada' al-Bazzaziyyah: He delegated the judiciary of a district to two men, neither of whom has the power of judgment individually. And if he appointed two men on the condition that each independently exercises judgment, there is no transmitted ruling. Imam Zahir al-Din said: It should be permissible because the judge is the deputy of the sultan and can act independently. End. So it is established that if the authority is given to two or more judges, each in a separate locality, then independent judgment by one is valid, and the consideration is based on the defendant. And if they are in the same locality equally, it has been heard that none of them can act independently, so there is no benefit in choosing one of them. And if each is ordered to independently act, it is permissible. Then there is no apparent difference between being in separate localities or together. So what the author of Al-Tanwir understood is not absolute but according to this detail. End. And he should have mentioned after his saying 'it is permissible' and the consideration is based on the plaintiff. And the objective of this issue has become clear most completely. And Allah knows best. The benefit of his saying 'or creditor' is to establish the ratio. Reflect.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage first discusses a disagreement about responding to the plaintiff. Then it moves to a specific issue about multiple judges. The commentator refutes al-Ramli's criticism. Then he explains the ruling on delegation of judicial authority to multiple judges. The main point: If the sultan delegates the judiciary of a district to two judges, they cannot individually judge unless specifically ordered to do so. However, if each is assigned a separate locality, they can act independently, and the relevant party is the defendant (العبرة للمدعى عليه). If they are in the same locality, they cannot act independently without an order. Imam Zahir al-Din holds that it is permissible because the judge is the sultan's deputy. The correct view is detailed: the delegation must be interpreted strictly. Finally, the commentator explains that the phrase 'or creditor' (أو دائناً) earlier is beneficial for establishing the ratio among creditors (المحاصصه) in debt distribution.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judiciary or judgment
ناحية (nahiyah): District or locality
تفرد (tafarud): Acting independently (in judicial matters)
ولاية (wilayah): Authority or jurisdiction
محلة (mahallah): Locality or sector
محاصصه (muhasasah): Proportional distribution (among creditors)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always
B) No, they must act jointly
C) Only if the defendant agrees
D) Only for certain cases

Key Takeaway
The validity of a judge's independent ruling depends on the terms of delegation; the phrase 'or creditor' ensures proportional distribution among creditors.
